## 3 GERMANY: A NEW CULTURE of THINGS

![](images/2cd762d6e73702eeaa9f95ba3f5bf0a30d114021d7a0dd37436d4313c4b3a1aa.jpg)

ered cheap, poorly made, tasteless, and above all, to have estranged the workingman from any control over the work process. Voices of rebellion and reform first emerged in Britain. Great Britain was the leading power in the world politically and economically; it served more than any other country as a model for the young German Empire. The writings of British Arts and Crafts theorists, such as John Ruskin and William Morris, as well as knowledge of objects produced in the workshops of Morris, C. R. Ashbee, and other British designers, reached Germany and acted as a catalyst in this early phase of the movement, known as Jugendstil (Youth Style, after Munich's avant-garde journal Jugend). As the end of the nineteenth century approached, German design reform was driven by two pressing concerns: to create goods that could compete economically with other major European powers and to establish a greater sense of national identity following the country's unification of 1871. Britain was the country to learn from.'

Despite the admiration for Great Britain, the basic conditions for an Arts and Crafts culture in Germany differed in many respects. A major premise in Britain—the idealization of the Middle Ages—had little resonance in Germany. And while the English Arts and Crafts movement originated in large cities, such as London, Birmingham, and Manchester, its most profound associations were with nature, country life, and the country house, where "the simple life" was the paramount ideal. The reformist design culture of the era of Wilhelm II in Germany (1888—1918) was, in contrast, oriented more toward cities; beginning around 1896, reform experiments developed rapidly in regional urban centers throughout the newly unified German state.

From the beginning of the Arts and Crafts movement in England, its modern design culture was shaped by architecture and by the interplay of interior and exterior space. In 1859, for example, William Morris began plans for his home, the Red House, which he, the architect Philip Webb, and other friends designed and furnished. Conversely, the German Arts and Crafts movement gained momentum not from working architects but from artists who had formerly focused on painting, so the development of reforms in Germany took a different direction from those in Britain from the very start. Large-scale interior design projects in the modern style first became an artistic issue in Germany toward the end of the century, when the movement for a comprehensive reform of art and life inspired German Arts and Crafts innovators to begin designing and furnishing houses themselves.2

The role played by British publications in disseminating Arts and Crafts ideas in Germany can hardly be overestimated. Works such as Walter Crane's Claims of Decorative Art (1892) and The Basis of Design (1898), along with the monthly London journal The Studio (founded in 1893), were widely read and discussed. Although their influence on individual artists has never been investigated in detail, it's clear that many German Arts and Crafts designers relied on these and other British precedents.3

Contacts with the Arts and Crafts movement occurred on many levels of artistic activity, and they were even spurred by government intervention. In 1896 the German government appointed the architect Hermann Muthesius to its embassy in London as attaché for trade and industry, with the specific assignment of reporting on British architecture and decorative arts. During the seven years he spent in London, Muthesius proved to be an astute observer and investigator. He traveled extensively throughout the United Kingdom and established close relationships with many British architects, designers, and writers. Through the numerous reports anartic he published durig these years Muthesis actively redhe Britih Art and Crafts ideal in Germany.

His studies resulted in the hugely influential three-volume Das englische Haus (The English House, 19045), which extoled the great artistic merit f the Arts and Crafts move-

![](images/d06d020af8ef1b9d5689d3650fdfc190db99c3d6839796a6b78d5a6bf6d94feb.jpg)

ment. Muthesius's pioneering book was published after he returned to Germany to take up an appointment in the Prussian Ministry of Trade and Industry, with responsibility for applied arts education. Despite the fact that Muthesius drew upon his British experience in initiating major reforms for crafts training in Prussia, he soon became a strong advocate of standardized German design and emphasized the limits of the English workshop as a model for Germany.5

British Arts and Crafts designers also became well known in Germany through exhibitions, competitions, and not least through private interior design commissions.' Many German designers traveled to Britain and became acquainted firsthand with the work of their British counterparts. For example, the Swiss-born designer Hermann Obrist, who became active in Munich, took a trip through Great Britain in 1887. His embroidery owed much to the ideas and impetus he gained from English models (3.2). C. R. Ashbee and his Guild of Handicraft provided nothing short of an archetype for German designers. One admirer, Karl Schmidt, the founder in 1898 of the Dresdner Werkstätten für Handwerkskunst (Dresden Workshops for Handicraft Arts) had spent a year in England and later commissioned architects such as M. H. Baillie Scott to design furniture for the workshops (3.3). Between 1907 and 1914, inspired by the German translation (1907) of Ebenezer Howard's Garden Cities of Tomorrow (London, 1902), Schmidt created the first

![](images/e61fb0339d0e0025ccfc8bb7b37bcd33bab6c2ff96bc7681b6d05202fb453d4c.jpg)

German Garden City, Hellerau. This large-scale undertaking incorporated housing, schools, a theater, and the factory for his firm, which expanded in 1907 to become the Deutsche Werkstatten GmbH Dresden-Hellerau und Muenchen (German Workshops, Inc., Dresden-Hellerau and Munich). The Munich-based Swiss architect and designer Hans Eduard von Berlepsch-Valendas, under the influence of his friend the architect Raymond Unwin, also disseminated English aims of artistic and social reform.8

Design reform ideas in Germany did not generate only from an advanced artistic consciousness; they were also promoted as a means of achieving economic supremacy. For example, in 1899 the sovereign ruler of the grand duchy of Hessen und bei Rhein, Grand Duke Ernst Ludwig, established an artists' colony on the Mathildenhöhe in Darmstadt with the hope that this undertaking would encourage production of high-quality decorative and applied arts throughout his state. An Anglophile, Ernst Ludwig had already introduced the Arts and Crafts to Germany in 1897 by commissioning Ashbee and Baille Scott to design two rooms at his Neues Palais in Darmstadt, which had been commissioned in 1864 by his grandmother Queen Victoria as a wedding present for his parents. Ernst Ludwig's enlightened patronage provided a valuable exampleher Grman sate; ew years laterhe russian Ministry  Trad nd Indusy hired modern artists to revive the ailing stoneware industries of the Westerwald, and the court of Weimar commissioned Henry Van de Velde, a Belgian designer who became active in Germany, to work with the local craftsmen of Thuringia to reform and modernize their design styles and production practices.

Works in the new style not only had to be conceived and executed, they also had to be mediated to the public. Justus Brinckmann, director of the Museum fur Kunst und Gewerbe (Museum of Art and Industry) in Hamburg, aimed to shape the taste of German designers and critics by collecting and exhibiting works of art from the past and the present, including the e lence. Brinckmann followed the path of Morris, not by actually working as a designer himself but by setting up opportunities for the new style to flourish.

Enthusiasm for Britain peaked around the turn of the century. After 1900 the embrace of British models began to be challenged—a result of the growing self-confidence of the German Arts and Crafts scene. According to the judgment of several art critics, Britain belonged to the German bloc," thus enjoying greater respect than France, but at the same time it was increasingly regarded as an economic and political rival. Furthermore, the British belief that handwork was the sine qua non of creativity was not accepted with as much conviction in Germany. Whereas handcrafting was a central (if not always realized) ideal for the English reformers, utility and li important to German designers. The issue of hand or machine—since Ruskin, a topic of constant debate in Britain—was for Germany a pragmatic option in favor of the machine. Nonetheless, this attitude did not develop overnight. In the quest for new product quality, Germany, too, had to first make the breakthrough via handicraft. Analogous to British precedent, numerous workshops and communities that made unique or limited production objects were set up around 1900. The German designers, however, used the model of the workshop selectively—the path they chose quickly led to mass production, albeit filtered with the British ideals of integrity, utility, and high-quality design for everyday life.

An accelerated pace of economic growth after unification ensured that Germany achieved in a few years what had evolved in Britain over several decades; German Arts and Crafts proponents even went beyond the original model of artists' guilds by setting up increasing numbers of workshops with highly developed production technology. By 1907 Peter Behrens was appointed the artistic consultant of the giant electricity firm Allgemeine Elektricitäts-Gesellschaft (AEG) in Berlin, and the Deutsche Werkbund was established as an advocacy association for industry, designers, and artisans. The ideal of handmade beauty, luxury, and spiritual edification was giving way to design professionalism and technical invention. A new, industrially motivated model had developed out of the original goal of the unity of design and execution, with the aim that objects of whatever kind would be well designed, functional, inexpensive, and above all economic assets suitable for export. Although particular aspirations of the Arts and Crafts movement continued to be significant, Germany had sloughed off the British example and was developing its own new Kultur der Dinge (culture of things)."

ART AND LIFE The reform movement of the late nineteenth century aimed to change conditions considered obsolete and incapable of meeting the challenges of the age. The leaders of the new movement in Germany—including designers, artists, architects, patrons, critics, and businessmen—wanted not only to transform the appearance of the products they created but also to improve the taste of the entire population. Traditional academic art had proved unsuitable for realizing this aim, and the applied arts were embraced as the medium by which to convey a new, young feeling for life to the people, and to open their senses to the modern world.

One attempt to address this goal was made by the Grand Duke Ernst Ludwig, who in 1899 had sponsored the founding of the artists' colony at Darmstadt. Building upon his successful experience with prior commissions of British artists Ashbee and Baillie Scott, as well as the German artist Otto Eckmann, to design interiors for his palace at Darmstadt, Ernst Ludwig envisioned an artists' colony as a prime opportunity to cast a spotlight on the potential of his capital seat as a center of art, culture, and economic progress. Alexander Koch, the influential editor and publisher of two widely read journals of the decorative arts, Innendekoration and Deutsche Kunst und Dekoration, also played an important role in support of the colony. "2

![](images/686dd2a0853e6be76734a8bb9ba1982904bc8548b1ac02333420f43e33dbccf3.jpg)

![](images/34048b7eb7b734e6dfd6d82256a09e063e8d3171e12b2e89241dbb331ffeb360.jpg)  
VNTER. DEM-ALERHCHSTEN.PROTECTORATE SR·KONIGL:HOHEIT.DE.GROSHERZOGS·VON·HETEN EIN.DOKUMENT.DEVTSGTER-KVNST

DARMITANT MAI- 0GOHER 1901 DIE-AVSSTELVNG DER KVNSTER-KOIONIE

![](images/c138db8a021a0a3570201f4e9265d1f1613cde9dfb14d2fa92ffa27d9c6aff88.jpg)  
DIE:KUNSTLER·KOLONIE:INDARMSTADTB BBEEHRTSICH ZU DER AM:I5-MAI0OD GODMORGENS-115: STATTFINDENDEN FEIERUCHEN-EROFFNUNGDER GGIBD AUSSTELLUNG:SEHR-ERGEBENST

![](images/41359dc6f100e5955bb795f348a434b1b0fcc592e6aa9b0f6e2eb57ac517494f.jpg)

Between 1898 and 1899, the Grand Duke selected the first group of seven colony members: Peter Behrens, Rudolf Bosselt, Paul Bürck, Hans Christiansen, Ludwig Habich, Patriz Huber, and Joseph Maria Olbrich.3 On April 24, 1900, the foundation stone of the studio building, the Ernst Ludwig House, was laid on the Darmstadt Mathildenhöhe. Residences for the artists were built at the same time. All were designed by Olbrich, except for the Behrens House. The public opening took place with grand ceremony on May 15, 1901 (3.4). The applied arts displayed in the studio building and salesrooms, together with the artists' own houses, were presented as an exhibition called Ein Dokument deutscher Kunst (A Document of German Art). This was the first large-scale exhibition of German Jugendstil. The opening celebrations included a drama by Georg Fuchs and Peter Behrens, Das Zeichen (The Sign), which was performed with great solemnity, complete with a chorus in Greek robes, on the steps of the studio building.The central message of the play, and of the colony itself, was the emergence of a new era that would integrate art and life. As Ruskin had proclaimed, the artist should stamp his individuality on the whole of life. The reform of art was also a reform of life.

A plaque of gratitude executed by Rudolf Bosselt and dedicated by the artists to the Grand Duke embodies the dramatic tone of the event—a priestlike figure enveloped in a halo and swirling incense fumes bears a model of the studio building (3.5). Behrens proclaimed that "everything that belongs to life should be infused with beauty." To create beauty, and imbue even the most humble objects with it, was the utopian aim. Nowhere else had there been such a theatrical demonstration in the name of the new movement; this was a heady mix of spirituality, symbolism, and the applied arts. The opening events served as a form of consecration: a new national culture was to be built and the shackles of old beliefs abandoned.'

![](images/97adbb94b11f56a557fedb8501d2fbb6bde42fe927faa2a3a470b291c596a5f3.jpg)

Paul Bürck's exhibition invitation, depicting an athletic young man with bare torso and a flowering sprig in his raised hand, represents these ideals (3.7). A sacred aura is also manifest in the exhibition poster designed by Peter Behrens (3.1). A female figure reaches up toward a crystal, the image of light and inspiration, which hovers above her between two ducal crowns.

One innovative aspect of the exhibition embodied the mission of unifying art and life—the opening of the members' houses, created as case studies of modern interior design, to the public. Thus it was not only a museum or an exhibition hall that provided the context for the new art and design products, but also the private houses of the artists. The visitors were given the opportunity to experience how the artists integrated art into their homes and into their lives.

Peter Behrens was the most successful of the group in achieving unity of the architecture and interiors. While not trained as an architect, Behrens convinced those in charge of the colony that he could design his own house. The dining room was central to his vision. Its white-painted furniture, tableware, and accessories all followed a basic geometric system of parallel linear arches and lancet ovals touching or overlapping one another (3.8 and 3.9). Even the table linen adhered to the same decorative principle, and the linear ornamentation on the table service and cutlery was repeated on the ceiling. No other room of the exhibition was worked out in such detail or expressed such exquisite beauty. In the music room, the furniture and doors were decorated with crystalline rhomboid patterns and with cast bronze and gilded ornament (3.10); the chair frames were stained black and inlaid with intarsia of wood.

Each room was designed as a Gesamtkunstwerk, a total work of art." After the cacophony of historicism, such rooms and houses created around 1900 reflected new ideals of clarity and unity. The artistry consisted of relating all objects to one another, in bringing the varied forms and media into harmony. An early signal in this direction had already been given by Henry Van de Velde's four rooms designed for Siegfried Bing's Paris gallery L'Art Nouveau, which Bing had presented at the Dresden exhibition of 1897. While the critics generally received these rooms favorably, they questioned the character of individual designs as showpieces rather than elements of a unified whole. More positively received were Richard Riemerschmid's music salon at Dresden (1899) and his room for an art lover at the Exposition Universelle in Paris (1900). In the end, however, all of these interior ensembles seemed forced and contrived, and it remained for

![](images/4cd082a4de48d0885b1580ba78079166afdda05e5045149087217c20e078ddd2.jpg)

![](images/290151dc62afaf579afc79839c8c03256c43fd9b35d89e9e0df6f469887df15f.jpg)

![](images/4e9868d33d0966f5b89749c42e36d2709dbd11da17f9fbc958cbc8e6cc0d1dae.jpg)

![](images/dd9450028cf149047f79df34d73ad1e386f84a78a2876395d83a98eff870c517.jpg)

Behrens and his rooms at Darmstadt to realize for the first time a design that communicated a true domesticity and an intimate atmosphere.

More artists soon turned to creating stylistically homogeneous rooms and harmonizing objects to enhance the experience of home life. This was the intention of Patriz Huber, who, around 1900, designed the interior of the house for the furniture manufacturer Julius Glückert, who also lived in the artists' colony and worked in collaboration with its members (3.12).'8 In 1901 Alexander Koch advertised an international competition in interior design for a Haus eines Kunstfreundes (House for an Art Lover). Thirtysix artists took part. It was no coincidence that among the most highly regarded entries were two representatives from Great Britain—Baillie Scott and Charles Rennie Mackintosh—a reflection of the high estimation in which the British were held and of their influence on domestic design in Germany (see 2.47).19

PUTRI2 HUBER M9U DARMSTAOT.

In autumn 1902 the Wertheim department store in Berlin invited several artists, among them Baillie Scott, Behrens, and Huber, to design rooms

for an ideal home. The commission was further demonstration of efforts to improve design standards through the unity of art and life. In the department store, art was placed in the context of ordinary commercial life; anyone shy of entering a museum could feel free to wander, have a look at things, and be encouraged to place an order. Behrens again presented a dining room, this time in dark wood, with robust, angular forms (3.13). The sensitive, gentle curves of the Darmstadt dining room had given way to a geometric rusticity; applied decoration was avoided, and ornamentation was integrated into the furniture's construction (3.14 and 3.15). The ceiling lamp was particularly interesting (unfortunately no example seems to have survived); it differed from the otherwise predominant hoop lamps in its vertically arranged squares and rectangles suspended at various heights. Rectilinear shapes also defined the carpet decoration and the tablewares, their insistent rhythm, not unlike electric circuits, an indication of the increasing technical awareness in Behrens's vocabulary of forms (3.16-18). With this design, Behrens had transformed his style to one of objective geometry and rational utility. Thus he was on a direct route to his designs for the appliances, advertisements, factories, and workers' housing of the AEG, and to the application of the Gesamtkunstwerk in the creation of a corporate identity (3.19).

![](images/9b3426a8e1d16e7c70dd479bb6794f8d5ecfda77b36d20711a342e05f89a02ea.jpg)

![](images/91b2abcc14f5a745c3c4aafa1bb7d24c6aacc51e767a091fe7663037cdba8deb.jpg)

![](images/ad3f5029db77c630f9a831379aa52945ec5cfbbe0fcb206663155278ea17690c.jpg)

## ART AND INDUSTRY In 1897, the same year that Ernst

Ludwig began promoting design reform   
with his commissions to Baillie Scott   
and Ashbee, a revolutionary experiment   
took place in Munich: the presentation of   
two small rooms at the International Art   
Exhibition at the Königliche Glaspalast   
(Royal Glass Palace) (3.21 and 3.22). The   
Kleinkunst (minor arts or applied arts) sec  
tion covered a mere fraction of the total   
area. It had taken considerable effort for   
the artists who collaborated on the creation of   
these two rooms—including Otto Eckmann, August   
Endell, Hermann Obrist, Bernhard Pankok, and Richard   
Riemerschmid-to be allowed to include these few   
examples of furniture, wrought iron, wall hangings, and   
embroidery (3.23). Yet the art world's response to these   
installations was one of astonishment. The exhibition brought   
together most of the designers whose works soon came to repres

![](images/ef0edc3599c7203ef2b51697cf955e3a247ea1547397cb95addd779c1d7ef9aa.jpg)

Jugendstil, and their designs were hailed as the beginning of a new German style. Wilhelm Bode, who later became the general director of the Berlin Museums, reviewed the exhibition, finding the work of the then little-known Richard Riemerschmid—for example, a buffet made of yew, oak, and rosewood, with iron mountings—worthy of special mention because of its notable simplicity (3.20).21

The desire for a contemporary decorative arts exhibition had been repeatedly expressed, and at first there seemed to be a clientele who wished to have their homes designed in the new style. To this purpose, several of the Glaspalast exhibitors, including Obrist, Pankok, Riemerschmid, and the newly recruited Bruno Paul, resolved in September of 1897 to set up a cooperative workshop, the Vereinigte Werkstätten für Kunst im Handwerk (United Workshops for Art in Handicraft) (3.24). The workshops, which became a limited liability company in 1898, were intended to produce high-quality goods and to deliver complete suites of room decoration. The workshops employed the latest technology and emphasized close cooperation between the designer and the craftsmen who executed the work. Cabinetmaking provided the nucleus of the enterprise, along with studios for embroidery, glass, and metal (3.25). Cast bronzes and fine enamelwork were also executed. Since each of the artists was self-taught as a designer, the collaboration with actual craftsmen was essential, particularly in choices of materials and in construction methods. Riemerschmid would certainly never have become such an outstanding furniture designer had he not been able to combine design practice with the experience of the workshops' craft production.

![](images/91c2c2ff3162a96a5dc0653ff0e8018bead6d65e64e0632396e6c0a0b79ef70c.jpg)

![](images/47592c4d0f5cc069b7d4f107aca947cc610b623ce176680b9508459cc93fcae4.jpg)

![](images/5c1adaeb0be79a441b2e5a3c5c782ad5942103a52a23f00e46e7fe300c71b256.jpg)

Munich was the cradle of the new movement in Germany. Commissions at this early stage, however, were few; therefore, several of the most respected Jugendstil artists, including Berens, ndell and Jakob Julu Scharvogel, let theciy. Two desgers, Obrist and Wilel von Debschitz, attempted to provide new creative opportunities in Munich by establishing in January 1902 the Lehr- und Versuchsateliers für Angewandte und Freie Kunst (Training and Experimental Studios for the Applied and Free Arts)—later renamed the Debschitzschule. One of the first institutions of its kind, the workshop-school devoted its entire curriculum to practical work studies, thus challenging the hitherto predominant reliance on the preparation of ornamental drawings as the basis of crafts education.² The school trained many major designers and craftsmen—among them, Friedrich Adler, Fritz and Karl Schmoll gen. Eisenwerth, Wolfgang von Wersin, and Carl Georg von Reichenbach—and many of them stayed on to become teachers. A

![](images/5bfdbd1829db4f55fcb44d9fb2205050485222477a206e9f0e434e16784b3d10.jpg)

progressive aspect of the Debschitzschule was that it did not differentiate between the training of men and women. However, as in other countries during this time, most women could find employment only in the traditionally feminine spheres of textiles and ceramics.

Some of the students at the Debschitzschule gained hands-on experience in applied arts through working with manufactories. For example, together with his training at the Debschitzschule, von Reichenbach studied glass production techniques with the Benedikt von Poschinger firm in the Bavarian Forest. He designed some remarkable glass, deftly handling the medium with idiosyncratic use of color; sculptural shaping; and thread, filigree, and prunt techniques (3.26).

![](images/72c6296ae6cc9a61a8ff8d4a6c610fd6de2653a6ed3c53c6a618342b6e9132be.jpg)

![](images/525b1418bc6a1ec42552ffa8cb9f2c21492bf9528f9f9709a612829442dd1908.jpg)

![](images/05ad1a57c826c943d915ebc615f1124e9393e12fe17214bc9adf1ccb33aa8d4f.jpg)

The new art developments in Germany around 1900 were characterized by numerous parallel expressions in regions across the country. While conditions varied from city to city, each of the endeavors sought to promote modern design. In Darmstadt, where the artists' colony had been established, Ernst Ludwig was truly idealistic about uniting art and life, but he was equally interested in exploiting the new design as an impetus to trade and industry in Hesse.2 Modern design could provide an opportunity for collaborations that would serve not only to improve taste and the quality

of production but also to boost economically weak parts of the province. Furniture, metalwork, stoneware, and earthenware—all were produced in the grand duchy.

Objects by Behrens, Christiansen (3.27), Huber, and Olbrich were made in workshops in Darmstadt and the surrounding areas: for example by Ludwig Alter, J. C. Boysen, Julius Glückert, and E. L. Vietor (3.28), in Darmstadt; M. J. Rückert in Mainz; Robert Macco in Heidelberg (3.29); and the ceramic factory at Wächtersbach.® Only porcelain, glass, and pewter, which had no local manufactories, were executed elsewhere—for instance, at the Gebr. Bauscher porcelain factory in Weiden (see 3.9), the Rheinische Glashütten AG, Cologne-Ehrenfeld, and the Eduard Hueck metalsmith company in Lüdenscheid. Other workshops were established within the colony during the subsequent period: in 1904, plans were initiated for Jakob Julius Scharvogel to move his ceramic manufactory from Munich, and in 1907 the Ernst Ludwig Press was founded as a private press modeled after Morris's Kelmscott Press, with Friedrich Wilhelm Kleukens as its director.2

At the turn of the century, Germany sought to assert itself as a major industrial and imperial power; simultaneously, it struggled to build a national identity following unification. One strategy to meet both needs involved reviving the flagging traditional earthenware and stoneware industries by hiring modern designers to update time-honored (but by now timeworn) forms and decoration. These efforts had the imprimatur of government support coupled with the enthusiastic participation of commercial companies, who saw their financial salvation in the hiring of modern designers. The factories in the region of Westerwald—in the eighteenth century the center for the manufacture of salt-glaze stoneware—had been eclipsed by the popularity of porcelain, a more refined material and one produced in other regions. These factories provided a paradigm of how Arts and Crafts ideals—local materials, integrity f production, utility—could be adapted to meet the desire to be both modern and German, qualities seen as necessary for the country's success in the new century. In 1900 the Westerwald Council asked Henry Van de Velde to provide designs for new stoneware vessels. While he incorporated the blue-gray body characteristic of the local vernacular, the decoration reflected his own individualistic style (3.32). For many other pieces, Van de Velde abandoned traditional colors entirely to work with copper reduction glazes (3.30).28

![](images/74c5af31c275e1f33383388f9de7c9dcb21fbfa4d6acf559159d792709cad524.jpg)

![](images/5b76f5cd1ca6e79444f589533d37ecc716ac08a004fad71b70d6037b8138fcb7.jpg)  
Jwy cer, c.101.Joseph Mara Olbrich, Robeacco

Other companies joined in this immediately acclaimed strategy. By 1900 Richard Riemerschmid had begun his collaboration with the manufacturer Reinhold Merkelbach (3.33). His jug demonstrates his interest in traditional materials (in this case, brown salt glaze and pewter) as well as forms. At the same time, his creation is a modern statement of simplicity and asrcin wial o ha ould ademol nthus ws ital sive production. Similarly, designs by Behrens for Westerwald companies achieved the same end, adopting the original stoneware blue-gray body but with an attenuated form and stylized decoration that could not be mistaken for a strictly traditional product (3.31).30

This formula of promoting national crafts by hiring designers to "modernize" them was repeated with many variations in other parts of the country. Already in the early 1890s Max Laeuger, professor at the Karlsruhe School of Arts and Crafts, had begun advising a pottery workshop in Kandern, near the Swiss border.3 From 1897 to 1914 he was the artistic director of this Tonwerke (clay pottery works). Laeuger's role was not primarily the execution of the pottery but is desn and decoation.He painted thevessels with colore slip, updatingtraditinal techniques characteristic of the Black Forest region (3.34). Like many other designers, such as Elisabeth Schmidt-Pecht in Konstanz and Theo Schmuz-Baudiss in Munich, Laeuger took inspiration fromfolk rtcovering hisvessels wit sronglytlne sylizd fowermo.

The issue of improving trade and industry while enhancing design standards was also central to the work of the Belgian architect and designer Henry Van de Velde in his first years in Germany at the turn of the century. Van de Velde moved to Weimar in 1902 by appointment to the Grand Duke Wiel Ernstaxony-Weimar. His patron, thecollector Harry Count Kesse, had assumed the artistic direction of Weimar's Museum of Art and Cultural History (an honorary post without payment) in the same year and planned to make the city into a new hub of European art. Van de Velde was asked to advise the struggling Thuringian handicraft industry and to provide the local craftsmen—above all, the potters and basket makers—with modern designs.3² As part of these efforts, Van de Velde established an Arts and Crafts seminar and a ceramic study collection that same year.

Van de Velde's ability to fundamentally improve the local crafts situation was only achieved, however, with the establishment of the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Arts and Crafts) at Weimar, which was intended to include apprentice workshops in accordance with the recommendations of the Prussian Ministry of Trade and Industry in Berlin. These would allow the apprentices to work directly with materials, a practice that had already been established at the Debschitzschule in Munich. "Information on the model of English arts and crafts and the success of the workshop training there underlined his [Van de Velde's] requirements."3 The school was built in two phases (during 1904 and 1905, and in 1911), with Van de Velde as its first director. Workshops were created for bookbinding, goldsmithing, enamel work, and weaving.

![](images/26c8a703c7d6589e0b5ba44b9a1a5f81521698c1c7a7b9f2f79be74af5a01d0f.jpg)

![](images/7bf4f068f0cfd3590036c1897392cd47c3f6d56b24e0eb68385bc93708b9db3a.jpg)  
e c. tr Bers Reiol an nW0He nVlWe chrReioerke

In 1903 and 1904 Van de Velde designed a dinner service for the Meissen porcelain manufactory that was available in two colors—blue and, much rarer, gold—and that became known as Peitschenhieb (Whiplash) (3.35). This service is an example of Meissen's experimentation with new product lines. Porcelain was a well-accepted tradition in German luxury decorative arts, but despite efforts by porcelain firms such as Meissen, KPM, and Nymphenburg to produce modern services designed by artists, these were not particularly successful in commercial terms. The fact of their production, however, was an important step on the path toward the democratization of taste in Germany.34

Van de Velde had declared himself a disciple of Williar Morris, but he read Morris selectively, embracing those aspects of his writing that emphasized the importance of individual expression. Van de Velde's greatest achievements took place during two periods: the time from 1895 to 1902 (before he moved to Weimar, although he had been working regularly in Germany since 1899), when he made his most important furniture, and from 1902 to 1906, when he worked intensively on metalware and ceramics. In autumn 1902, he received the commission from the State of Saxony-Weimar to design an enormous set of silver tableware comprising 335 pieces for the forthcoming marriage of the Grand Duke. The set was executed by the Weimar court jeweler Theodor Müller, in association with the Bremen silverware factory of Koch & Bergfeld, since the huge commission was too much for Müller's medium-sized enterprise. For Van de Velde, the wedding service meant the start of an extended and intensive preoccupation with silver hollowware, which served his clients—patrons and friends, like Harry Count Kessler and Karl Ernst Osthaus—very well: he created some of the most beautiful silver pieces of the twentieth century fo them (3.36).35

![](images/37aa9713953bb55e205ab3e67abaacc0bcb4815649f2e92983dccc8f9efb5117.jpg)

Although major artists in the reform movement—including Behrens, Christiansen, and Eckmann—came from Hamburg and other parts of northern Germany, the new style was accepted slowly in that part of the country. Impetus came from the directors of Hamburg's tw art museums: Alfred Lichtwark (Kunsthalle) and Justus Brinckmann (Museum f Art and Industry). Lichtwark led the movement for art education in Germany. He believed that the public had a key role to play in the elevation of taste and the appreciation of art (including, of course, all the applied arts). He was convinced that the makers of art needed to form an alliance with consumers, and that this could be achieved with practical ducation

Despite a shared affnity for French art, both Lichtwark and Brinckmann were also admir ers of the English Arts and Crafts movement, and both championed traditional German crafts. In his lectures, Brinckmann addressed the writings and works of Morris, Ashbee, and Crane. Around the time of the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 1900), he acquired not only numerous textiles from Morris & Co., but also a large-scale wall tapestry, The Adoration of the Magi, after designs by Morris and Edward Burne-Jones, as well as a copy of the Kelmscott Chaucer (see 2.21). Brinckmann also collected examples of Japanese, Scandinavian, and German folk art, as well as posters and samples of exemplary artistic bookbinding (see 7.21).

![](images/de6dc99c4ac375254701790e2b7369030b8058688ecc6f2c68485ab56090c3de.jpg)

An 1895 lecture on Scandinavian hand-weaving given by Brinckmann (and attended by the Trondheim [Norway] Museum of Applied Art director, Jens Thiis) spurred government support of a tapestry-weaving school in Scherrebek, a village in North Schleswig that had been annexed to Germany after the war between Germany and Denmark in 1864. The school opened in 1896. Brinckmann's assistant, Friedrich Deneken, was put in charge of the administration of the school; the first artistic director was Brinckmann's daughter Maria; and the Norwegian weaver Katrina Dons was engaged to teach weaving techniques.38 Deneken's friend Otto Eckmann supplied the design for Schwanenteppich (Swan Tapestry), the first wall hanging of the manufactory, which was subsequently executed more than fifty times. This was followed in the next seven years by more than one hundred designs from almost all of the renowned German representatives of the reform movement. Thus an age-old technique, which had practically disappeared from common practice, was revived. Eckmann supplied most of the designs, creating thirteen original patterns. Besides Swan Tapestry, the wall hanging Weiher im Mondschein (Pond in Moonlight) is one of the incunabula of German Jugendstil textile art; it was displayed at the 1897 Royal Glass Palace exhibition in Munich (see 3.21 and 3.22).

![](images/6abe2e2b7722be78cec0e87b50a96f101d363394e53ffd7d626b8533f0d8d83b.jpg)

![](images/2321e8ad5bd2a52d5a70dd2f32f80e22d85f4dba06315bac9b36372fefb77e92.jpg)

Another early associate at Scherrebek was the illustrator and craftsman Heinrich Vogeler. His two wall hangings Dornröschen (The Sleeping Beauty) and Der Spaziergang (The Walk) were inspired by fairy tales and sagas (3.37). Idealized figures of medieval kings, knights, and princesses are also depicted in his printed graphics. His light touch as a draftsman is at times reminiscent of Aubrey Beardsley, and Vogeler was undoubtedly influenced by British art. In 1898 The Studio devoted an essay to his work. Vogeler was practically unique in northern Germany as an all-around designer. His oeuvre included book decoration, furniture, lamps, textiles, porcelain, flatware, and even houses.41

In 1895 Vogeler bought and renovated an old farmhouse in the village of Worpswede, near Bremen. The house, known as the Barkenhoff, became the meeting place for the Worpswede art community, which included the painters Otto Modersohn; his wife, Paula Modersohn-Becker; Clara Rilke-Westhoff; and her husband, the poet Rainer Maria Rilke. Set in the middle of a large garden, the Barkenhoff was the scene of poetry readings, concerts, and performances—a refuge dedicated to art. Of all the German models involved in the reform movement, it was the one that most closely approximated the English ideal of a retreat to the country. At this point, stylistic elements derived from vernacular traditions began to play a greater

role in Vogeler's work, and this tendency, particularly in his furniture design, led to the elimination of elaborate decoration and the increasing use of traditional motifs, such as the rose and the tulip.

In 19o8 Vogeler and his brother Franz founded the Worpsweder Werkstätte, specializing in rustically inspired furniture decorated with chip-carving and painting. The chair seats were rush, and the high backs were cut out with decoration such as owls, the traditional emblem of Lower Saxony (3.38). Vogeler also demonstrated his growing commitment to social issues with his designs for worker housing; his main objective was to provide well-designed, high-quality products at a price that would be acceptable to people of the region. To achieve this, the components of his furniture were made by machine, with only the final finishing and decoration done by hand.

Antecedent to Vogeler's volkstümliche Möbel (traditional and popular furniture) was Riemerschmid's so-called

![](images/2b5d06b28c64ebbdde4d2224108a91a6186b777106c9c856ee1f657986296aa9.jpg)

Maschinenmöbel (machine furniture) (3.39—3.41).2 Conceived as serially manufactured furniture, it was a breakthrough in meeting the demand to combine design quality with an affordable price. For German design, which was increasingly characterized by sobriety and functionalism, it provided concrete proof that the decisive criterion for improving the quality of life was not found in the elimination of the machine, as Ruskin had demanded, but in the democratic maxim of offering good art for all. Many critics had observed that the English Arts and Crafts movement had experienced the problem of expensive handicraft's incompatibility with wide accessibility; German reformers thought they had solved it. Like no other artist of the reform movement, Riemerschmid created an oeuvre that reflected both the crafted, exclusive, luxury furniture of the haute bourgeoisie, as well as the popular, traditional furniture of the less well-off classes. Riemerschmid's experimentation with simpler furniture began in 1900, when he designed a rustic ten-piece living room suite at a minimal manufacturing price (3.42 and 3.43). The decisive factor here was the market-oriented assignment: narrowing the designer's creative scope and forcing him to work with a simple, yet not stringent, concept.

In the following years, Riemerschmid's new ideas were disseminated through a collaboration with his brother-in-law Karl Schmidt, the proprietor of the Dresden Werkstätten and soon to become the founder of the Garden City Hellerau. In 1906 Riemerschmid and Schmidt presented ensembles of machine furniture (each with living room and bedroom suites and kitchen) in several price classes: "An all-inclusive program of this type for three social classes, which could be diversely combined and extended, was an impressive innovation in its logical consistency."4The use of machine-saw compliant wood that could be easily joined with wooden bolts resulted in an attractive price; also, the furniture could be shipped cost-effectively in a disassembled state, with the components first put together in the workshop branches. The German national economist Friedrich Naumann had proposed the idea of German machine-made Volksmöbel (people's furniture) already in 1904; Maschinenmöbel fulfilled this mandate, so that the Deutsche Kunst l

Within the space of ten years, Riemerschmid had progressed as a furniture designer through a range of styles—encompassing Gothic case furniture and reform furniture for the upper middle classes—to home design for the less well-off classes, including a logically conceived, practical, and attractive form of people's furniture. He had discarded the motifs of his native Bavaria and instead created a supra-regional style, which in its simplicity was felt to be universally applicable. In Germany the idea of supplying functional home furnishings and appliances, of educating taste and thus shaping a kind of national identity, was something that carried great weight. Providing quality goods for the consumer was valued higher than showing consideration for the human workforce, which Morris had championed. Correspondingly, the Arts and Crafts scene in Germany never really had a problem with multiple or even mass production, which could potentially alienate men and women from their work.

![](images/e9bb1fe05b6a630fad53173835ea8f4f78484c12bd9148ba9f78d7a712ae0e82.jpg)

![](images/df1ff3b35a77a7d7092138f14828cf688c326e904fe1ccc4de98508529700ab0.jpg)

Concerning the national economic agenda, what mattered increasingly after 1900 was progress based on rational criteria, which was seen as providing a better lifestyle, and, consequently, would require standardized production and type. This issue was where the reform movement split into two camps: on one side were the advocates of individualistic artwork, which represented crafts and luxury art; on the other were the supporters of industrial design, functionalism, and efficient production. This conflict had not yet fully erupted, though. When the Deutscher Werkbund was founded in 1907 as an association of artists, craftsmen, and producers for improving German quality products, this contradiction was disregarded, but it came to define the events of the following years. Thus Henry Van de Velde was stillable to have court silver made in Weimar and Karl Ernst Osthaus could start up a silversmith's workshop in Hagen after the models of the Wiener Werkstätte in Vienna and the Georg Jensen company in Copenhagen, while around the same time Peter Behrens could design high-technology appliances for the AEG (see 3.19).46

The contradiction of these two principles—exemplified by the artistic, unique piece and the industrial, standard product—erupted in a public argument at the Cologne Werkbund conference in 1914, during which the agenda put forward by Hermann Muthesius as the representative of industry and the promoter of commercial interests clashed with the protest of artists such as Endell, Van de Velde, and Walter Gropius. Muthesius and his supporters saw progress as a rational pact between designer and producer. His arguments were rejected, which led to his withdrawal from the Werkbund executive board." Yet in the end, the ideas of industrial design, standardization, and cooperation between producer and artist were called for more than ever after World War I. In comparison, crafted luxury products appeared more problematic for the future; at any rate, they never managed to answer the needs or ameliorate the condition of the masses.

ART AND NATIONAL IDENTITY Germany, like many other European countries around 1900, was searching for its own national style and cultural identity. A look at the art literature of the turn of the century reveals the considerable attention paid to the topic. What is most striking is the ubiquitous assertion that design reform could create "healthy" German art and combat "signs of decay." This seems like the incantation of a magic spel, conjured up in the hope that the inner unity of the nation would be advanced by a unified concept of art. In nationalist circles, the country had always been perceived as a victim of outside influences. Now, leadership and resolution were called for, so that Germany might command its own fate. As a newly founded empire, Germany had to develop a unique culture, one that could match Britain and France. In general, it was thought that French art should be suppressed as un-German, and British art, despite its estimable qualities, should be considered with a more critical eye. There was a noticeable trend from around 1900 of increasing reservations concerning the British model, for it was perceived to be mired in individual artistry and not headed toward a comprehensive reform of life and society.

What were the criteria of national identity? The primary goals of late-nineteenth-century reform had already been formulated by Britain: the return to well-made goods, their greater accessibility through designers collaborating with manufacturers, the desire for logical consruction, and the use of materials that would reflect the nature  the region where the jects were made. Germany, however, was not striving, in the long run, for beauty and elegance of conn   ly    qual w whi leb inational identityheoverstimulated palatcraves for plaibrown bre

A nationally entrenched culture had to be democratic: the new art had to possess properties to benefit everyone. Since entitlement to equality sailed under the national flag, it did not involve a risk for the ruling classes. It was obvious: Germany had to define itself as a modern nation; only the raising of standards could prepare it for the future. Even the German states that preferred to distance themselves from Prussia and the German imperial house—for example, Hesse under Grand Duke Ernst Ludwig—shared these intentions. The more prominent the claim to equality, the more expansive became the national base upon which a new Germany was to grow. The historicism of Renaissance grotesquerie and Rococo were scorned and replaced by native vegetal decoration. Traditional rustic furniture, jewelry, and dress increasingly became the focus of museum interest, proving—in the opinion of the advocates of folk art and native traditional art—that there was sufficient creative potential in the people; it had been buried by nineteenth-century industrialization and only had to be revived. Activating these forces would trigger the revival of a homogeneous national culture.

Allusions to fairy tales, folk tales, and sagas—invented histories of the mystical dawn of time and great heroes of the past—formed an intellectual context for the discussion surrounding the reintroduction of old techniques. During the period around 1900, design reformers sought ate

Some endeavored to protect the ancient rustic and peasant customs, and to breathe new life into whatever had been precipitated by Heimatkunst (native traditional art and architecture); others wished to revive national traditions by means of handicraft, placing crafts such as ceramics and textiles on a "peasant" basis once more. In contrast, the progressive representatives of art and culture—Josef August Lux, Friedrich Naumann, and Muthesius among them—applied the concept of folk art in an entirely different way. They did not associate it with a renaissance of art in the native rustic style but with a practical, reasonably priced, and highquality utility art that would beneft all classes of people. This included the early standardized furniture lines of Bruno Paul and Richard Riemerschmid, as well as the home appliances of Peter

Behrens: prototypes without personal gesturing, involving a strong technical element compliant with the work of an engineer.51

Initially Germany addressed the issue of its new functional art in terms of its educational value, with the main emphasis on the recovery of a healthy society and the raising of taste. Later, in a second phase, the German art industry focused more on the challenge of international competition. This meant that the agenda of the Arts and Crafts movement, which originally served as an instrument of reform and part of a healing process, was being suppressed more and more by considerations of cultural and mercantile politics. A modern movement that had been formulated by men such as Muthesius, Behrens, and Riemerschmid could have been the source of an enduring national culture, but the idea existed only briefly, until the outbreak of World War I. After the war, the traditionalists took over the helm once more. When the modern movement energetically and rigorously responded in the 1920s with the Bauhaus, the subsequent backlash proved all the more vehement, even state-ordained. The dream of a unified culture embracing all levels of society, which had seemed within the nation's grasp between 1900 and 1914, was never fulfilled.

Translated from the German by Abigail Prohaska

# AUSTRIA: IDEALISM or REALISM

![](images/9d811f211a00e5464ef732eb8a277a848555cae1b260d2182197fce9ef1967b1.jpg)

![](images/ebe6ab038dd8f73da2245e75958874f5225b44e98fd8c18bd5cf933610f293b7.jpg)

A exhibiting body, the Künstlerhaus, and formed their own society, commonly known as the Vienna Secession (4.1). The Secessionists—led by the group's first president, the painter Gustav Klimt—were outward looking. Believing the culture of their own city to be parochial, they wanted to bring in the best of modern art from the rest of Europe. This was a key moment in the history of modern Viennese art. Six years later in 1903, two leading members of the Secession, the architect Josef Hoffmann and the painter and designer Koloman Moser, set up a craft workshop in Vienna called the Wiener Werkstätte with the backing of a textile industrialist, Fritz Wärndorfer. Its sophisticated products stand at the center of any account of the Arts and Crafts movement in Vienna (4.2 and 4.3). The Werkstätte was partly modeled on the Guild of Handicraft, the workshops of the English Arts and Crafts designer C. R. Ashbee, and it is often seen as evidence of the remarkable influence of the British Arts and Crafts movement in Europe and America around 1900, Modernity in Viennese architecture and applied art is thus intimately connected with its response to the outside world. While accepting the importance of these influences in the formation of the Arts and Crafts movement in Vienna, this essay aims to interpret the Werkstätte and other Arts and Crafts work in the context of the city's own history and culture. Outside influence, particularly from Britain, was strong, but Viennese Arts and Crafts were distinctly Viennese.

AUSTRIA, INDUSTRY, AND URBAN LIFE The circumstances of the Arts and Crafts movement in Austria were different from those of the movement in Britain. First, the country had experienced little industrial development. In Britain, the birthplace of the Industrial Revolution, the Arts and Crafts movement was in part a reaction against industrialization, one stimulated by the mid-nineteenth-century writings of A. W. N. Pugin and John Ruskin, which criticized the quality of British everyday life in aesthetic and moral terms. The economy of the Austrian Empire, by contrast, was still dominated by agriculture and handcrafts. The making of everyday useful objects was to a great extent under the rigid control of guilds, which were opposed to free trade until after the mid-nineteenth century.' Industrial production, when it did develop, was concentrated almost exclusively in the crown lands of Bohemia and Moravia. Major industrial cities like those in Britain and Germany did not exist. As a result, Arts and Crafts ideas in Austria focused on aesthetic matters rather than on society at large, and that was only toward the end of the nineteenth century.

Second, the origins and the leading players of the Arts and Crafts movement were confined to a single city. Vienna was the capital, the imperial seat, and the administrative and cultural center of the Hapsburg Empire. It was a city shaped by the Hapsburg monarchy and centuries of Catholicism. And it was large. By 1828 Vienna and its suburbs had a population of 290,000,

![](images/42132ff504343042b2b16fab230f3e9bc128d568d18fc88d40ac832831ce6908.jpg)

![](images/2ba94c674cda5b192a6ac5f49b082010f4b59db6a50a5c6bebf8ea63dc9cd5fc.jpg)

making it the third largest city in Europe after London and Paris. Between 1857 and 1869 the population grew from 476,000 to 607,000, and by 1891 it had reached 1,356,000. Vienna was the center of a multinational empire, and a magnet for those seeking work and a career, but because it had almost no industry, it did not have an industrial proletariat. Thus people in Austria did not necessarily associate the city with poverty and

![](images/f6deced31fcad6012741f8f6f0a950eb09b8b51e7d64141767c4ae2bf87ecdde.jpg)

moral depraviyTheiy iewas neverdemoize andtherewas otendency  lba life and establish artists' colonies in the countryside, as was the case in England.

The people in Vienna who had the wealth and progressive taste to buy objects that reflected an Arts and Crafts ideology—and after 1903 this means almost exclusively the products of the Wiener Werkstätte—were the rich, and mainly Jewish, haute bourgeoisie. These were, for themos partmilhat hamove  Vinafrom Germany he Austrincown lans an had prospered in the second generation. The new style of turn-of-the-century Vienna was connected much more with local traditions, such as folk art and the solid, unpretentious furniture of the early nineteenth century known as Biedermeier, than with the conventional Baroque vocabulary of the imperial court and the old Austrian aristocracy. Having these associations, it offered the haute bourgeoisie cultural assimilation and social differentiation. It encouraged an independent, contemporary, and Viennese form of expression at the same time that it fostered the haute bourgeoisie's traditional, prestige-oriented culture, which was based on an aristocratic lifestyle. The Viennese critic Berta Zuckerkandl saw this in 1905 when she reviewed an exhibition of work by the Wiener Werkstätte at the Galerie Miethke (4.4): "We strongly recommend collectors, the connoisseurs of the beauty of the past, to see this exhibition.. They will rediscover the delight of refined enjoyment.. From the solid ground of functionalism [Sachlichkeit] has sprung the full-

BEFORE THE ARTS AND CRAFTS: PROGRESSIVE ARCHITECTURE AND DECORATIVE ART IN VIENNA, C. 1860-C. 1895 In their study of the Arts and Crafts, Elizabeth Cumming and Wendy Kaplan identified the four principles of the movement as unity of the arts, joy in work, individual artistic expression, and emphasis on regional identity. Between about 1860 and the early 1890s, these ideas were only partly present in the progressive architecture and decorative art of Vienna. In some ways these decades anticipated the movement, and in other ways they did not.

The unity of the arts was mainly understood as a relationship between architecture, painting, and sculpture, rather than between the fine and applied arts, as William Morris, the leading figure of the Arts and Crafts movement in Britain, had recommended. Architecture was much more artistically advanced in Vienna than sculpture and painting, so the unity of the arts implied that painting and sculpture could reach the same artistic quality. Thus, Heinrich von Ferstel's Italian Renaissance design for the Museum für Kunst und Industrie (Museum of Art and Industry; now the MAK, Museum fur angewandte Kunst—Museum of Applied Arts) of 1868 was a model for the integration of these three fine arts. At the same time, the applied arts were not completely forgotten: the museum was also the first building in Vienna to achieve coherence in every detail, starting from the architectural proportions and materials right through to the design of the door handles. In the 1870s and 1880s the unity of the arts was seen as a principle to be observed in the artistic handling of a particular task. This was the view of Jakob von Falke, the second director of the museum and one of the first in Vienna to attempt to break out of the aesthetic cul-de-sac of historicism." Later, about 1900, Viennese designers approached artistic unity more ambitiously, as aceat pripl i  wn i,the ar point or Gerktotal work t.

The moral dimension of the Arts and Crafts had a part to play in progressive Vienna, but it remained at a general, social level, unconnected with the personal notion of joy in work. It was a question of the relationship between designer, producer, and consumer. This view is a constant in Vienna. We find it as early as the lecture at the opening of the Museum of Art and Industry in 1871 by its founder and first director, Rudolf von Eitelberger, and then several decades later in the 1905 Arbeitsprogramm (Work Program) of the Wiener Werkstätte. Both deplore a society in a state of radical change, with its culture of superficiality and false luxury brought about by economic speculation, cheap mass production, and false artistic expression.6

Artistic individuality was strong, especially in architecture. It was much more marked in progressive Viennese architecture than in the equivalents in Berlin and Paris, and for the first time, an issue was made of the naming of designers responsible for handmade goods.' As in England, though, individuality was seen to be hindered by its expression in historicist terms. In his Aesthetics of Handicraft, von Falke wrote that historical models restricted the quest for a contemporary style. They were at best an interim, if necessary, stage on the road to creative freedom.8

On the whole, the Viennese were negative about national or regional identity in these decades. Although the German Renaissance style was available as a way of expressing the authority of Austria within an empire of many nations and 54 million people, it was too closely

identified with the power of the newly unified Germany for political comfort. In the end the Viennese preferred the Italian Renaissance style, because its tectonic clarity helped them with the problem of structure and ornament. The decision to build the new Museum of Art and Industry in an Italian Renaissance style confirmed this choice. It was only later, around the turn of the century, that a regional spirit developed.

The leading progressive architect in latenineteenth-century Vienna was Otto Wagner. He trained in Vienna, and at the beginning of his career his work was inspired by the Renaissance. By 1877 he had reinterpreted this into a more personal form of expression— in his apartment block at Schottenring 23 (4.5). In 1889 he called this transformation a "free renaissance that has absorbed our genius loci." Wagner, however, did not identify him-

![](images/a7982b8f833af603c0e36cc77d9498dc6b169109c80ce4aa40fe9e8b72ce42c9.jpg)

drive ut of whic would evolve  "NutStil," afunctional, or utility, style His concept was thus defined in terms of function, not aesthetics, and in this sense he can be seen as a precursor of the modern age. If we think of Viennese architecture at this time as hovering, intellectually, between functionalism on the one hand and artistic expression on the other—in other words, between realism and idealism—then Wagner decides the matter in a practical, inclusive way. He thought that a good architect should have an acquired practical knowledge, but he should also have indiviua ceative talent.ny the artist who coulactually build deserved the tiearchit Two of his favorite sayings were, "Anything impractical cannot be beautiful" and "Artis sola domina necessitas" (Art serves only one mistress, function).

Wagner's idea of a functional style also had an economic dimension; it implied the use of artistic resources. In this respect he was like von Eitelberger, the leader of the Viennese designreform movement, who thought that local creative talent could compete economically on the international market. This idea became effective at the turn of the century, when the Viennese art critic and biographer of Wagner, Josef August Lux, described the city's artistic avant-garde as that invaluable economic commodity TALENT."3 Design became a marketable commodity. In the

Salonecke Entwurf Professor Joseph Hoffmann bentwood industry and elsewhere, it was no longer the manufacturer's name that sold a product, but that of the designer, as demonstrated in an advertisement for Kohn factory furniture that emphasized Hoffmann (4.6). From 1903 onward, the Wiener Werkstätte was to make the elevated status of the designer an important part of its marketing.

In 1893 the Viennese art historian Alois Riegl published his standard textbook on the history of ornament, Stilfragen: Grundlegungen zu einer Geschichte der Ornamentik. Riegl interpreted works of art as the product of a struggle between their material properties and a creative drive in human nature, which he called the will to form. (Here is another example of the tension in Viennese thought between realism and idealism.) This view places great faith in individual artistic expression, and because its most direct manifestation is ornament, Riegl's interpretation promotes the value of the applied arts. This book was influential in progressive art circles devoted to the demolition of traditional hierarchies. In 1899 the art critic Ludwig Hevesi described the new methods of teaching drawing at the Kunstgewerbeschule (School of Applied Arts), which was attached to the Museum of Art and Industry. Under Hoffmann, it was

no longer pictorial representation but ornament that was most important. Nature was broken down into its elementary formal components, and the capacity for abstraction was encouraged in this way. 1g

We can see Riegl's influence in the role of ornament in the Vienna Secession's monthly journal, Ver Sacrum (The Sacred Spring), which began publication in 1898. In Ver Sacrum, ornament becomes an art form in its own right, and the distinction between the fine and applied arts has been eliminated, in accordance with the tenets of the Arts and Crafts movement. Decorative vignettes form a striking part of the page layout; they are given their own captions citing the name of the designer, and their status is acknowledged as equivalent to that of painting, sculpture, or architecture. All this seems very different from Wagner's functionalism, and yet we find an article by Wagner in Ver Sacrum, "Die Kunst im

![](images/83a5c2230336181b45087589deb12729d4c751ef153f1b50160ab58e3b5eb2c1.jpg)

Gewerbe" (Art in Industry), which is introduced with a self-consciously artistic decorative initial, identified as Wagner's own creation (4.8).'" The illustration shows one of the first modern pieces of Viennese furniture, the armchair Wagner designed between 1898 and 1899 for the dining room of his apartment in Köstlergasse in Vienna. The text sums up the state of the arts in Vienna at that time.

In 1894 Wagner was appointed professor of architecture at the Vienna Academy of Arts. This confirmed the informal status he already had, as the center of a Wagnerschule (a group of disciples) that included Josef Hoffmann. Wagner led these young men out of the impasse of historicism, guiding them by the twin lights of

function and individual artistic expression. His appointment to the academy in 1894 marked the beginning of the actual break with historicism in Vienna.

THE DEVELOPMENT OF THE ARTS AND CRAFTS MOVEMENT IN VIENNA The ArtS and CraftS movement in Vienna developed rapidly in the second half of the 1890s, and the impulse for this came, in part at least, from Britain. The quiet penetration of Arts and Crafts ideas can be traced in the Museum of Art and Industry's library acquisitions. Before 1885 the library's British holdings included only technical works on art by Ruskin and two picture books by Walter Crane. In the late 1880s and early 1890s, under the directorship of von Falke, it bought two works by Ruskin that were seminal for the Arts and Crafts, The Stones of Venice and The Seven Lamps of Architecture. In the late 1890s and early 1900s, under the Anglophile director Arthur von Scala, the library purchased more books by Ruskin, picture books and Arts and Crafts essays by Walter Crane, and several books by William Morris in German translation, including his early lectures, Hopes and Fears for Art. In 190o it also bought two books printed and bound at Elbert Hubbard's Roycroft Press in East Aurora, New York, suggesting that Vienna was beginning to take note of the Arts and Crafts movement in the United States. This suggestion is strengthened by the fact that there were copies of the American magazine The Craftsman in the library of the Wiener Werkstätte, and also by the comment of the critic Hermann Bahr, made as early as 1898, that the United States played an important role in the development of Viennese Arts and Crafts, especially in the understanding and artistic treatment of materials. '6

The new artistic aspirations of the movement became public in 1897, when the younger and more progressive artists from the Künstlerhaus left to form the Vienna Secession. The Secessionists were progressive in their modernism and in their internationalism. Over the entrance to their newly built exhibition rooms, they inscribed "Der Zeit ihre Kunst, Der Kunst ihre Freiheit" (To every age its art, to art its freedom), and they displayed work by artists from France, Germany, Belgium, and Britain. They were also progressive in embracing the decorative arts. In the first issue of their journal, Ver Sacrum, the editors proclaimed their mission: "And here we appeal to you all, without any distinction of class or wealth. We know no difference be hig nd 'ler bw art or the i nart or te po. Ar s or vy Thus Vienna, without actually naming its English models, adopted the ideas of William Morris and the Arts and Crafts movement.

The first manifestation of Arts and Crafts ideology in Viennese applied art can be seen in Josef Hoffmann's early furniture designs. Honesty of expression was embodied in the plain softwood boards, sawn so that their outlines also acted as ornament. Hoffmann designed the bedroom suite for the house of the painter Ernst Stöhr, a founding member of the Secession who was receptive to this kind of simplicity and averse to furnishings that expressed convention or prestige. The suite includes a cupboard (in this exhibition), a double bed, night tables, and a chest made of softwood formerly stained green (4.7). The combination of bed and chest is medieval in type and so is the plank construction, but there is no specific medieval source, and the plank construction itself has been transformed into a decorative motif. The idea of a useful object designed in an artistic spirit was also adopted in a competition to design table glass for an ordinary household, organized by the Museum of Art and Industry in 1899. The design by Koloman Moser that won the competition was executed in several decorative variants and successfully sold by the glass distribution firm E. Bakalowits & Söhne (4.9).

In the same year as the founding of the Secession, 1897, Arthur von Scala became director of the Museum of Art and Industry and the Kunstgewerbeschule. Von Scala was an Anglophile and a personal friend of the director of the South Kensington Museum in London.® In the tradition of English design reformers like Henry Cole and Owen Jones, he encouraged art teaching that was focused on draftsmanship and designing for industry; he did not fully support the Viennese avant-garde. Under his directorship, the museum's new official publication, Kunst und Kunsthandwerk, begun in 1898, was richly illustrated and embellished with English-style typograpy  he  s is a Wic ll he  V y h, though he stressed that what was needed was "indigenous accomplishments" along similar lines.'® Wickhoff described the commercial English furniture that von Scala promoted, as well as the revived interest in local Empire and Biedermeier work, which, like the English work, was traditional, functional, and perceived, at that time, as made for the middle class.

Joining forces with these English sources of inspiration, which were supported by, among others, a traditionally Anglophile Austrian aristocracy, were the ideas of the Arts and Crafts movement. Reports in Kunst und Kunsthandwerk in 1899 included reviews of modern English bookbindings and an excellent overview of the various English craft guilds. By 1900 the dam seems to have burst and the English Arts and Crafts movement became the leading topic of the journal. In 1900 Kunst und Kunsthandwerk included a detailed article about the craft reforms of C. R. Ashbee and a review of the Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society's sixth exhibition in London by Count Vinzenz Latour.2 In 1901 it published a long article on M. H. Baillie Scott.22

Von Scala's direct influence on art education came to an end in 1899, when the Kunstgewerbeschule became independent under the directorship of the painter Felician Baron von Myrbach. In the same year Josef Hoffmann, then twenty-nine, and Koloman Moser, then thirty-one, joined the school. They were appointed professors in 1900. The arrival of von Myrbach, Hoffmann, and Moser was one of those strokes of luck that brings the right people together in the right place at the right time, giving them the opportunity to put their ideas into practice. Von Myrbach was not important as a painter or draftsman, but he understood the signs of the times. He carried out a thorough reform of the curriculum, setting up workshops and reorienting the school toward practical training, of the sort introduced into some British art schools in the 1890s.² The pottery workshop, started in 1899, was the first of many. A potter was employed and two wheels purchased for the ceramics class, which, until then, had only produced designs on paper (4.10 and 4.1). For the first time in the history of the school, the students were put directly in touch with materials. Design and execution were united once more. Through their creative teaching, Moser and Hoffmann fostered the independent, innovative ceramics style of the school; at the same time, they endeavored to introduce their students to the realities of business life, making contact with nationally important manufacturers who would execute and sell their designs. The manufacturers with whom students—as well as Moser and Hoffmann— collaborated included Johann Backhausen & Söhne for textiles (4.12); Johann Lötz Witwe glassworks (4.13); and the bentwood factory of Jakob & Josef Kohn and the Prag-Rudniker wicker factory for furniture (4.14).

![](images/5fa15dac777a092bbaedd6774c94ccf15054ba96b2a0a364d459dde999c7fdb6.jpg)

Jutta Sika's coffee service was designed for the Viennese porcelain factory of Josef Böck (4.15). Sika's career was typical of many young designers in Vienna. She trained at the Kunstgewerbeschule under Moser between 1897 and 1902, and she designed for various firms besides Böck and the Bakalowits glass-distribution firm. She was also a founding member of the organization Wiener Kunst im Hause (Viennese Art in the Home). This was essentially the first group of students from the Kunstgewerbeschule trained under the triumvirate of von Myrbach, Moser, and Hoffmann. These graduates emerged full of enthusiasm for the idea of developing objects from the original design through to eventual manufacture. They did not have workshops,

![](images/6a71420e1ff9df054c3261e15736f47d9375175337e7eeda0aa9c66bc63514c2.jpg)  
4.10 Vase, c. 1902. Kunstgewerbeschule, Vienna 4.11 Vase, c.1903-5. Kunstgewerbeschule, Vienna

![](images/a3f56dda9a13fd20d4b05687c47f7df1db692a31324354c942faed05c9ff32fb.jpg)

![](images/1a957e62286f6b27000853a415ca445d1535d596ffd2d560a6564375398e2cf6.jpg)

![](images/1a022566d9de7b823d45508dc0663cb5853afa815b84c83018518d5ebb73f8f2.jpg)

but they formed themselves into an exhibiting body in 1901. Working with leading manufacturers, they created fully furnished interiors at their first exhibition, which opened during Christmas of that year.24 They were a precursor of the Wiener Werkstätte.

At the Kunstgewerbeschule, Moser, Hoffmann, and von Myrbach believed that they understood the British Arts and Crafts movement better than von Scala at the Museum of Art and Industry, whose outlook was tied to English antiques and the earlier design-reform traditions of South Kensington. (Although the Wiener Werkstätte became the principal Arts and Crafts workshop in Vienna, the museum did not exhibit or buy any of its goods until Eduard Leisching replaced von Scala as director in 1909.) Hoffmann was invited to organize the Secession's exhibition for the autumn of 1900,

![](images/10661fc23ab036e5087fe52f8b5c270526b4a1d0c9022c92849702e15dde6f30.jpg)

and he decided that it should be devoted todecorative art. In the spring of 00, von Myrbach was planning a trip to England to visit Ashbee's Guild of Handicraft. Hoffmann wrote von Myrbach, explaining his intentions for the autumn exhibition:"o present an overview of the entire modern stn n arts an cra where theyar t home. It woud be wel worth the efrt how l Egsh rt and ca  nd rl Va wo i hea har c. Hel we have seen has been either English antiques r English goods made for exprt.

For the Viennese, this eighth exhibition of the Secession, which ran from November 3 to December 27, 1900, amounted to an international display of strength in the wake of the 1900 Exposition Universelle in Paris. The show was almost entirely devoted to the applied arts, and English and Scottish work proved to be prophetic for the stylistic and technical development of Viennese arts and crafts. Indeed, the Viennese were confronted with two different worlds at the exhibition: the rectangular work of C. R. Ashbee (4.16) versus the sensuous work of Charles Rennie Mackintosh, with its subtle curves and smooth paintwork that one cannot help stroking (4.17). It was as if the English and the Scottish exhibits had, uncannily, presented to the Viennese the very tension between realism and idealism that characterized their own situation. In his review of the exhibition, the Viennese critic Ludwig Hevesi described Ashbee's furniture as plain brown bread compared to the "Lucullan menu" of Viennese furniture. He imagined Ashbee's designs coming "from a square planet inhabited by square, burly peasants. Everything upright, rectangular, ninety degrees. It is actually English Biedermeier, simple, capable, cumbersome."26 Hevesi was perhaps thinking of the writing cabinet by Ashbee that stood prominently in the middle of the exhibition's central hall (4.16 and 4.19). The influence of this design, with its ball feet, can be clearly seen in a dressing table designed by Hoffmann or Moser and made by the Wiener Werkstätte a few years later (4.18).

![](images/1cf3409b3617c0b3591489485cb81fcdf1b773d1de9e7698ff13cd63b3eb1dcb.jpg)

In contrast with Ashbee's sturdy work, the work of the Scottish designers—Margaret and Fra Maconald harReackish,  .Herbert Macair—s ure-

![](images/e10d0c413e03734d5e6cd83d016dfee46f0e7cdfc04d26e25a4ba09ef3388277.jpg)

thetic, beyond good and evil, so far as Hevesi was concerned. He was entirely disconcerted by the group's artistic freedom, even though he understood that something completely innovative had arrived, something never before experienced in the scope of arts and crafts in Vienna. In his review, he nicely identified their extreme development of individual and sensuous artistic expression: "The form is not developed, but is manifest symbolically, as if still in a bud."2? Free from the theories of art criticism and from moral concepts, the Scottish designers were to support Hoffmann and Moser in their own visions and encourage them along their chosen path. From the artistic point of view, it was not Ashbee and Morris—who were perceived as backwardlooking and revivalist in their work—but Mackintosh, an outsider on the margin of the Arts and Crafts, who was to be the point of reference for Hoffmann, Moser, and their Wiener Werkstätte on the way to modern design.2 28

![](images/d0b387bf44284deb9f56291074501dc4433420cc0c3cf91a7e3087607b94e8ea.jpg)  
. Writing cabinet, c.1898-99.C.R.Ashbee, Guild of Handicraft, UK

![](images/df96b28333d8ae3e57a954ced1dddcea7a348cd14cea1f984ac2d5fdeec3c508.jpg)

THE WIENER WERKSTäTTE In 1903 Hoffmann, Moser, and the textile manufacturer and art patron Fritz Wärndorfer established a cooperative workshop in Vienna, the Wiener Werkstätte. Wärndorfer was to finance the project, and Hoffmann and Moser were to be artistic directors. They set up a metal workshop first, perhaps because they were concerned about the lack of contact between artists and metal craftsmen—there was no such workshop at the Kunstgewerbeschule (4.20). Later in 1903, they moved to larger workshops at Neustiftsgasse 32-34, where they also created departments for bookbinding, leatherwork, cabinetmaking, and lacquerwork, as well as an architectural office. Later still, departments for printing, fashion, and textiles were added. For glass and ceramics, they used their own designs or those of craft students, which were fabricated by outside manufacturers. Hoffmann and Moser must have been aware that their new role as businessmen gave them a chance to develop a convincing artistic enterprise, and they aimed to cover all the crafts necessary to create artistic unity in interior

decorating. They aimed, that is, to create a Gesamtkunstwerk, using their own workforce and workshops.

![](images/269a164c46124d5f621612cbf430b8a8c0a88c7a8fe00ae1b4a8d416282c4fc3.jpg)

The idea of the Gesamtkunstwerk originated in the belief that humanity could be redeemed through art; therefore, the separation of art from life must be eliminated. Morris had first taken up this challenge in his attempt to restore the unity of fine and applied arts. The artists of the Secession responded to the challenge in their exhibition design and organization. For example, their fourteenth exhibition in 1902 was conceived as a Gesamtkunstwerk to showcase a single work of art—Max Klinger's monumental sculpture of Beethoven.
