The Kunstgewerbeschule professor and Ver Sacrum editor Alfred Roller created the mural on the wall behind the sculpture (his poster for the exhibition [4.21] was a detail of this mural); Gustav Klimt painted the famous Beethoven frieze in another room (4.22); and Hoffmann was the principal designer of the exhibition, which unified not only all the visual arts, but also art with music.

The most famous examples of Gesamtkunstwerk associated with the Wiener Werkstätte were the schemes for two large buildings—one public, the other private. The Purkersdorf Sanatorium (1904—5) was designed by Hoffmann, with interiors by Moser and Hoffmann. It combined white walls, clean-cut functional furniture, a dominant motif of geometric forms, and a light, clinical austerity (4.23 and 4.24). The other example, designed in 1905 and completed six years later, was the sumptuous family home of the Belgian banker Adolphe Stoclet in Brussels. Hoffmann served as the project's architect, and a whole host of Werkstätte craftsmen and Viennese decorative artists were involved in making the furniture, ceramic decorations, gold- and silverwork, embroidery, and stained glass. Four monumental figures in beaten copper stood on the tower, and, most sumptuous and original of all, a great mosaic frieze by Gustav Klimt adorned the dining room (4.25). This was the Wiener Werkstätte orchestra in full flood, with Hoffmann wielding the baton. But the idea was not confined to showpieces like these. It could also be used on an intimate scale, as it was in the apartment at Wiesingerstrasse 3, where, in 1903, a Dr. Hölzl, who had recently married, had an apartment furnished in part by the Werkstatte.® A bookcase from the apartment

![](images/021b48ef6293ab440badbd21ca151b0f2d6428dc180eecd9009ca782e8d56026.jpg)

![](images/3fb41014362446d08af1dbcba5080af211d65be84b086e6120bfc2b9b6c0c1ef.jpg)

![](images/3bab6f8f1bf6453843f99b3eda2de06b27495d7701b58dc914dcdcdb4745f539.jpg)

![](images/b2edd8a7666b19d5990200b7eab692c20ea85b20a7549fbe9be5464906f8a623.jpg)

![](images/1d15f005bb73f44a7327d79fc08e49febfb8788f06a998ef0964b0dfad2d7381.jpg)

![](images/6a6191b23ceef225e68267735621803c1e06ca21c3f517191fc26b413786b1de.jpg)

![](images/6ffd7d036124e1bf8ba4be660b3df30c5443809e87ce6748a20d334c4e248007.jpg)

shares the motifs of squares and overlapping arches with other pieces in the scheme (4.27).

While the Palais Stoclet demonstrates the international standing of Viennese Arts and Crafts, local identity also had a place in Hoffmann and Moser's Werkstätte.3 Their regionalism drew on two different kinds of work. On the one hand, they were attracted by vernacular cdemrate y the ifluenc Slavifol dsin he ntricat patter  the textile designed by Carl Otto Czeschka, who worked for the Werkstätte from 1905 (4.26). On the other, they admired the Biedermeier style of their grandparents' furniture and everyday things. Biedermeier designs were considered simple and functional; they came from the preindustrial period, when design and execution still lay in one hand, and they were local in style. A 1904 article, "Biedermeier als Erzieher" (Biedermeier as Teacher), from one of the leading progressive Viennese magazines, Hohe Warte, explains the value of Biedermeier as a model for design, while an interior designed by Hoffmann displaying a Biedermeier table near the windows (4.28 and 4.29) provides another example. Hoffmann stained the table black, presumably so that it would fit in with other rooms in the house, which were furnished by the Wiener Werkstätte in Hoffmann's favored color combination of black and white.

When Hoffmann, Moser, and Wärndorfer were planning the Werkstätte, they turned to Charles Rennie Mackintosh for advice. Wärndorfer wrote Mackintosh in March 1903 to get his opinion on the workshop project. The central message in Mackintosh's enthusiastic reply was that each object produced in the Werkstätte should unconditionally convey an individual artistic expression, its execution should be immaculate, and it must be a vehicle for beauty. At the beginning their efforts should be restricted to work done on commission. Only after they had succeeded artistically and fnancially should they aim at industrial production, so as to be able ter funcional and beautifully designebjects in large nubers nd at the lowest possile prices.² This message would have been welcome to the three Viennese, who saw the Werkstätte as an attempt to combine artistic and business activities. As things turned out, however, the Werkstätte never achieved the second stage that Mackintosh proposed: industrial production and democratic consumption of good design eluded them. Their market was confined to a small circle of wealthy patrons who were connected with each other. For most people in Vienna, the products of the Werkstätte would have been unattainable. However, the Werkstatte's production of luxury goods did fulfill that part of their Work Program (published as a pamphlet in 1905) that called upon the bourgeoisie—the urban middle class—to realize its artistic mandate: "Our midle class is as yet very fr from havg fulflled is ultural ask. Its turn has come to d full

![](images/701d1baa26cb3af77b3ef2e0523d985b29cf18d149dedb9c306704092ab9dca2.jpg)

and wholesale justice to its own evolution."3

![](images/23c789717f422c2711bea8c8f6d71d5d33c03d25833362cb60dca43656466568.jpg)

They also knew, of course, of C. R. Ashbee's Guild of Handicraft, both from direct   
experience and from published reports. (Hoffmann's early designs for jewelry and sil  
verware often demonstrate this specific stylistic influence [4.30].) The Werkstätte was to some degree modeled on the Guild of Handicraft, and some of the social values of Ashbee's project were actually realized in Vienna. The 1905 Work Program made the human dignity of the worker and the workplace a basic condition for production,34 Contemporary newspaper commentary shows that this el waly u  e. Thr 25, 1905): "The 'Werkstätten' are today an organization employing more than 100 carefully chosen, frst-class workers (incidentally, nearly all of them socialists—in fact, ardent socialists for these 'entrepreneurs')." The Arbeiterzeitung reported (November 17, 1905) that Werkstätte employees not only got the day off for the opening of parliament but were also paid for it. At least in the early years, the craftsman's skill in execution was given the same respect as the desger's risictalnt. The Werksätte niiated the pc— revolutionary r handcafted productio nVin—sampihe craftsmen's work with their own monograms, which were designed n. W al marks—those of the firm, the designer, and the craftsman (4.31). In

![](images/cbca274dade2f7ecaf23195216dad6d5d5ffde5180999dad6b5d9ae1c602f7ce.jpg)  
n from the Werkstätte's Work Program, 1905. Wiener Werkstatte

![](images/e792893cbdb42b98874a9b4b2fdafc4601ee63cd03b202d02aa19412001d83f6.jpg)  
4.2 Armchair, Sitzmaschine, 1908. Josef Hoffmann, J. & J. Kohn

the Work Program of 1905, the craftsmen were given the same publicity as the artists. The Werkstätte, however, was not prepared to revert entirely to the medieval practice of unity in design and execution. The craftsmen did not make their own designs but simply executed the designs of the artists working for the enterprise.

The classic designs of the early years of the Werkstätte, when Hoffmann and Moser were doing most of the designing, were mostly in metalwork, leather, and furniture. Although the commentary on these designs often focuses on the square as a decorative motif (it was used so frequently that it became almost a signature of Viennese Arts and Crafts), the quality of early Werkstätte designs often depends on a delicate and creative interplay of squares and circles.

Consider Hoffmann's Sitzmaschine of 1908, made by the bentwood firm of Jakob & Josef Kohn, an austere assembly of squares and rectangles on a large scale and in detail, which is, so to speak, qualified by the massive curve at the back, the balls at the joints, and the general softening of corners (4.32). In Moser's 1904 plant stand of perforated and painted metal, the circle is a controlling element of middle size—in the curves of the central stem and the circles of the plant holders—whereas the square is a tiny dynamic unit of the design, working at its own miniature level as a network of tiny voids that mysteriously make up a structure (4.33). A similar interplay can be seen in Hoffmann's designs for jewelry, in which the fat, curling leaves are gracefully contained by a fine, rectangular frame (4.34 and 4.35). In the early work of the Werkstätte, the square was a wellspring of invention, even though it seems so closed and formal. It had the same role in the designs of Mackintosh and Macdonald, despite their sensuousness.

These designs belong to those years when Hoffmann and Moser were the firm's main designers and Wärndorfer's backing was secure. All these things changed. The austere and substantial forms of the early work gave way to a playful and decorative style of fanciful objects, spriggy ornament, and allover patterns. The designers changed: Hoffmann was there for the whole life of the Werkstätte, subtly adapting his style, but Moser left in 1907; the new, decorative manner came in with Carl Otto Czeschka, who joined in 1905, and with Dagobert Peche in 1915. Peche was the principal source of decorative fantasy. A critic wrote, "In Josef Hoffmann's case everything rests firmly on the ground, in Peche's all is shooting upwards."35 In 1916 the Werkstätte set up the Künstlerwerkstätte (Artists' Workshop), which provided studio space and equipment for decorative artists and gave the Wiener Werkstätte itself an option on their work. This greatly increased the number of the firm's designers, now mostly women who had studied at the Kunstgewerbeschule. Their work was more various than before the war, but no less fanciful. And the backers changed. In 1914 Wärndorfer left Vienna for the United States under awkward circumstances. His place was taken by the banker Otto Primavesi, but the new situation was never stable. There were attempts in the 1920s to involve the Werkstätte with industry, but the artistic individualism of its prewar years was too strong for that, and it passed through a series of managers and financial crises in the 1920s, sustained less and less by its reputation. The workers were finally dismissed in 1932.

![](images/6c45ae19c9c40b223449df0a5fac301b52dbc3390b8296b080505b995b1dc304.jpg)

![](images/f7bfa86e5151cba86fd8dfe40fbc05bcbff3c7b1089bbe8245524be9cea80e7b.jpg)

FORM OR CoNTENT? Given that the original purpose of the Wiener Werkstätte was to create modern, functional, and well-crafted objects for daily use, the question of how well it implemented its own credo must be addressed. Indisputably, the Werkstätte holds a position of international importance in the history of modern design. It is also well known that there were two important protagonists of the modern movement in Vienna at the turn of the century, and that they held opposing views. One was the Werkstätte's Hoffmann and the other was Adolf Loos.

Hoffmann stood for renewal through form: the world of design was to be made new, and modernity achieved, through the style of the Werkstätte's work. Loos, on the other hand, was an architect and cultural critic who concerned himself not with form but with content, not with the appearance of things but with the things themselves, with the way people live and use objects. He objected to the creation of new ornamentation for its own sake, and he was a critic of the culture as a whole." History has shown that Loos held the more profound and enduring view. Questions of content cannot, over time, be solved through the polishing of surfaces. The Werkstätte's superb handicraft production of objects designed by individual artists is incompatible with the needs of a modern society.

This is a lesson of history. In early-twentieth-century Vienna, however, the issue was not at all clear. What was clear was that the traditionally authoritarian, Catholic culture of the city made it very diffcult for any individual to express himself artistically, or even in the circumstances of daily life, and both Hoffmann and Loos experienced this. Vienna had the good fortune to bring forward two such gifted, controversial spirits at one time. Their different starting points offer two possibilities: to decree modernity through the artist's work, and especially through his control of the Gesamtkunstwerk; or to understand modernity as a social process in which the artist can play an enabling, but not a controlling, role. In this Viennese scenario, everyone must set his own priorities, must choose either form or content.

Translated from the German by Abigail Prohaska

## HUNGARY: SHAPING A NATIONAL CONSCIOUSNESS

arg   Wal  wor everni   ság Iparmúvészeti Múzeum (National Museum of Decorative Arts) in Budapest in October 1900, and in the weeks that followed, this eminent English designer was feted by Hungary's cul  
tural and political elite (5.1). At a dinner held in Crane's honor, the venerable novelist and patriot   
Mór Jokai pronounced him "the great master." The education minister spoke passionately of   
Crane's faith in the essential unity of all arts and crafts and their potential to transform socity.

There were tours of the capital's art schools, monuments, and museums, before Crane was escorted to the famous Zsolnay porcelain works in Pécs, and then to distant Transylvania, where he encountered the traditional dress, crafts, and architecture of that region. Crane's visit was a manifestation of the pro-British outlook of those most prominently involved in the Arts and Crafts in Hungary, an outlook that derived from a long-standing sympathy for British ideals and cultural values among the country's ruling classes.² Like his compatriot C. R. Ashbee, who visited Hungary for a commission in 1905, Crane could not fail to pick up on the strength of patriotic feeling that united the promoters, patrons, and practitioners of contemporary Hungarian arts and crafts.³ What also struck him forcibly was, on the one hand, the extreme modernity and prosperity of Budapest ("the most up-to-date city I have ever seen"), and on the other, the strength of the surviving folk traditions and the diversity of racial mix in the rural peasantry. He was made acutely aware of the social and economic disjunction between the country and the city, and the destructive conjunction of "feudalism and capitalistic industrialism" that made the inte gration of surviving rural crafts into a cosmopolitan context so problematic.4

Crane's experiences pinpointed the paradoxical combination of modernity and backwardness in Hungary, the politicization of the arts and crafts, and the strong official involvement in their promotion—all features that shaped the movement as a whole in this part of Europe. For three decades the social, political, and economic role of the crafts had been debated and a supporting infrastructure put in place, but Crane's very presence was symptomatic of an orientation toward British models that set the tone for new artistic approaches in the years that followed.

SHAPING A NATIONAL CONSCIOUSNESS THROUGH THE CRAFTS FOllOWing the AuSgleich (Compromise) of 1867, which accorded Hungary a relatively privileged position within the Hapsburg Empire as a partner in the "Dual Monarchy," the country had undergone a period of rapid industrialization.® Despite the economic advantages and degree of political autonomy this entailed, the partnership was doninated by Austria and overshadowed by memories of a protracted, at times bloody, independence movement. This situation drove the repeated calls for a national style in design. The Tulip movement, for example, expressed support for Hungarian industries through the wearing of badges and jewelry in the form of this vernacular motif. Led by prominent members of the upper classes and supported by the cultural authorities, the initiative for patriotic consumption was part of a wider economic and cultural program to resist Austrian domination that continued until World War I. In a global context, this entailed ambitious and sustained participation in international expositions, starting with the Exposition Universelle in Paris in 1900, when Hungary exhibited independently of Austria.

National archetypes were also changing. Increasingly, the Hungarian peasantry rather than the aristocracy was perceived by politicians, artists, and writers as the embodiment of the national spirit, and the related focus on vernacular crafts reflected this changing concept of "nation." In particular, the search for a cultural identity was tied to the ethnographic exploration and recording of folk arts in

![](images/12ac46d10e99a72493e5e00f6efa776486c71f43eca58adddc86b70cc5102436.jpg)

remote Transylvania, where traditions were believed to have survived in their purest form, relatively free of Turkish and Hapsburg "contamination." Traditional crafts provided a common ground, bolstering the concept of nation and offsetting the destabilizing effects of rapid and uneven socioeconomic change by referring to a natural, spiritual order. Sustaining those crafts that survived while simultaneously developing a modern idiom became central to the selfconscious invention of tradition and nationhood.

Above all, vernacular embroideries and dress became symbolic referents of national identity that all Hungarians—aristocrat or peasant, man or woman—could identify with on some level. More than any other craft, the textile arts in Hungary also had the potential to communicate the values of social interconnectedness and continuity central to Arts and Crafts ideology. In every rural district distinct stitches and patterns were elaborately embroidered on pillow and table covers, and national costumes were still worn in many regions. In particular, the szúr, a richly decorated, woolen overcoat worn by male peasants and shepherds, was widely discussed as an icon of the new peasant consciousness, and it was studied as a source of inspiration for modern design (5.2). Its simple structure and bold decoration were reworked in the elegant cot designed by Júlia Zsolnay (5.).The loose-fitting shape of this update reflected principles of international dress reform; more importantly, the coat also made a political statement as an update of the nineteenth-century díszmagyar (a kind of ceremonial dress worn by members of the gentry and aristocracy).

![](images/4bb83851ac0d815b348943b29ac818ea7c449761725bce241b5b8a71c9daf18a.jpg)

STATE PROMOTION OF THE CRAFTS, 1867-1897 The preservation and further development of Hungary's craft traditions were viewed as important state responsibilities, and in the decades following the Ausgleich an infrastructure was put in place that would shape a definable Arts and Crafts movement. Following the examples of London and Vienna, the National Museum of Decorative Arts was established in Budapest in 1872 "to gather the most precious remnants of the derativeart  the ps, and by sthem ateaching materil,  presrve andi ancient techniques in today's decorative arts..and further, through the education of mass taste, to create a larger public for these pieces of art." Under the auspices of this museum, the Magyar Iparmúvészeti Társulat (Hungarian Decorative Arts Association), its magazine Múvészi Ipar (Artistic Craft), and the Országos Magyar Királyi Iparmúvészeti Iskola (National School of Applied Arts) were established by 1885.

Outside Budapest the state encouraged the development of rural crafts, or home industries, as a means of countering deep-seated problems of agrarian decline and seasonal unemployment. Government ministries worked in tandem with local manufacturers and private organizations to arrest the falling away of skills and markets by providing training and equipment, assistance with the collection and modification of old patterns, price regulation, and improvements in retailing and distribution.

![](images/2e7cd33e6e7dd8a3db081c990a228e12bf429e9af7cbdf2adfbe7da8581584dc.jpg)

One of the earliest and most successful home-industry centers was run by Mrs. Gyarmathy (born Etelka Hory), who organized the production and sale of textiles embroidered by peasant women in the Kalotaszeg region of Transylvania (now part of Romania) (5.4). Already famous as a novelist and writer of short stories about rural life, she helped popularize the region, and by the 1890s she had agents in Berlin, Munich, Brussels, Paris, London, and New York. (It was through her London agent that Walter Crane was invited to Hungary in 190o.) The involvement of upper-class and aristocratic women in such enterprises conformed to a tradition of female philanthropic patronage that operated increasingly through an international network. As they became more successful, such organizations faced the perennial problem of steering between the commercial imperatives of the marketplace and the desire to preserve the integrity of the genuine folk culture on which the home industries drew.

Calls for a national style and fascination with vernacular culture had been accelerated by the prominent position given to crafts and peasant life in exhibitions that brought ethnographic research into the public domain. From the 1870s the Zsolnay company raised general awareness of regional patterns by improving the body and glaze of local ceramics marketed at national fairs." At the Budapest National Industrial Exhibition in 1885, the most popular pavilion was the Kalotaszeg House, reconstructed and furnished with traditional artifacts by Mrs. Gyarmathy and based on her research into rural traditions. Thereafter home-industry products from around the country were continually represented and marketed in the capital, initially through the new Museum of Commerce, and from 1895 to 1899 through a special bazaar, thus encouraging the rising taste for peasant-style crafts.² Paradoxically, as idealization of the country's folk culture became more prevalent among the urban bourgeoisie, conditions among the rural poor worsened. Bridging the growing socioeconomic divide between Hungary's urban and rural populations became a major issue that exponents of the Arts and Crafts movement sought to address both practically and symbolically.

The key event in the cultural landscape of Hungary at this time was the Millennial Celebration of 1896 commemorating the arrival of the Magyars in the Carpathian Basin one thousand years earlier. Not only indicating the political maturity and economic progress that Hungary had achieved as part of a major imperial power, the millennium celebrations (especially the exhibition that opened in May) also marked a critical moment in the crystallization of Hungarian nationhood. Due to its landlocked position at the crossroads between East and West, Hungary had long been a melting pot of different ethnic and religious groups. To meld and assimilate this heterogeneous collection of peoples into a recognizable nation entailed the "magyarization" of many minority groups, some of whom, like the Croats and the Slavs, were seeking political independence from Hungary, just as Hungary had done from Austria. Among the symbolic representations of a united autonomous realm with secure foundations was A Millenniumi Falu, a "village" organized by the Ethnographic Association. It brought together twenty-four model houses from different regions within Hungary, presenting a panorama of ethnicities, all harmonized within the framework of "Magyarorszag." In this symbolic process, geographically and culturally remote architecture and craft traditions were naturalized in the capital. Budapest

also laid claim to the heritage of Transylvania in a full-scale reconstruction of the Vajdahunyad Castle, associated with the Renaissance Court of King Matthias, which was to become a permanent feature in the urban landscape.'4

An equally prominent but more controversial addition to the cityscape was the new building designed by Ödön Lechner to house the collections of the Museum of Decorative Arts (5.5). An influential statement about modern crafts and the need to establish a new Hungarian style, it challenged the prevailing historicism of landmark projects like the Neo-Gothic parliament building. Just as the Hungarian language, distinct from all Slavic and Romance tongues, was a unique and immediate signifier of national identity, a comparable "design language" was seen as crucial to the shaping of a national consciousness.' Lechner's design gave monumental expression to the complex debate around the origins of Hungarian folk ornament. Károly Pulszky's 1878 study, for example, had related the basic geometric patterns of peasant textiles to comparable ornament in the art of ancient Assyria and Egypt, as well as that of Scandinavia and various East Indian cultures. In particular, Lechner drew on the ethnographic researches of the painter and drawing instructor József Huszka, who had prepared an evolutionary narrative tracing the ornamental patternsTransylvanian folk arts back toancient Persian, Indian, and Semiticcultures. The museum's covered entrance porch referenced Huszka's description of Persian gates, while

![](images/a71acdd1a33a53cfc2eecd46eec09f3389a242667503e562640c22fbefa6c388.jpg)

the interior recalled the durbah courts of Indian Mughal palaces, prompting one critic to remark, "Its seed was transported here by a western wind, was grafted with an Eastern scion, and is blooming in this land."17

Lechner also attempted to harness architectural crafts with modern building technology and rational principles of design. The cast-iron framework allowed light to flood into the museum's central hall and galleries, while the colorful pyrogranite ceramics produced by Zsolnay embodied technological as well as aesthetic experimentation, providing a durable and attractive means of combating urban grime. Brick was a cheap, traditional building material, one formed from the earth of the Hungarian plains, which expressed an organic relationship to the environment rather than hiding behind a skin of masonry or stucco. The specially commissioned craft elements—stenciled decoration, wrought-iron gates and railings (5.6), and stained glass by Miksa Róth—connected the interior to the exterior and amplified the institutional role of the museum in promoting contemporary design. As done for other major projects, such as the furnishing of the parliament building (completed 1902), the museum brought groups of designers, manufacturers, and craftsmen into a working alliance and provided a training ground for a generation of craftsmen.

On a more abstract level, the design of the museum established dress and textiles as the paradigm for a modern architectural style. The building was "dressed" with floral motifs derived from traditional embroidery and clothing, while the domes and colorfully patterned roofs evoked the structural role of textiles in the rug-covered tents of Hungary's distant Hunnish past. In this context, far from belittling the art of architecture, the association with textiles imbued Lechner's design with dignity and political resonance. The idea that ornament and forms could migrate from one craft medium to another had become well known in Central European intellectual circles through the theories of Gottfried Semper and Alois Riegl.

![](images/a5a9197b9a2a765447a1eb3c920f4d525f5a93b40c420930d2f01c4637bf5e82.jpg)

Although the building was admired enough to inspire immediate imitations, it also prompted intense debate. Some felt that this kind of "Hungarian" style was forced, incompatible with being "modern," and that mixing design with politics indicated a chauvinism that compromised the artistic integrity of architecture.'® Others reiterated the view that a national style would evolve naturally, but only once Hungarians had studied and internalized the lessons of more progressive and confident countries, such as Britain, France, and Germany. Despite criticism of both the building and the institution, the new museum gave the decorative arts in Budapest an aura of prestige and modernity that was beginning to rival that of the fine arts.

FROM HOME INDUSTRIES TO ARTISTIC CRAFT, 1898-1905 The new museum and the Decorative Arts Association now took the lead in demonstrating how folk arts and home industries could be transformed to suit the emergent view of "Hungary"; at the same time, they became "modern" through becoming "artistic," a mission reflected in the new title and graphic identity of the association's magazine, relaunched in 1897 as Magyar Iparmúvészet (Hungarian Decorative Arts) (5.7). This policy coincided with a shift in overall responsibility for the crafts from the Ministry of Commerce (which closed its Home Industries Department in 1899) to the Ministry of Education, where the art department was headed by Elek Koronghi Lippich. Together with the museum's director, Jenó Radisics, Lippich now galvanized the aesthetic development of the crafts through highlighting the role of the professional artist-craftsman (az iparmúvész) and adapting the intellectual framework of the international Arts and Crafts movement to the particular political and socioeconomic context of Hungary. In line with the British model of the South Kensington Museum and its associated school, the museum complex in Budapest accommodated the existing National School of Applied Arts, and its role, in part, was to foster the development of satellite collections, temporary exhibitions, and a network of apprenticeship and special craft schools throughout the country.19

An important function of the museum was to introduce recent developments in international design, creating a more dynamic cultural infrastructure that would encourage prominent designers to move freely between centers in Europe and America. Starting with an international show of modern art in 1898, Radisics organized an impressive series of exhibitions featuring

![](images/9bd7415e170d0a867d677fb20960d4864c9ed88093e59e3bda813ed6438db6d0.jpg)

![](images/51771ed48def5b9928c77b50e1ef19703913061a78769ff0eb6d719733cf22e5.jpg)

#

# BRITIPARMUVÉSZETI KIALTAS-1902

the work of major Hungarian and foreign artists, such as Louis Comfort Tiffany from America, Akseli Gallen-Kallela from Finland, and the leading names associated with French Art Nouveau. Radisics championed British Arts and Crafts in particular. An exhibition of South Kensington Prize winners in 1898 was followed in 1900 by the Walter Crane retrospective, and in 1902, by the largest exhibition of British Arts and Crafts seen on the continent, the majority of works coming from Glasgow (5.8). Radisics made astute purchases for the permanent collection from these exhibitions and from world's fairs of the period. Through its alignment with modern design in countries beyond Austro-Hungary, the museum underlined Hungary's capacity to forge international relations without the mediation of its powerful neighbor, Austria.

This increased exposure to outside influences was evident in experimental Hungarian work featured in Magyar Iparmúvészet and in the annual exhibitions organized by the Decorative Arts Association. The association's exhibition in 1898, for example, featured elements of a dining room setting that Count Andrássy had commissioned from the painter József Rippl-Rónai, including hand-painted ceramics produced in conjunction with Zsolnay, one

of the few "culturally prepared" manufacturers in Hungary with

whom Rippl-Rónai found he could work. The fluid "handp of his designs was later repeated, and similar plates were exhibited in Hungary and abroad (5.9).²' The critical response to the dining room installation was mixed.²2 Nevertheless, the setting offered a model of adventurous patronage for the blending of fine and decorative arts, and for the creation of a domestic interior as a work of art. While developing this commission Rippl-Rónai was juggling operations between several European countries, above all France, where he was a member of the Nabis and one of an international stable of artists, designers, and architects associated with the entrepreneur Siegfried Bing.23

Links like these were important for the development of Hungarian crafts in the buildup to the international exhibitions at Paris (1900) and Turin (1902). Miksa Róth's glass mosaic screen (shown first in Budapest, then in Paris) was inspired by Tiffany's opalescent glass exhibited in

![](images/10ea26d1bc704e876eff22b9b44b25abf92ebabc9021ffd8a1910da34faebab1.jpg)

Budapest in 1898 (5.11). For Róth, Tiffany's work represented a "break with soul-killing faco-type roductionn hefel urniture, the boxy form n derativestrap hin oak cupboards designed by Pál Horti combined an exploration of Hungarian folk ornament with British innovations (5.10), while the critic Károly Lyka was particularly struck by the simple and orginal designs o Ede Thoroczkai-Wigand, which were reminisent f the o-called plank style originating in Germany and Austria but had been "adapted for Hungary" (5.13). 6

Such designs were also disseminated through the Mintalapok, folios of pattern sheets issued regularly by the Decorative Arts Association and the Ministry of Commerce from 189. Intended as teaching tools in industrial workshops and specialist schools throughout the cuntry, these folios wererganizd by medium. The aim was to modernize and refine the look of Hungarian crafts to make them more economically competitive. Wigand's "rusticconstructive" designs were explicitly set alongside the work of influential Scottish designers Charles Rennie Mackintosh and John Ednie in two of the folios issued in 1902. Although the early issues were dominated by historicist and elaborately ornamented designs, simple forms and boldly stylized patterns were characteristic by 1903 or 1904, when the Arts and Crafts style of artists like Aladár Körösföi-Kriesch, Árpád Dékáni, and Mariska Undi appeared in force (5.12). This reflected the extent to which the influence of British models was becoming enshrined in official policy on the crafts. Even as some Hungarian Arts and Crafts leaders were looking to Britain for ideological leadership in the renewal of crafts, others perceived the actual products of the British movement to be increasingly exclusive and irrelevant. Writing in 1902 about British exhibits in Budapest, the Hungarian socialist József Diner-Dénes warned, "We are not rich enough,

![](images/3d6130b1acec9cf598205147e1773ea07193213762bdd35f029bd9e702e231db.jpg)

![](images/ba6a5e8d29f49f0118fcfd9224652561362bdd200266983e5c5cafd3064cb6d4.jpg)

![](images/c7adc5a2029b572e977595ca891e6696a4ca17db79602e716889e240b2ae10f4.jpg)

![](images/4bf5efe1b5c7a99e4396daa0d18b8b8d4f3198b6bafa625f1af2fa5f1f803a6b.jpg)

VIGAND EDE-EBEDLO BUTORZATA  
![](images/1e920daac51566e251817b0e216e45857f5e9191ab6cc4b3b7cbaece3706d68f.jpg)

we are not aesthetic enough to encourage this art," and he urged Hungarian designers to turn instead to Germany and America.28

Benchmarks for technical proficiency and artistry were also set t integrating the innovations of professional artists and designers into the industries. The Budapest designer Pál Horti, for example, was commissioned in 1901 to tour provincial towns, giving instruction to local potters "with the object of improving the form of their wares and imparting a more artistic touch to their glazing and decoration" (5.14). The results were exhibited to great acclaim in Budapest and at the international exposition in Turin (1902), where they struck British and German critics as a triumph of popular design policy.²9 Halas lacework produced by a female cooperative executing designs by Árpád Dékáni was also introduced at Turin in 1902.3³° Like Horti, Dékáni was a teacher and avid folk-art collector who urged students to study Hungarian vernacular crafts and transform their "spirit" into contemporary products. He recognized the potential for building a home-industry network around the international demand for fashionable lace. Soon his designs were earning major awards at the international exhibitions in St. Louis (1904), Milan (1906), and London (19o8), not least for their delicate, modern coloring and distinctive reworking of Hungarian motifs (5.15).

In 1897 and 1898 Radisics had purchased tapestries by the Norwegian Frida Hansen (see p. 182), and the German Otto Eckmann, thus providing a model for the development of home-industry weaving.3' Although Hungary had no tapestry tradition, weaver and textile artist Sarolta Kovalszky was encouraged to establish the Scandinavian Scherrebek weaving technique (which Eckmann in particular had popularized) at Nemetelemér in Torontál County, a provincial town on the southern edge of Hungary. The strategy was to take advantage of the region's traditional methods of producing kilims and update them. Between 1898 and 1903 Kovalszky organized the weaving of tapestry designs by artists such as Pál Horti, Sándor Nagy, Aladár Kriesch, and János Vaszary, which were showcased at the expositions in Paris (1900) and Turin (1902). Despite critical acclaim, however, it was clear that there was no market for such work in Torontál, and in 1904 Lippich closed the workshops, transferring Kovalszky, her students, and their looms to Gödöllo, just outside Budapest, where Kriesch and Nagy were establishing a small art colony. Here Kovalszky continued her association with these artists. Vaszary's potted peony design, initially commissioned by the Decorative Arts Association in 1898, was rewoven at Gödöllo for use on curtains and chairs selected by Radisics for display at the Venice Biennale of 1905. The design was brought into the register of sophisticated urban taste through the adoption of fashionable pastel colors and mahogany-veneered chair frames designed by the architect Lajos Jámbor (5.16). The wall hanging entitled Vásár (The Fair) was a rather literal translation into a craft medium of Vaszary's artistic preoccupations at that point with rural realism (5.17). The bold flattened figures and strong diagonal composition clearly referenced paintings of the Nabis and other Symbolists, but the medium—wool—was also part of the message, resonating with the vernacular subject matter more than oil paint could ever have done.

![](images/1b3f6108d5add070c0fc17e8526543cb1f7f0a8b926a41e5768b232700ea496c.jpg)

![](images/36fe8dd9b9eccd8ae77c8783a0b99c8cd336cebe3637fba8849969beea3c7df8.jpg)

The transference of the weaving workshops from Nemetelemér to Gödölló emphasized Lippich's policy toward the home industries as the basis for generating a metropolitan style of decorative arts rather than as a means of alleviating rural poverty in the remoter regions of Hungary. The proximity of Gödölló to Budapest kept Kriesch and Nagy close to the center of offcial policy making and to the art press; it also facilitated their collaboration with Budapest-based manufacturers and artists, such as Undi and Róth.

Lippich, Radisics, and other members of the Decorative Arts Association were also actively involved in the selection and design of Hungarian exhibitions abroad. Since the international exposition in Vienna (1873), the richness and diversity of Hungarian folk arts had attracted an international audience, though initially serving to reinforce rather than challenge the perception of "magyars" as a subject people within the Hapsburg Empire."² At the Paris Exposition Universelle in 1900, however, visitors could see how folk sources had been combined with sophisticated assimilation of progressive currents worldwide. At this and subsequent exhibitions prior to his untimely death in 1907, Pál Horti proved to be a particularly effective ambassador of his country's brand of modern design.3 His entrepreneurial experience in so many different areas of the decorative arts made him ideally suited to coordinate the Hungarian installation at the 1904 St. Louis world's fair, the Louisiana Purchase International Exhibition, for which he was awarded a gold medal and Diploma of Honor." In the Manufactures Building he created a modern version of a wooden Transylvanian country house and church with characteristic towers, fence, and Székely gate (5.18). The pavilion was a brilliant piece of stage-set propaganda, illustrating boh the survival of an ancient culture and its role as a source for modern art and craft. At the same time Horti was on an economic mission for the Hungarian government: he was to report on American decorative art manufacturing, especially in the furniture industry. The following year he was commissioned to design furniture and graphics for two American companies, Limbert  Grand Rapis nd Hollan Michigan, and the ho therafters nCincii, Ohio. Horti was attracted to these manufacturers as entrepreneurs purporting to operate businesses along Morrisian lines, particularly in the sense of promoting quality craftsmanship and improved working conditions for artisans, but combined with mechanized production methods and a modern approach to marketing and distribution. His desk and chair (5.19) were produced at the height of his professional career and international reputation. Designed by a Hungarian and manufactured in America, they were hybrid pieces that reflected progressive tendencies in both the United States and Hungary.

![](images/0e8fa84ba55b9591b01786c4d28ca56ad2f15997ab6fa529380b4752720d2fda.jpg)  
U Vhaa

![](images/3859245dd6015d77912470301b247b49f9852f36d69a2390b22449c7bf53626b.jpg)

While showcasing the immense expansion and achievement of contemporary Hungarian design, these international exhibitions also highlighted a growing tension between the two types of modern craft activity—házipar (home industries) and iparmúvészet (artistic craft). The more traditional home industries attracted considerable popular interest, and the products, particularly the embroideries, sold in huge quantities to an international audience. By contrast, the artist-designers garnered more critical attention and were given greater official support. This pattern was established by about 1900, and it has continued to shape the analysis of the movement in general. In Hungary, as elsewhere, there has been a tendency to discuss the Arts and Crafts movement in terms of a relatively small number of individual artists, architect-designers, and intellectuals who were given special attention in art magazines of the period. This type o craft activity, iparmúvészet, has been emphasized at the expense of the more diffuse, traditional, and nonprofessional dimensions of the movement represented by the házipar. Yet the idea of creating a modern national style, rooted in folk culture, that would embody moral and community values was nurtured by the home industries. In the context of domestic and international exhibitions, both types of craft were presented as part of a continuum. In reality, however, there was little love lost between figures like Mrs. Gyarmathy and Elek Lippich's protégés at Gödöllo, dehearathhe ir.

![](images/2011ad4001b412c505f1834f751f67c85755cbf205393fbd23e03ccede47629b.jpg)  
De nha0m  Horor he Shohe as

![](images/90d94ab3898d028a3db8232106d892d0f65430fc96968fac04889a4817188680.jpg)

ART INT0 LIFE, 1906-1914 Hungary's high-profile participation in exhibitions continued to attract international attention in the years leading up to the outbreak of World War I. At the same time, in the context of domestic policy, the Arts and Crafts became associated with ambitious programs of social reform and urban development, generating a more sustained and stylistically coherent engagement with the international Arts and Crafts. This final phase of the movement resonated most strongly with developments in Finland (a country linguistically close to Hungary), and with the work of English architect-designers C. R. Ashbee and M. H. Baillie Scott. Akseli Gallen-Kallela and Eliel Saarinen were protagonists of the Finnish movement who employed design as an agent of national identity. These Finnish leaders and their British counterparts, Ashbee and Baillie Scott, were all in direct contact with leading figures ofthe Hungarian Arts and Crafts during this period.3® Two centers of activity dominated—one at Gödölló, and the other around architect Károly Kós, the leading figure in a group known as the Fiatalok (Young Ones).37

The link between the two groups was their involvement in the recording and publication of folk arts, activities supported through Lippich. Many of the individuals were involved in the preparation of the monumental five-volume study A Magyar Nép Múvészete (The Art of the Hungarian People), published between 1907 and 1922. This experience brought a new understanding of the folk arts and how they might be used in contemporary architecture and design. With his design for the National Museum of Decorative Arts, Ödön Lechner had performed a major function by focusing attention on the issue of a "national language of form," but among his followers and admirers many were increasingly critical of the older architect's selective appropriation of vernacular motifs from published sources rather than from firsthand observation. The aim of this new generation was to transform the artistic power and spiritual intensity found in humble folk arts into "all walks of modern arts and life," In their ethnographic studies, scientifc rigor was infused with a mystical sensibility that aestheticized the folk arts, projecting them into mythological time and space. The sense of entering a world of living legend was graphically communicated in the illustrated publications of Wigand and Kós that depicted figures of

![](images/9e4dba4b8631df42efffcfb0127cbf81264fcc9cfb993fb572ba4b8b632ac326.jpg)

Hungary's distant past in humble peasant interiors; as, for example, in Wigand's drawing of the infancy of Attila's son Csaba (5.20). In Hungary in the late nineteenth century, there was a huge popular investment in the concept that the Hungarians were descended from hunters and horsemen from the East. This idea was fostered by ubiquitous imagery derived from mythic cycles that identified Attila and his family as the originators of the Hungarian people.39

Nostalgia for the unity and integrity of rural life inspired the establishment of the arts and crafts colony at Gödölló. Aladár Körosföi-Kriesch had settled there in 1901, and he was joined over time by others in search of a fulfilling, philosophically rich lifestyle. These like-minded artists and

their families included Leo Belmonte, who came to run the weaving workshops in 1905, Kriesch's sister Laura, and her husband, Sándor Nagy. Others like Ede Wigand, István Medgyaszay, and Mariska Undi spent periods of time at Gödölló without settling there permanently. In the spirit of Ruskin and Morris, and the heroic vision of peasant life celebrated by Leo Tolstoy, the group embraced social idealism as well as more conventional artistic aims.4° This was apparent in enlightened communal aspirations, and in the varied social, religious, and ethnic profile of the community. The Gödolló artists tried their hands at a variety of media, producing stained glass (5.21), frescoes, ceramics, furniture, bookbinding, wallpaper design, embroidery, and most importantly, carpets and tapestries. Through immersion in such processes, and through exploration of Hungarian legends and ballads, they hoped to recapture the inner spirit of Hungary's medieval culture and folk arts. The quality of their oeuvre was inconsistent but, in common with the work of many Arts and Crafts groups, it was produced in a spirit of millenarian optimism that transcended mere aesthetics.

The search for totality of expression and a belief in the socially transformative, spiritual power of art were rooted in the concepts of collaboration and the Gesamtkunstwerk, the total work of art. In particular, the artists' houses associated with the colony provided a model for the integration of home and work and, more generally, for the reform of domestic design and lifestyle. István Medgyaszay, the only Hungarian pupil of Otto Wagner, gravitated toward

![](images/207fa496b2e93435ff05af9cb66d76c8abf1ca237a803530fc867b81d0cc2b41.jpg)

Gödölló, where between 1904 and 1906 he designed modest houses for Leo Belmonte and for Sándor and Laura Nagy in a modern idiom informed by his study of peasant dwellings (5.22). These houses were brought to public attention at the international exhibition in Milan in 1906 through exhibits that re-created "the home of an artist." Medgyaszay's architectural framework was elaborated with furniture by Nagy and Wigand, embroideries by Laura Kriesch, and a variety of other domestic objects designed by members of the colony. Likewise the artist-designers Mariska Undi and Ödön Moiret, inspired by Wigand, shaped their immediate surroundings with plank-style furniture and the symbolic use of color and ornament derived from nature and the traditional crafts (5.23 and 5.24).41

Through their influential connections—above all, their friend Lippich—the group was heavily promoted at international exhibitions and in the art press, and they were given opportunities to work on large public projects, such as the interior decoration of the Academy of Music in 1907. The community's wider educational role as a training center within the home-industries network was confirmed in 1907, when Lippich established Gödölló as the weaving branch of the National School of Applied Arts. In economic terms, such support and government subsidies shielded the colony from the harsh realities of the marketplace. Only in this sheltered environment was the community able to pursue its quasi-mystical ideal of a life lived in harmony with nature and rooted in the traditions of the rural peasantry. Despite the radical tenor of their pronouncements on art and the unconventional aspects of their lifestyle (vegetarianism, reformed dress, sandal-wearing, nude bathing, and sleeping outdoors), the Gödölló artists were never genuine outsiders to middle-class urban society. At the end of a suburban train line, and with a Baroque royal palace, the town of Gödollo provided a picturesque destination for bourgeois day-

trippers from Budapest." Ultimately, despite their left-wing sympathies, the artists did little to address rural poverty in the remoter regions of Hungary or the working conditions of the urban proletariat.

While Wigand worked with Kriesch and Nagy, and was associated with their endeavors at Gödölló, he never became part of the community. In 1907 he turned his back on Budapest to settle in Marosvásárhely (now Târgu Mure, Romania), where he immersed himself in the study of local vernacular buildings. He was not formally trained as an architect and therefore could not execute buildings in Budapest; however, no such restrictions hampered him in Transylvania, where he designed an innovative range of multifunctional community buildings and modest houses.3 Critics stressed the functional, logically constructed design of his buildings and his sensitivity to topography, which was described as comparable to that of designers like Baillie Scott.44 The apex of his work in Transylvania, and a project on which he collaborated with Róth, Nagy, and Kriesch, was the interior decoration of the Palace of Culture at Marosvásárhely (1912). In a dazzling display of stained glass, murals, ceramic decoration, and tapestries, the interiors presented a mythologized view of Hungarian identity, depicting themes from national history and

![](images/90b728ea4c82eb8dc9a6d1a49cb26be115732610af2abd5cc63e6a3cf3bd435b.jpg)

![](images/acdd18f92736a5e3b843b84027eca7d39603fbbf9573b94f49914a8a3c779e66.jpg)  
5.3 Sketch, The Artist' Studio, 1913. Mariska Undi 5.24 Chair, c. 1907. Ödon Moiret

![](images/f8624c3c386e31da9b930c860e2b9bd39e96528ff4c43540b02155320ae7eaad.jpg)

myth blended with universalizing references to Symbolist and classical art (5.25).

National identity and the theatrical power of the Gesamtkunstwerk were most memorably demonstrated in the context of ephemeral exhibition design. At the international exhibitions in Milan (1906) and Venice (1909), artists from Gödölló collaborated with Géza Maróti, whose pavilion designs took the synthesis of architectural crafts and innovative spatial layout to a new level. The culmination of these events, and the exhibit that most powerfully expressed the combination of patriotism and art, as well as an integrated approach to the design of the pavilion and its contents, was the Turin International Exhibition in 1911 (5.26 and 5.27). King Attila's Tent-Palace, designed by Emil Töry and Móric Pogány, was singled out by many as the most striking display of the entire exhibition, "a poem on the banks of the Po." The orientalized character of the pavilion and its "feeling of mysteriousness" were believed to reflect the pensive national spirit of Hungary, while the structure and decoration of the three tentlike pyramidal forms at its center restated the cultural significance of textiles and made a mythological allusion to the three hills traversed by the nomad Huns. Brilliant Zsolnay ceramics were used outside and within to achieve a profound synthesis of form, ornament, and content. The vases and stools that were part of the ensemble in the Fountain Court, for example, embodied the architects' colorful and poetic conception in miniature (5.28 and 5.29). Inscribed with verses of Sándor Petófi, national poet and martyr of the 1848 uprising against Austria, the vessels became symbolic receptacles of collective national memory and poetic sentiment, rendering any literal comprehension of the verses unnecessary.

![](images/9488f9f48b127e72224487b30a847b1e6b9d1e448f5aa6c0e7b3bd7d71efa36f.jpg)

![](images/1cd602b2309402d318f7fa6e52aec8e6259f2e859581d9a7f8a4a86bbe40919b.jpg)

![](images/530f5ff2084db948ad124fc969966f26db9adeb6cfa08c87ce77d7ee5682fabd.jpg)  
5.8Sto, 1911.ml Tryn o Fy 5.9 Vas 1911. Emilryny,  Fcy

In Budapest the architectural scene was enlivened by the precocious group of architectdesigners known as the Fiatalok (Young Ones), who emerged from the Technical University in 1907 and 1908. At their center was Károly Kós, a native of Transylvania, who worked briefly in Maróti's studio and collaborated with Wigand in Marosvásárhely. The group took up many of the themes addressed at Gödöllo, not least the shared belief in the ideal of a modern national style based on the village architecture of Transylvania. The town of Körösfo (after which Kriesch had named himself Körösföi) assumed iconic significance for the Fiatalok. As the critic Elemér Czakó observed in 1908, the distinctive form of the Körösf6 church was "engraved upon their spirits," as Mount Fujiyama was for Japanese artists. That year Lajos Kozma featured the church in his cover design for A Ház (The House), the new journal that served as the principal mouthpiece for the group's theories and designs (5.30). The magazine's coherent graphic identity, which indicated a clear debt to Wigand, emphasized the Fiatalok's adherence to ideals in book design that looked back to the Private Press movement initiated by William Morris's Kelmscott Press. In fact, Kós was experimenting with just this sort of activity alongside his architectural work, printinga serisf hand-lluminated texts basedon a combination of his ethnographicsudies and Transylvanian folk tales. Kós eventually set up his own printing press and guild at Crow Castle in Transylvania, which consciously emulated British models. His study Erdély Kövei (The Stones of Transylvania) was a tribute to Ruskin's Stones of Venice.

Fellowship, respect for the environment, simple living, and the integration of art into all aspects of life—these traditional values were being tested on a small scale at the Gödölló colony, but they were now harnessed to a more substantial program of social and municipal projects, which was facilitated by the end of a depression in the building trades and a change in the political climate. One of the key figures in this story was István Bárczy, the mayor of Budapest, who aimed to confirm the capital as a world city run by an enlightened municipal authority, a centerpiece for the nation-state.48 In planning housing projects, schools, facilities for leisure or for the poor, the authorities looked to foreign models, but they adapted these models to suit Hungarian needs and commissioned some of the most talented artists and architects available.

![](images/d88ec7e9ce00268399301347e37d623fc5794a7b44a1a985f75f5f7812166143.jpg)

![](images/f38dbc54109c6cf36eb7cdf0d58e724880a45b1d78dadc445b210890e4f36060.jpg)

In 1907, for example, the city launched a comprehensive redevelopment of the zoo. The commission for the seventeen pavilions went to Kós and Dezsö Zrumeczky, only two years after the completion of their studies. In the heart of stony Budapest, the two young architects set about creating a remarkable "village" for the animals, employing roughly hewn and carved timber above heavy rubble foundations, all based on vernacular prototypes (5.31). Further demonstration that this Transylvanian vernacular style was applicable to one of the most modern cities in Europe came the following year, when Kós won the commission to design housing for

a new garden suburb for low-income families on the southeastern edge of Pest. The Wekerle Estate, developed between 1909 and 1926, would be the largest housing project built in the city during the first half of the twentieth century. In mixing city and country, the planners looked primarily to the British Garden City movement, but Kós based his actual designs for the main square on the German fortified towns of Transylvania, forming each of the main access points into covered walkways reminiscent of medieval gateways.

Bárczy's most ambitious and lasting project was a huge school-building program, part of his campaign to improve standards of literacy and general education. A major issue in Hungarian politics since the Ausgleich, a national education system was seen as an important element in "nation building." In the space of five years a remarkable series of fifty-five new schools was built throughout the city, the most outstanding of which was the Commercial School for Boys designed by Béla Lajta (5.33). Lajta had been collecting folk artifacts since 1900 and had direct experience of British architects Norman Shaw and M. H. Baillie Scott, whom he had met during an extended study and work tour of Britain and France. The façade of the school was austerely geometrical and functionalist, reflecting the commercial and industrial nature of the training provided within. In the detailing around the entrance, however, icons of modernity—such as a locomotive, a zeppelin, and an iron bridge—were combined with boldly stylized ornament derived from Lajta's study of folk arts, a decorative strategy that he carried through to the colorful ceramics, stenciling, stained glass, and other furnishings inside the building (5.32 and 5.34).

![](images/f557e3208b0a365d4b39903ecb944185abe9b05e8241cf23cb1e241fd0fefd92.jpg)  
5. Cabinet on a stand. from the Commercial School for Boys, Budapest, 1912. Béla Lajta 5.33 Commercial School for Boys, Budapest, 1912. Béla Lajta 5.34 Desk lamp, from the Commercial School for Boys, Budapest, 1912. Béla Lajta

![](images/6e2650bbaedf45611afa7886c6be72f36a47aea07777badf93225b145753fd96.jpg)

![](images/f0b55ec92e2f421c81ddca1b0296dd299e6542148fccef83100c1c9e3a16fd07.jpg)

At the same time, Lajta was commissioned by his brothers to design an office building with ground-floor shops in Budapest. The interior was designed by Lajos Kozma and executed by the Budapesti Mühely (Budapest Workshop), which Kozma had just established along the lines of the Werksttten in Vienna and Prague (5.35). Kozma, like othermembers of the Fiatalok and his mentor Lajta, had immersed himself in folk arts, but he increasingly blended his interest in vernacular sources with references to eighteenth-century traditions of English and Hungarian cabinetmaking (5.36). The distinctive "Folk Baroque" that resulted was well suited to the sophisticated and wealthy clientele he now began to cultivate

Compared to such high-end craft practices, relatively few attempts were made to engage with the industrial methods and character of the urban proletariat. Representations of work as a redemptive noble activity were invariably images of the solo woodworker, clad in an amalgam of peasant and medieval dress. In mural designs for a workers' club in Budapest, however, Maróti depicted groups of factory workers in modern clothing, universalized and dignified by allusions to Hungarian, classical, and Germanic myth (5.37). Largely funded by the family of Manfréd Weisz, a prominent industrial manufacturer, the project had been started in 1916, developed under the Bolshevik Republic of Councils in 1919 (after the war), and finally opened in 1920.

For many involved in the shaping of a Hungarian national consciousness through the arts and crafts, identity was not so much a matter of ethnicity or religion as a sense of being bound together by the abstract ideal of an enlightened, multiethnic state. The same style could be as applicable to a workers' club as to an art lover's house, to a Calvinist prayer school or Roman Catholic church as to a Jewish synagogue or tomb. The intellectual process of conceptualizing nationhood was nowhere clearer than in the way designers changed their names, deliberately identifying with an associative geography of the imagination. Around 1905 Béla Lajta, for example, had changed his surname from Leitesdorfer to reference the river that formed part of the border between Austria and Hungary. Similarly, Géza Rintel added Maróti to his surname, associating himself with Aranyos-Marót in northern Hungary (now Slovakia). Lajta and Maróti, like Kozma, Töry, Pogány, and many other design professionals involved with the Arts and Crafts in Hungary were all of Jewish extraction, exemplifying a significant facet of Budapest's intellectual and cultural life in the early twentieth century. For these individuals there was no conflict between their ethnic or religious identity and their intense patriotism.

By contrast, the craft activities of those who did not identify with the overarching ideal of Magyarorszag were officially relegated to the background. Many Slovaks in Upper Hungary, for example, aligned themselves with the Pan-Slavic movement rather than with Greater Hungary. Although reported outside Hungary, manifestations of the Slavic Arts and Crafts revival within Hungary, headed by Duan Jurkovic, were conspicuous by their absence from Budapest-centered publications, exhibitions, and collections. The simmering ethnic tensions that lay behind such omissions and exclusions were to erupt in the context of World War I.

1920 TREATY OF TRIANON—THE PARTING OF THE WAYS The outbreak of war in 1914 brought this period of heady activity in the Arts and Crafts to an abrupt halt, and in the process pitted Hungary against Britain and France, countries that had provided so much intellectual stimulus to the movement. The political momentum that had given the Hungarian Arts and Crafts such a clear trajectory was now fractured into different directions. In the aftermath of the conflict—the short-lived Bolshevik Republic of Councils in 1919 and the dismemberment of Austro-Hungary in the 1920 Treaty of Trianon—the territorial shape and cultural identity of Hungary were fun-

![](images/bd30a8cede2b74a7585ea408874640ff79c3898e59ba7a97f5f2654612bcc14c.jpg)

damentally altered. The moody coloring and symbolism of Kriesch's last great tapestry design-Eagles over the Tomb of the Hero, which incorporated a Transylvanian church and wooden grave markers redolent of nationhood and ancient spirituality— poignantly commented on both the human Ioss and the cultural schism that had taken place (5.39).5³3 For Kriesch, as for so many others, the folk arts of Transylvania had been central to the development of a national Hungarian style, yet under the terms of Trianon, this whole region was ceded to Romania. This and other losses (such as the ceding of the northwest regions to what became Czechoslovakia) triggered identity crises at the levels of the individual and the state. Several of the key figures involved in the Arts and Crafts died or sank

![](images/b54bfe2bcc6545e65e60951df9430d249f4ed88cdb4d48e49a87476d1ed21579.jpg)

into obscurity. Others became economic or political migrants; Maróti, for example, spent five years in the United States, where for a while he found refuge at the Cranbrook Academy of Art outside Detroit with his Finnish friend Eliel Saarinen.

Trianon highlighted the provisional nature of national boundaries and identities, but the concept of Greater Hungary remained an emotionally powerful idea that was difficult to dismantle. Arguments about culture and "national spirit" featured prominently in protests by the Hungarian government against the injustices of Trianon, and the Arts and Crafts ethos, which had become strongly entwined with Hungarian patriotism, continued to surface in propagandist

images like that of the sower on the cover of the political text There Is No Czech Culture in Upper Hungary (1921), but now in support of a more insular and divisive nationalism (5.38). In the increasingly anti-Semitic climate of the years leading up to World War II, the style that so many Jewish architects and designers had helped to forge in the early years of the twentieth century—in particular, its oriental streak—now became negatively associated with their Jewishness.

A more positive legacy of Hungary's engagement with the Arts and Crafts is evident in the untarnished idealism and breadth of vision in projects such as Maróti's text and model reconstructing the lost city of Atlantis in 1937.54 Similarly, in the relative obscurity of his native Transylvania, where he worked until his death in 1977, Kós continued to develop the forms that had proved so exciting before the war, unswerving in his commitment to the ideology of the British Arts and Crafts movement (5.40). In 1923 he had argued for the need to reassert the values of guild craftsmanship: "Someone may ask: why are you telling us all this? Don't you realize it is 1923, we are in Transylvania, and we have neither a Ruskin, nor a Morris, nor an Ashbee, and our people are not English? Nor do we have anonymous patrons who are able to dedicate large sums of money to communal purposes."55 Kós knew these things all too well, but for him the shining ideal of an all-embracing, socially committed, and spiritually driven art, an ideal that Walter Crane's visit to Hungary had highlighted in 1900, was still relevant and urgent. Art belonged to everyone; it belonged to life.

![](images/444327b0c656c30973fff7f39eef8b1fac2d860a8faf07079667b7ac76fc122f.jpg)

# 6 SCANDINAVIA: BEAUTY for ALL

![](images/d685018dac34f73a8013d0e974197fb38a67e3c8e75672441e0e3e224d537a89.jpg)

period when the influence of the British Arts and Crafts movement was greatest—the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries—as it is today. Swedish, Norwegian, and Danish, though different, are mutually understandable, and at this time educated Finns spoke and wrote Swedish despite nationalist efforts to promote Finnish, which belongs to an unrelated linguistic group. Consequently, these four Nordic countries also shared language as a common denominator.

Yet, separate nationalist movements existed throughout Scandinavia at the turn of the twentieth century. Norway was striving for political independence; for centuries it had been in the shadow of Denmark. As a result of the Napoleonic Wars, the country became the subordinate partner in a union with Sweden in 1814. Only in 1905, after decades of strenuous effort, did it succeed in achieving self-rule.

Finland had also been part of Sweden, but after Sweden's disastrous war with Russia (1808-9), Finland was annexed by its larger neighbor. Russia initially allowed Finland wideranging autonomy and its own constitution. The Pan-Slavic movement of the 1890s, however, motivated Czar Nicholas II to limit the Finns' freedoms. This, in turn, spurred the drive for Finnish independence, finaly declared following the Bolshevik success in the revolution of 1917.

The search for national identity that characterized all four countries was greatest and most self-conscious in Norway and Finland, since each was engaged in a struggle to free itself from the political control of another. While the ceding of Finland, and Norway's increasing restiveness, had also triggered a period of self-examination in Sweden, the country had always remained independent. Denmark, too, had suffered losses—first its dominance over Norway, and then its rule over two provinces after bloody wars with Germany in 1848 and 1864. As with Sweden, the need to establish an independent cultural identity was not quite as compelling, but it was still a critically important impetus for design reform.

The new nationalism was manifested in each country by a growing interest in its past. This was first seen in literature in the early decades of the nineteenth century: ancient legends and sagas were rediscovered, and, particularly in Finland, the native language was studied. Later in the century, this passion for time-honored traditions became evident in material culture— through the revival of folk crafts and vernacular architecture. The transformation of folk arts and crafts and other indigenous practices to express a modern national identity—now known as Romantic Nationalism or the National Romantic movement—was found in all four countries. The Scandinavians' deep awareness of and penchant for the British Arts and Crafts movement went hand in hand with this approach to design reform, and each of the four countries adopted the Arts and Crafts movement's moral aesthetics, adherence to regionalism, and glorification of the handmade.

However, other profound influences came first. By the 1870s, following the ideas of German architect and theorist Gottfried Semper, many manufacturers and designers in Sweden, Norway, and Finland were eager to improve the applied arts. They produced ornamentation rife with national symbolism (and marketed as such) toembellish industrial design. Yet, the followers of Semper were mainly promoters of the Neo-Renaissance. It was their approach—garnering inspiration from their own artistic past and emphasizing the value of the applied arts—that was progressive, rather than the styles that resulted from it.

Exposure to Japanese art was the most significant factor in the rejection of historicism. Japanese applied and graphic art had already generated great interest at the International Exhibition in London (1862), but Scandinavian artists had their most significant encounters with Japan during their stays in Paris in the 1880s. The Swedish painter Carl Larsson exclaimed in a letter, "My home country as an artist is Japan." Siegfried Bing's shop, LArt Nouveau, was known and admired by Scandinavians; also, L Ja Artistique, a periodical Bing published, rached

Norway and Sweden. In 1885 the Danish art historian Karl Madsen published the first book on Japanese art in a Scandinavian language. Contact with Japanese art encouraged direct inspiration from nature and bold asymmetrical compositions.

Knowledge of William Morris and the Arts and Crafts movement did not come to Scandinavia until the late 1880s and did not really make a significant impact until the 1890s. Industrialism had developed late and traditional farming society still continued, making a return to natural materials and craftsmanship less pressing as a statement against mechanization and urbanization. The movement reached Scandinavia mainly via Germany, since contacts with that country were much closer than those with England. As German was the best-known foreign language, one can assume that the new attitude toward the fine and applied arts was disseminated through German periodicals.

![](images/b71cd2663c9c71656965ddc89a919b9097513d0c69beacf62aad30b0ded06b87.jpg)

However, direct contacts were also important, and the teaching methods used in British schools of design and applied arts were well known. In 1886 the Norwegian architect, H. M. Schirmer, who lectured at the Statens Handverks- og Kunstindustriskole (National School for Handicrafts) in Christiania (now Oslo), made a long study tour of Britain and reported on the state of art teaching there. Through this and other accounts, Norway became familiar with the modern English attitude toward the applied arts by the beginning of the 1890s.2

In Sweden the journalist Gustaf Steffen wrote not only about John Ruskin and William Morris and the latter's workshops at Merton Abbey, but also about the Aesthetic movement and Liberty & Co.' Since 1887 he had been the special correspondent of the Göteborgs Handels- och Sjofartstidning (Göteborg Newspaper for Trade and Shipping), and he was interested in social as well as aesthetic questions. He had met Morris at his home, although the article describing this visit was not published until after the designer's death. A photograph taken about 1895 of Steffen and his wife on a Sunday outing with Morris's daughter May and her husband shows the connection to have been personal as well as professional."

An even more important link with England was forged through Erik Gustaf Folcker, an assistant at the Nationalmuseum in Stockholm and later the secretary of the Svenska Slöjdföreningen (Swedish Society of Industrial Design). A substantial inheritance enabled him to embark on the great adventure of his life, a series of travels to England, where he visited the foremost advocates of the Arts and Crafts movement. On his first journey, in 1891, he called on William Morris and Walter Crane at their homes. In Leek he met the textile printer Thomas Wardle. He also became acquainted with William De Morgan and the director of the wallpaper firm Jeffrey & Co. In 1892 he opened a small shop in Stockholm, Sub Rosa, and carried the very best English wallpapers, fabrics, ceramics, and artistic photographs (with the exception of products from Morris & Co., since Folcker did not obtain the right to sell these). Folcker was not a businessman and the shop closed three years later. Its large stock of fabric samples and wallpapers was taken to Folcker's home, and it remained there until 1985, when Folcker's son donated the material to the Nordiska Museet in Stockholm.5

In the 1890s some Scandinavian designers and architects received government subsidies to travel to England. They visited art schools and embroidery centers, and they studied with admiration the collections of the South Kensington Museum (now the Victoria and Albert). Their travel accounts describe their enthusiasm for Morris & Co. and the work being done at Merton Abbey. The Studio was universally read in artistic circles from its first issue in 1893. The magazine, in turn, covered Scandinavia to a certain extent, including an article on Finnish art that illustrated work by the artist Count Louis Sparre. Sparre and his wife visited The Studio's editor, Charles Holmes, at his home outside London, Red House, which had been built originally for William Morris. According to Sparre's wife, the visit to England inspired the establishment of the Iris Works, which produced exclusive furniture and applied arts in Porvoo, Finland (6.3), and which will be discussed in more detail later.6

Since the process of industrialization had only begun in Scandinavia around the midnineteenth century, traditions of craftsmanship really had not been disrupted. Furniture factories were now mechanized but most of the work was still done by hand. The output of the large porcelain fctories was largely machie produced, with products mad in mols and then decorated by transfer printing. Individual studios, however, were maintained formaking artistic wares, especially to prepare the tour de force objects intended for big international exhibitions, where receiving coveted medals and diplomas boosted company sales and national pride. International expositions—such as those at Paris (1889 and 1900) and Turin (1902), and the large Nordic exhibitions such as Copenhagen in 1888, Stockholm in 1897 and 1909, and Christiania in 1914—were important stimuli for exclusive production in all media, and many of the objects illustrated in this essay were purchased from such exhibitions for museum collections. Jenó Radisics, director of the Museum of Decorative Arts in Budapest, for example, purchased the tapestry Pentecostal Choir by the Norwegian textile artist Frida Hansen from the Stockholm exhibition of 1897 (6.4).

Industrially manufactured goods were imported (in large part from Germany) to the Scandinavian countries. These goods represented the cheapest alternative for the new working class, former country dwellers who now crowded into the cities. Even the old peasant culture began to be replaced in these new circumstances—store-bought goods were regarded as superior

![](images/ff0003947a23bd5bacff49b2fa7fa4b8380fb862315ce61c78a480e7ce2a24c3.jpg)

to those made at home. The bourgeoisie also purchased factory goods, albeit more expensive ones. They particularly embraced the German Neo-Renaissance, at least for dining rooms.

By the end of the century, many museums had been created to save national cultural heritage for posterity. Here Sweden took the lead. In Stockholm Artur Hazelius founded the Nordiska Museet in 1873 and the world's first open-air museum, Skansen, in 1891. Skansen simulated peasant life by housing its collections of artifacts in vernacular buildings, a strategy that was imitated throughout Europe and America. The Folkmuseet in Oslo opened in

![](images/f89b427d92682eed99704b0a87751c903a359bec233f20c44356bf65e4b384fb.jpg)

1894; the Dansk Folkemuseum outside Copenhagen in 1897; and the open-air museum Seurasaari in Helsinki in 1909.

Preservationists turned especially to the textile arts as a source of renewal. A wealth of folk weavings and embroidery still remained, and continued to be made, but the application of complicated techniques was on the decline, and chemically dyed yarns were coming into use. The preservationists researched and collected traditional techniques, then attempted to record what was locally distinctive. They also sought out elderly women weavers who could pass on their skills to others new to the craft. Associations were set up by upper-class Scandinavian women (as was also the case throughout Europe and the United States) to safeguard and continue this heritage. The weavings and embroidery produced were actually intended for a well-heeled bourgeoisie. This, too, was the pattern found in all other European countries and America—the market for these goods was not the peasants themselves, but a wealthy, urban clientele committed to both promoting crafts as an expression of national identity and providing the rural poor with an alternative to factory work in the cities.

The Föreningen Handarbetets Vänner (Friends of Textile Art Association), established in Sweden in 1874 with both artistic and patriotic aims, developed from about 1895 onward in a modern spirit by leading artists. In 1879 the Suomen Käsityön Ystävät (Friends of Finnish Handicraft) followed the model of the Swedish association; it too produced outstanding modern work based on old techniques. For example, the ryijy rug, traditionally used as a blanket, was transformed into modern applied art, as will be discussed in more detail later.
