The Den Norske Husflidsforening (Norwegian Association of Home Crafts) was established in 1891 by the amalgamation of a number of older associations, and it advanced above all wood carving and tapestry weaving rooted in old prototypes. And in 1899 the Föreningen for Svensk Hemslöjd (Swedish Handicraft Association) was founded in Stockholm by Lilli Zickerman, a woman of unusual organizational as well as artistic talent. The Dansk Husflidsselskab (Danish Association of Home Crafts) was founded in 1873, and in the following decades it promoted tapestry weaving, the revival of native lace, and embroidery with motifs considered native to Denmark. Like their British counterparts, all these organizations sought not only to preserve and copy the old but also to create incentive for the new. From associations such as these, Nordic textiles began to flourish around the turn of the century.

The successful transmission of Arts and Crafts goals meant higher status for the applied arts. Painters and architects started to design furniture, ceramics, and textiles in a way that had not been done earlier. Carl Larsson threw down the gauntlet by writing an enthusiastic letter after visiting the Exposition Universelle in Paris in 1889. He exhorted artists to "go out and preach to all the joyous and beauteous message of art: whittle stoups and stave vessels, carve doors and cupboards, storm the porcelain factories and drive away the Germans with their dreary Lutheran art!" The artists themselves, however, seldom executed their own products—they designed objects that were mostly intended to be made in tandem with ceramic workshops, textile studios, silversmiths, and cabinetmakers. The ideals of breaking down the distinctions between fine and applied arts and creating an environment where everyday objects would be infused with beauty were universally held, but the course of development veered off in a different direction in each of the four Nordic countries. The rest of this essay is devoted to illuminating these istinct paths f development by taking a brie look at eac country's leding designers and workshops.

DENMaRK In Denmark, Skonvirke was a concept introduced in 1907 to describe the renewal of activity in the decorative arts. The name of both an association and a periodical, Skonvirke was also used during the period to refer to all design, except architecture. Scholars have long debated whether it was the influence of the British Arts and Crafts movement that made the decorative arts in Denmark so rich and exciting, or if it was Japonism, combined with intensive contacts with modern French art, that provided the greatest influence. Paul Gauguin, whose

wife, Mette, was Danish, showed paintings and ceramics in Copenhagen, and Siegfried Bing brought his collection of Japanese decorative art to the Great Nordic Exhibition in Copenhagen in 1888. German art periodicals were widely read, and many other international contacts played a role as well.

Danish porcelain, especially innovations in painting under the glaze, was the medium most admired internationally. These wares, made by the Royal Copenhagen porcelain factory under the direction of Arnold Krog (6.5) and by the porcelain factory Bing & Grondahl under the leadership of Pietro Krohn, were a great success at world's fairs. Museum directors such as Justus Brinckmann bought many examples from these expositions for the Museum of Art and Industry in Hamburg. (The floor vase on page 186 was purchased at the Exposition Universelle in Paris in 1900 [6.6]). Brinckmann acknowledged the companies' most obvious stylistic debt: "their painters have learned a lot from the Japanese," but he also observed that "their nature has remained entirely Danish."8 Not only were Danish porcelains acquired by museums and collectors throughout Europe and the United States, many ceramic firms also copied Danish designs. The Rookwood Pottery's Iris line, for example, is very similar in both palette and imagery to Royal Copenhagen.

Silver was the other material that Danish applied arts became renowned for at the turn of the Iast century. No Nordic silversmith is better known internationally than Georg Jensen, who started his own shop in 1904. His early work, in particular, demonstrates the kind of self-conscious handwork (for example, the retention of hammer marks) and, especially in his jewelry, use of local materials (often native Danish stones) that is typical of the Arts and Crafts movement. This work is vastly different from the refined, expensive pieces made at that time in Paris by craftsmen such as René Lalique.

![](images/3a86b8c13f3f97b825c89a9a5b89294fe4e98b26f1b86f3b98a4e5fea6322f88.jpg)

![](images/0596c9c3a37db4f6908d6400a90ce23d2d71d22812a48b3e8605303e68257037.jpg)

![](images/400d9df4ab86b22a4e1056ecc06d635e1dd9870b4b40085804230ece3d82f787.jpg)

In 1905 Jensen designed his first piece of hollowware, a small teapot with a flowershaped finial on the lid and an ivory handle (6.7). His later work is often much more elaborately decorated, but the silver always has the special texture produced by small, dense hammer blows. Jensen achieved such success that the workshop soon had to be expanded, and in 1909 he opened his first shop abroad, in Berlin. The scale of production became so large that Jensen needed to hire many talented craftsmen, the most notable being Johan Rohde, who joined the firm in 1906. Rohde originally trained to be a painter, but the experience of designing objects for his own home convinced him to switch to the decorative arts and to follow Arts and Crafts principles.

Although the large porcelain factories and Jensen's silver company had the most international audience, the story of the Arts and Crafts movement in Denmark is best told through an examination of smaller-scale production. Trained as an architect, Thorvald Bindesboll provides the ultimate Scandinavian example of an artist who became a master designer in all media.'" His ceramics, embroidery designs, bookbindings, and silverwork, like his carving and furniture, all speak the same powerful, sensual language. Decoration is abstract and two-dimensional, and motifs often consist of rhythmic cloud formations or remarkable plant-life forms. Although his father was a Neoclassical architect, Bindesboll was most influenced by Japanese and Chinese ornamentation, which he transformed and made almost primal. There is no harking back to stylistic history here, no sense of national identity—everything is new and vigorous.

![](images/96e83bbf1310db559c06433fbc0049583257016a5265edd5b0906a6cdb884e2d.jpg)

![](images/79c59bcc44e93fd8805b55ee18eb46de9d9b2811c25251a84b5f409429d3c46f.jpg)

Bindesboll was one of the many artists at the turn of the century who chose to make his ceramics of earthenware, which was simpler to fabricate than stoneware or porcelain (6.8). A coarser material, earthenware seemed more expressive to artists trying to work closer to nature and with less-manipulated materials. Bindesboll sketched the shape and abstract decoration of his vessels and participated in their production by incising the decorative elements in the wet clay before painting and glazing. His ceramics, made between 1883 and 1908, consist mostly of big swelling vases and large plates. Production was executed in different workshops; the most important ones being Wallmann in Utterslev, Herman A. Kähler in Næstved, G. Eifrig in Valby, and P. Ipsens Enke in Copenhagen. Because his style for the different workshops remained consistent, it is often difficult to identify precisely where a particular piece was made.

Between 1873 and 1903 Bindesboll's sister, Johanne, ran an embroidery shop with another craftswoman, Kristiane Constantin Hansen. Bindesbøll designed a number of half cross-stitch patterns for the shop. His motifs resemble clouds and fungi, and they are rendered in subdued colors against a dark background; the occasional color produces a strong, luminous contrast. They are true originals, far from the motifs typically embroidered in cross-stitch from German patterns at this time (6.9).

Bindesboll also designed silver—first in collaboration with the well-known Danish silversmith A. Michelsen in Copenhagen, and from 1904 onward with Holger Kyster in Kolding. The cloud formations recur here, but now they are hammered in relief with a soft, rounded surface. Bindesboll's silverwork and ceramics, with their innovative ornamentation, created a sensation at the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 1900). His book designs and other graphic works have equally powerful, clear forms; his label for the well-known Danish lager Carlsberg Pilsner is still being used today, one hundred years after it was introduced.

Like Rohde, many other Scandinavian and European designers during this period were first trained as painters, but they quickly embraced the applied arts as well. Lorenz Frolich received a classicist training from the Royal Danish Academy, but as early as 1854 he was designing furniture for his own home inspired by medieval examples with painted decoration"The dnng room chairs made in 1895 as a wedding gift for his daughter Edma (6.10) seem closely related to ohRevival urnur  Bri rhit... Frolic iv i Ln from 1870 to 1871, it is possible that he saw some of the furniture made by William Morris's company: his may have trenthened his resolv  cntinuealong thesame path.

![](images/80461f0827b074d370271fa4a5d0413eed847f8cade03177307b60aef8890bd5.jpg)

About 1890 the painter Harald Slott-Moller designed silverware inspired by Japanese art, and around the turn of the century, he worked as a designer at the Aluminia earthenware factory, where he produced both form and decoration. A cradle that he and his wife, Agnes, made for their daughter in 1893 is late medieval in style, with elaborate carving, painting, and gilding full of symbolism (6.12). On one side, outlined against a starry sky, a Doric column appears with the words "A thing of beauty is a joy forever," a quote from the Romantic poet John Keats. This

![](images/d8c9d04bf01f38edd19237aac216455ad6d223df2cf8ed354693ed548b03b4f2.jpg)

is one of the few Danish examples of an unequivocal relationship with the British Arts and Crafts movement. Slott-Moller called the cradle "painted wooden sculpture" when it was exhibited at the Copenhagen exhibition in 1894 and at the Exposition Universelle in Paris in 1900.

While Denmark is the Scandinavian country with the fewest documented direct contacts with the British Arts and Crafts movement, it shared with all other European countries the British inclination to associate the preservation of traditional ways of making with national identity. And like the other Scandinavian countries (as well as those in the rest of Europe), this attempt to revitalize and update traditional designs began earlier in the nineteenth century, independent of the British movement. The Arts and Crafts movement at the end of the century, however, provided an ideological language to relate crafts—textiles, in particular—with national identity.

In the farmland west of Copenhagen a special type of whitework embroidery, known as Hedebo needlework, was made during the first half of the nineteenth century. '³ These embroideries were studied intensely and were later animated with nationalist content; a society for their study and revival was established, chaired by the architect of Copenhagen's new city hall, Martin Nyrop. Inspired by earlier peasant embroidery, a number of artists created modern whitework embroidery patterns on linen tabby (6.11). These embroideries acquired such national, symbolic significance that they were translated into stucco and decorated some of the door lintels of Nyrop's city hall.

![](images/588ef0dae54c4c7c95d94a7ca8fe940db6b22ff554cb84f552c41269ab5c45a4.jpg)  
1 Table mat, 1907 or after, whitework embroidery on undyed linen. Knud V. Engelhardt . Cradle, 1893, painted and gilt pearwood. Agnes and Harald Slott-Møller

NORway Archaeological excavations of Viking ships in Norway in 1867 and 1880 helped stimulate the Viking Revival, also called "dragon style." While the Danes and the Swedes had their own versions of this style, the Norwegians were particularly drawn to Viking legends, since they represented a time when Norway was the conqueror, not just the secondary partner in a forced union between countries.

Lars Kinsarvik's chair is an example of the Viking Revival style (6.14). Carved dragon heads top the back stiles, and an intricate polychromed interlace pattern covers the entire surface except the seat. The cabinetmaker Johan Borgersen was also known for furniture in this style, and he received praise for it at international expositions, such as the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 1900) (6.13).

Norway made its most distinctive contribution to Scandinavian Arts and Crafts through silverwork, particularly through a technique, pioneered by the firm Jacob Tostrup, that combined filigree and plique-à-jour enamel. The technique was greatly refined by Torolf Prytz, who became head of the company in 1882. Prytz had closely examined what was called "Hungarian wire enamel," as well as French and Russian experiments with this difficult process, and he developed a variant that became a hallmark of Norwegian work.' Many other silversmiths mastered this technique, and some, such as Marius Hammer, made a specialty of its application to vessels in the shape of Viking ships, with dragon heads for handles and exuberant interlace decoration (6.17). While these motifs were the most common, silversmiths applied the technique to objects with a less nationalistic agenda, such as a cup by Gustav Gaudernack, the leading designer for the firm David Andersen, the Tostrup company's biggest competitor (6.18). Although both artists also worked in the Viking Revival style, their best designs are more individualistic, with forms of

![](images/2a3c6bc286862bcbf9ac6f91f49861a12b9f5e245c5a48eeece5e2935ecdd59c.jpg)

![](images/6319e1e7daf9d119045d6af145c232b2313a4f1e6c42d5bc2dc446716fafbb57.jpg)

aue Oaon broq r Julen efn Selaurd om Buaren. O ber raler Arer arita Magmedrap:

Corter  Moty Sem d trd Nen alore fiy rol; Dartit fore fro Parbar.

ony Magus forde on Warn vfer of Drg, esa liger Imoer

Dr s Bwrie  o r.Da b Emnd Daron MengeBg b ang   y breathtaking delicacy abstracted from nature.

The dragon style, however, was the most conspicuous demonstration of the search for a unique Norwegian identity in design. Frederik Holm's tankard (made at the David Andersen company) exhibits the characteristic dragon heads and interlace that evoke Norse legends central to the development of national consciousness (6.16). The panels are chased with scenes from the death of King Olaf, who converted the country to Christianity. These scenes were adapted from illustrations in a new edition of Kongesagaer (Kings' Sagas, 1899), for which the artist Gerhard Munthe contributed initials and borders, as well as many of the renderings (6.15). Norwegian scholar Alf Boe writes that the work would have been unthinkable without

6.16 Tankard, 1899. Frederik Holm, David Andersen   
6.17Salt cellar, 18901900.Marius Hammer

![](images/8b119e8bb5b464440c05e14e14e3db8c37bea2670a7303780c2955f84d1632ac.jpg)

![](images/e1320fcabb3a0a7f513d83e09fcdd62952d7b23346fc92c6a11150cea9a34d68.jpg)

the inspiration of William Morris and the Kelmscott Press. 5

Munthe's work exemplifies how traditional native art could foster the regeneration of national identity. But these efforts were also dependent on other sources. While Morris and his press influenced the design of the Kings' Sagas, Japan was an even greater influence on artists such as Munthe. Art historian

![](images/2610e764adeb968c4b295e15a04fa4c6db7a586343c4c9f1a122388ef5b6d823.jpg)

Widar Halén has examined the way Munthe combined a Japanese approach with motifs drawn from his own country. In 1892 Munthe designed a service decorated with blue anemones for the Porsgrund Porcelain Manufactory: the use of a local flower shows clear nationalist tendencies, but the pattern itsel is inspired by Japanee art (6.1).

Munthe also made cartoons for tapestry weavings with themes inspired by Norwegian folk tales and folk songs. Their flat, geometric patterns and stylized figures made them particularly suited to the medum of tapestry, which itself was an important part of reviving national traditions. The Golden Hair is based on an old ballad, from which the inscription at the top is k"op ha  .

Many of these tapestries were executed by the school of art weaving at the Nordenfjeldske Kunstindustrimuseum (Trondheim Museum of Applied Arts), which had been established by Jens Thiis. As the director of the museum, This played the same seminal role in promoting national design as did Justus Brinckmann in Hamburg and Jenô Radisics in Budapest.' Here again, contacts between Scandinavia and Germany were as influential as anything absorbed from Britain. In 1896 Brinckmann, who greatly admired Norwegian weaving, asked Munthe to adapt one of his watercolor designs for use as a tapestry. (Brinckmann had played a leading role in the establishment of the weaving school at Scherrebek, and a Norwegian weaver, Katrina Dons, had been hired to teach there.)

Norwegian design was disseminated through vehicles like world's fairs, art journals, and hotels—all of which served as agents for the international promotion of the country's independent identity. Munthe was commissioned to furnish a dining room at Hollmenkollen Tourist Hotel, just outside Christiania. The so-called Fairy Tale Room was probably completed in 1896 (6.19). Contemporary descriptions are effusive; no surface was left undecorated. Large carved reliefs—with motifs from folk tales, songs, and legends, framed by abstract patterns in high relief or stenciling—covered the walls. This room was a truly unique creation, inspired not only by Nordic design, but even more by Aesthetic movement interiors, such as those by architect Edward Godwin and the painter James McNeill Whistler. Halén points out that Munthe would probably have known Whistler's famous Peacock Room. 18

![](images/77e06817b31d0518cafaa8ffeda6db093ea9b4c87c4a6fc8c49bea2f090ffbb3.jpg)

![](images/03fcb5c031b0cc2ac1acac93349b8af6be57b29f7f3e856409330a4e60837f4b.jpg)

Textile artist Frida Hansen had her roots in the home crafts and learned to weave by copying old tapestries with biblical motifs. But she went further, developing a special style through contact with French Symbolist painting (see 6.4)." Hansen invented a "transparent" weaving technique, in which sections of the warp are left exposed (6.21). Stylized flowers and foliage stand out against the bare warp, which creates a luminous effect. Hansen dyed all the yy among the finest produced anywhere in Europe during this period. Despite her triumphs at the international expositions in Paris (1900) and Turin (1902), she encountered strong resistance in her native country. Thiis did not collect her work because he did not consider her techniques or decoration to be sufficiently "Norwegian." With her symbolic images and international connections she did not, until recently, achieve the sort of esteem enjoyed at home by Munthe.

SwEDEN An essential quality of the Arts and Crafts movement is that it produced domestic art, intimately tied to home life. From a good home come good citizens who build a good society. This idea was central to Ellen Key, a Swedish author influenced by Ruskin and Morris. Her books were read throughout Scandinavia and reprinted many times in Germany. Some of her writings have also been translated into English by the American "Mamah" Borthwick Cheney, who left her husband in 1909 to live with Frank Lloyd Wright, and who learned Swedish expressly in order to translate Key.20

Key was involved in social and political causes from early in her career, and her interests also embraced art and morality. In her small volume Skönhet for allα (Beauty for All, published by the radical student association Verdandi in 1899, she develops her thoughts on the home. Key's main thesis is inspired by the philosophy of the Arts and Crafts movement: beauty must be an integral part of everyday life. Beginning in childhood, one should grow accustomed to beauty so that it becomes essential. Women should not waste their time sewing meaningless embroideries, but they should create an aesthetically pleasing home (they should also read good books and study art).

With lectures, essays, and exhibitions, Key and a number of young architects tried to improve the aesthetics, morals, and hygiene of the home. A home with a light, cheerful atmosphere, simple furnishings, flowers in the windows, good reproduction art, good books, and well-scrubbed inhabitants would bring about a renaissance. No longer would people need to escape the harshness of daily life via alcohol or other sordid means; they would instead become aware of the beauty in art and nature. And it was not only the workers who were to reform their homes. Key also makes an assault on the "Germanness" of bourgeois furnishings, with their heavy plush curtains and stands holding dust-gathering ornaments and arrangements of dried flowers and feathers.

![](images/0e7b8f5e3f0e865f90276ee6454ef303a902a4e8872086a3e9b8e0764ee4982d.jpg)

Key used the home of artists Carl and Karin Larsson in the province of Dalarna as a model: the Larssons had created beauty in their domesticenvironment, and without great expense. In 1888 the couple were given a simple red-painted peasant cottage that had belonged to members of Karin's family. Lilla (little) Hyttnäs in the village of Sundborn became the family's summer home, and after 1901 their permanent abode.

Despite being enlarged to accommodate the growing family, the home retained its original concept. The dining room, with its red-painted furniture and green paneling capped by painted mottes over the doors, is fairly dark. The drawing room, on the other hand, has a large window facing south and is very light. This room is considered the essence of Swedishness, with its tiled stove, scrubbed-white wooden floor, and early-nineteenth-century white-painted furniture. The Larssons used the furniture they had inherited and decorated the room in the beloved Gustavian style, the Swedish version of Louis Quatorze. In his bedroom, Larsson made a bold, individualistic statement: he placed the bed he designed at the very center of the floor (it is almost the only iu e; h..

The studio has an English-style lancet window decorated with medieval motifs and a plain brick fireplace (6.23). Karin's portrait adorns the top of a sliding door. Old and new were blended: the Larssons repainted the seventeenth- and eighteenth-century vernacular forms so that, like the other rooms in the house, the studio glowed with color. Larsson designed the settee, crowning it with a whimsical self-portrait. Karin played a central role in the entire décor; for example, she designed, wove, and embroidered textiles for the house. Her work was innovative and self-confident, even if her husband took credit for it. Their home was a total work of art, a Gesamtkunstwerk.

![](images/8b78202242b11f17209b4b8133192cad82d4ddda9896cd7447882465fb44cd7d.jpg)  
or o

Carl Larsson published a series of watercolors depicting Lilla Hyttnäs in Ett hem (A Home, 1899), which featured the illustrations in four-color printing. Here he writes that his home might serve as a guide for others. (And it did. Inspired by the Larssons, many people at the turn of the century painted their furniture white, red, or green.) The book was an immediate success and was followed by four others with very similar formats. Pictures of Lilla Hyttnäs were most widely disseminated via an inexpensive German edition, Das Haus in der Sonne (The House in the Sun), a compendium of texts and images from Larsson's previous books published in a large print run of forty thousand copies by Karl Langewische in 1909.2 The book had an enormous distribution, and now, with the forty-second edition, almost five hundred thousand copies have been produced. The German version was edited without consulting Larsson: the interior decorating ideas were played down, while family happiness, a child-friendly environment, and the simple country life, allseen from a patriarchal perspective, were emphasized. Color reproductions of the illustrations were included, both in Sweden and in Germany, as art supplements in magazines, and the images

could be ordered for homes and schools through bookstores all over Sweden. Nothing, except perhaps our present-day IKEA, has had a greater impact on Swedish interior design than these pictures.

There has been much speculation about where Carl Larsson and his wife acquired the ideas for creating their home. Both trained at the Konstakademien (Royal Academy of Fine Arts) in Stockholm, were widely traveled, and subscribed to German, French, and English art journals. Predictably, The Studio is found in their library from its very first issue. The basis of their design, however, was already established in 1893, before the influence of The Studio. It is an extremely personal blend of Arts and Crafts movement ideas on the home adapted to a Swedish environment; it draws on the country's rural past as well as the individual creativity of the two artists.

Key organized two small exhibitions at the Arbetareinstitutet (Workers' Institute) in Stockholm in collaboration with architects and artists. For the Gröna rummet (Green Room) display of 1899, the architect Carl Westman designed a suite of "workers' furniture" in green-glazed pine (6.24 and 6.25). The furniture appears to have sold fairly well—though, ironically, to radical intellectuals. It was too expensive for workers.

![](images/afeedbced67541075d9d0ba57d7afe89ac63525137d39d1429ad476e8b72140d.jpg)

![](images/f6d7835ef230547b5cb021ad4f5670059f0325f99273daa96ed17e19d1884216.jpg)

Westman was one of the best Swedish furniture designers of his day. For several years during the 1890s, he worked in architects' offices in the United States. A study tour of England, where he encountered the Arts and Crafts movement and the new domestic architecture, had a decisive influence on the private houses he built and the furniture he designed. He used Swedish woods—either stained or painted—for his distinctively constructed furniture, which was sometimes adorned with ornament that had been sensitively executed by his wife, Elin Westman. Many of his pieces were made by Bröderna Erikssons (Eriksson Brothers), the wellknown workshop in the province of Värmland.

Westman designed a private house with all its interior furnishings as the main prize for a large lottery sponsored by the Publicistföreningen (Press Association). The Pressens Villa (Villa of the Press) was completed in 1902 (6.26).³ The house was of red-painted wood with white

details, and thus conformed to Swedish tradition, but it also had an American-type open plan. On the upper story were the bedrooms and nursery. The nursery chair backs and furniture panels were openwork, with a playful decoration of trees, cats, and roosters. Westman also used this same chair for the nursery in his home (6.27). The inspiration for this particular decorative scheme clearly derives from English architect M. H. Baillie Scott; the general idea of providing artistically designed surroundings for children also comes from England.

The person in Sweden who took up Morris's message in words and deeds most palpably, although considerably later, was the cabinetmaker, designer, and educator Professor Carl Malmsten.²4 His work is characterized by the emphasis on the structure of the wood, clear joints, good craftsmanship, and simple design. He often incorporated motifs from Swedish vernacular furniture along with a pared-down classicism. His breakthrough as a furniture designer came in 1915, when a chair he designed won the competition for the furnishing of Stockholm's city hall (6.28), which was being built at the time. The masterpiece of architect Ragnar Östberg, the building was inaugurated in 1923. With its intense nationalism, contributions from the greatest Swedish craftsmen, and overarching design unity, Stockholm's city hall can be considered one of the last great statements of the Arts and Crafts movement. After the completion this commission,Malmsten establishe shools ocermics, textiles, and furniure making, which have contributed significantly to the survival of craftsmanship in Sweden.

![](images/f92acc6aa76495a102489bb8537bc9c09f9b7175118f61ee49974855a217d5d3.jpg)

As previously discussed, Scandinavian ceramics was the medium most recognized and disseminated internationally: the two leading Swedish firms exemplify this. In 1895 the Rörstrand factory in Stockholm employed painter Alf Wallander as artistic director, with the specific assignment of creating a modern design statement for the great Stockholm exhibition o . One of the pieces he made for the exhibition is quintessentially representative of his style: a boldly modeled urn, with starfish swimming and crawling as in Japanese drawings (6.30). Rörstrand hired a number of other skillful modelers, who soon followed this example with similar work and established the firm's reputation as a leader in porcelain production. These designs are more clearly associated with the international Art Nouveau movement, however, than with the Arts and Crafts movement.

Gustavsberg, the other great porcelain factory in Sweden, employed painter Gunnar Wennerberg during the same period. Wennerberg's artistic temperament was less dramatic

![](images/9052e1c4f2a77c10d864757ea6fcce771a28df449e09892da1fb4b9b0b2e68c7.jpg)

than Wallander's, and he used his knowledge of botany to create designs incorporating the native Swedish flora with a previously unknown freshness (6.31). Lily of the valley, cowslip, and lime leaves are characteristically nationalistic plant motifs used on services exhibited in Stockholm in 1897, and these were later reproduced in quantity. Wallander and Wennerberg were directly inspired by Siegfried Bing's periodical Le Japon Artistique.

Contemporary developments in France sparked the renewal of the Swedish glass industry. For the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 190o), Wennerberg designed a series of overlay-glass vessels, executed by the Kosta Glassworks, that were very much in keeping with the style of Emile Gallé and the Daum brothers (6.32). His sensitive way of rendering flowers was particularly suited to overlay glass, which was usually made in two layers around a core—a lower layer cut at different levels for the base, and an upper layer for the flowers. The skillful glass cutter A. E. Boman rose to the challenge of executing the color shades indicated by Wennerberg in his drawings.26

Wallander and Wennerberg were extremely versatile artists. Wallander produced tapestries and furniture of his design through his own firm. Wennerberg made the prototypes for embroidery patterns and tapestries for the Friends of Textile Art Association. Both belonged to the same artistic circle as Prince Eugen, the youngest son of Sweden's King Oscar II and an important artist. This silver bowl, with its molded rowanberry decoration, is part of a burgeoning desire for nationalist expression in the arts, which the prince also clearly communicated in his landscapes (6.33).

![](images/6b2243699a2458dc53c0991ee3a6bd2f322cec55a5b1d084721ca0f7ec9a84cd.jpg)

![](images/cb6d6a456ba977f7f710697aab49326c5a4761536f72bf4dbc6b545285575c49.jpg)  
a

While Prince Eugen was a gifted painter, he also played a central role as a promoter of progressive design, including textiles. As in Norway, modern textile art in Sweden developed from hemslöjd (handicrafts). A telling example is that of Märta Måås-Fjetterström, who, when dismissed as manager of the handicraft organization in Malmo (in southern Sweden) for being too modern, was hired by Lilli Zickerman and the Swedish Handicraft Association, which had a more up-to-date orientation. (Prince Eugen served as chairman of the association.) Maãs-Fjetterström designed rugs for the association that united the compositional principles of oriental rugs with southern Swedish ornamentation: they are architectonically developed with such motifs as stylized birds and stars in bold but harmonious colors (6.29). She also worked in the same free manner on tapestries and hangings. In 1919 she was able, with financial help, to open a weaving workshop of her own in Bästad, in the province of Skåne, where she now produced her work signed MMF.² She collaborated, too, with Carl Malmsten, whose furniture was often exhibited with her rugs. The weaving workshop still remains, and even with new artists in charge, Måås-Fjetterström's rugs continue to be produced.

![](images/6ad8b0d1b96f2ccd88f07b824084be813695f0245e16429284c9e77e8b57eed6.jpg)

![](images/25d45461f84af1963afbd6d03913ab6f853e768263de198495ea4d0d7cb9c48f.jpg)

FINLAND Seldom have the arts of painting, music, literature, and architecture interacted as intimately as they did in Finland during the 1890s. And since painters and architects also devoted themselves to furniture, textiles, and other interior furnishings, there were links between all aspects of artistic life. A collective opposition to Russian domination was the unifying factor. People sought out the primordial Finland not only in the spheres of ethnography, architecture, and folk music, but also in untouched nature. Finnish nationalism was intimately connected to the eastern province of Karelia, a remote wilderness, cherished as the heart of Finnishness. Oral traditions among the Karelians were the basis of the Finnish national epic, The Kalevala: The Land of Heroes, which was first published in 1835. Karelia and the Kalevalα inspired a generation of Finnish artists, including the poet Eino Leino, the composer Jean Sibelius, and the painter Akseli Gallen-Kallela.

Axel Gallén had changed his Swedish-sounding name to a Finnish one—Akseli Gallen-Kallela. He traveled on foot through Karelia with his Swedish colleague, Louis Sparre, and took inspiration and motifs for his pictures from the Kalevala and from Kanteletar, a collection of folk poems. One early example, Velisurmaaja (The Fratricide), underscores the power of these drawings, which are stylized and full of symbolism (6.35). Gallen-Kallela decorated the vaulting for the Finnish Pavilion at the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 1900) with Kalevala motifs; he later returned to the same theme for the vaulting of the Suomen Kansallismuseo (National Museum) in Helsinki.

Both Gallen-Kallela and the architect Lars Sonck built their own homes in a timber technique inspired by old buildings in Karelia. Sonck's greatest masterpiece in stone is the Tampere Cathedral, built between 1902 and 1907; another is the Helsinki Telephone Company Building, which features a façade of coarse-hewn granite in the romantic medieval style (6.34).

A tower with narrow window openings strengthens the impression of a fortress or other defensive building. The windows appear as if they might look into a dungeon or even a cave: a remarkable manifestation, indeed, for a building with a modern purpose.

Influenced by the Arts and Crafts movement, Sparre started Finland's first applied arts firm, the Iris Works, in 1897 in Porvoo, near Helsinki. His objective was "the development of Finnish taste to a higher and more artistic level."28 The furniture made there was mostly executed from his designs: it was characteristically simple, more or less undecorated, and imbued with a clear statement of structure (6.36). The firm's shop in Helsinki also sold fabrics from Liberty & Co.

![](images/fc63bce86cda5b58640566174242674f69e69ab9be752af504b907455f55e751.jpg)

![](images/1939d57aec395da3e1d65a1541c07b67a481a86df676cc1d725b2404dd56182a.jpg)

![](images/71446cbbe7f4d8db154fc3d61a6e50fbc6c4b7285580f426c018af35b7dad0a8.jpg)

Sparre's wife, Eva Mannerheim-Sparre, also an artist, helped with the company and made elegant bookbindings as well. During a journey to Belgium, Sparre met the ceramicist Alred William Finch (see 7.20), who became associated with the Iris Works in the autumn of 1897. Finch's earthenwares are simple vessels, glazed in powerful colors with austere sgraffito decoration (6.37). Neither Sparre nor Finch borrowed shapes much from the Finnish vernacular: they were modern and international, and the hallmarksf their work were good materials and craftsmanship. The Iris Works was short-lived, however—it closed in 1902.

Like those in other countries, Finland's commercial potteries that added an "art line" to their existing production were more financially successful. Arabia, the porcelain works, attempted renewal by using old peasant patterns in industrial production. The Fennia line of earthenware included bowls, vases, coffee services, jugs, and basins, and featured geometric decoration in clear colors; it formed an extremely popular expression of nationalism around the turn of the century (6.38).

Karelianism was a living concept for the three successful young architects Herman Gesellius, Armas Lindgren, and Eliel Saarinen at the Exposition Universelle (Paris, 1900), when

![](images/97dbb1d02f520f1d5ff446dd97bd3e8ec254d1f41a331fc78fb6feed54d65b02.jpg)

thr  won the rhitectural cpetition or  Finsh avilon . This ws e first international exhibition where Finnish art was displayed in its own pavilion, and the building became a central statement about the country's national identity. One room was furnished by the Iris Works and the Friends of Finnish Handicraft, for whom Gallen-Kallela designed chairs of birch upholstered with a woolen fabric embroidered with spruce twig motifs (6.40), as well as curtains of the same pattern. He also designed a ryijy rug, which was worked in a centuries-old technique, but whose display was a complete innovation in function: originally used as a warm cover on beds and in sleighs and boats, the rug was recast here as a wall-seat-floor covering.

After the success of the Finnish Pavilion at the Exposition Universelle, the   
architectural office of Gesellius, Lindgren, and Saarinen received so many substantial   
commissions that in 1901 they were able to buy a large piece of land in the forest west of Helsinki. There they created Hvitträsk, a house and studio. or more

accurately, a castle, of timber with granite foundations (6.41). Hvitträsk was a large complex of structures—one freestanding, the others connected with skylighted studios—built during the architects' most successful period of collaboration, between 1901 and 1903. (By 1905 Lindgren had already returned to Helsinki for his own practice; Gesellius left the partnership in 1907, although he continued to live at Hvitträsk until his death in 1916.)

Just as the Finnish Pavilion became a symbol of national identity, so, too, did Hvitträsk. Like the Larssons' Lilla Hyttnäs, it is a quintessential

![](images/6de0b55c2aa585652e746130594f6a78d7b1b291844d3d109bd264a7a0d8ba96.jpg)  
Fii vilioExon Univrsel Her GesesAs Linrenn Ele S l

![](images/aebe6e0bf26c70c45592f29988aada8f8303c33ae0506b3ac309eac51fabed7e.jpg)

![](images/7d085b0100ca8b712a5ca312311bc37c4a47747194060568c779d2b4ea6bdd19.jpg)

![](images/1c3d417c09e72daead774ca80501777aa2f7cd6bded78186a87eaa71c62ccae8.jpg)  
.1 Hvittäsk, Helsinki, 19013.Herman Gesellius, Armas Lindgren, and Eliel Saarinen ein

Gesatkunstwerk, in which the building, furnishings, and setting are fully integrated. Following the Arts and Crafts principle of harmony with nature, the elevation follows the contour of the landscape. Stone walls; a series of outdoor terraces, pavilions, and porches; and local building materials—granite, plaster, and logs, with pantiles and shingles for roofing—all serve to connect Hvitträsk to its site.

Saarinen played the leading role in the interior. The living room—filled with handmade furnishings of his design, and a ceiling and walls of round-log construction—is a model of the vernacular ideal (6.43). The ceiling lighting takes the form of a medieval church candleholder, comprising a large ring suspended from five cords. On the ground floor, there are handmade

![](images/84d89c1cabd7fbd916271620b661e374e4a252a820519c038b61527d36f0aa97.jpg)

furnishings (6.42), hand-woven textiles of unusual beauty, and huge fireplaces throughout—everything combining expressly to protect against the winter. The rooms in the upper-story living quarters, on the other hand, are light, with white-painted furniture, similar to the architects' work that the journal Moderne Bauformen had published during that period.30

In the Exposition Universelle's Finnish Pavilion, Gallen-Kallela had modernized the traditional ryijy rug through the use of strong abstract, geometric patterns; he also gave the rug a new purpose. The living room rug at Hvitträsk—a variant using different colors from the ryijy at the Finnish Pavilion—is also draped across wall, seat, and floor. (Ryijys designed by other artists became something of a specialty for the Friends of Finnish Handicraft [6.44]).

Saarinen did watercolors of the interior furnishings for many of the houses the partners designed. In these, one finds rich color, painted-glass windows, depth, different floor levels, and

![](images/dad580750da5d67c0caccca1c74f365198d7a6d1a54d3fc87de57b609b14a638.jpg)

vaulted rooms; there were intimate, light places for sitting near leaded-glass windows, and dark corners with great fireplaces and benches (6.46 and 6.47). No such watercolors depicting Hvitträsk, however, are to be found; probably because these kinds of drawings were intended only for client presentations.

The relationship between Finland and the Arts and Crafts movement becomes particularly clear in the case o architect-designed furniture. The wood is untreated, the structureemphasized, and the archaic ornamentation in relief carvings and wrought-iron mountings is meant as an echo of the Karelian forests. One example of this relationship is the dining room buffet designed by Valter Jung (6.45). By 1904, however, the Arts and Crafts had begun to seem outmoded. The interest in England had shifted to Germany and Austria, and peasant characteristics and great iron mountings were no longer considered progressive design.

The arts in Scandinavia reached remarkable heights at the turn of the century. Painting, architecture, the decorative arts, literature, and music flourished and were received with enthusiasm on the continent and, to a certain degree, in Britain as well. It was a time when the different branches of art nourished each other and friendships between artists were established across borders. In this era of openness, new artistic trends—including Romantic Nationalism and the Arts and Crafts movement—became important as each of the Nordic countries searched for ways to assert its unique cultural essence.

![](images/6029290cef1e6ccc19b6555dfb89bc65efd1213d627a5be5a6f367edd947e65d.jpg)

![](images/c96e54caf528a45192c5adc00affe7a68ec692af72e4c2f97afbef2802ed8949.jpg)

# \~ BELGiUM aNd FRANCE: ARTS, CRAFTS, and Decorative Arts

![](images/25352e848a99f9a723d17044b37dee39c225d7d8c46be50793689d48487df048.jpg)

![](images/0ce1e5830655a3dec829b6515a40787eb66ab92a3367b483463aef92ef988cbd.jpg)

Europe. Gustave Soulier, a prominent supporter of the decorative arts in France, commented, "We must recognize in any case that regarding the position and esteem accorded to decorative art endeavors, it is not our country that has set the tone..we have submitted to the influence of England." The extension of Arts and Crafts ideas into francophone Europe was profound and transformative, although many precepts of the British movement were not adopted transparently. British designers, schools, and shops were known, studied, and emulated, but the ideas of the movement were interpreted selectively in Belgium and France.2 While there were francophone studies of John Ruskin's criticism and of English painting and poetry as early as the 1860s, English decorative arts gained popular attention only around the mid-1890s.³ William Morris appealed not only to radical intellectuals, who published translations of his political writings, but also to conservatives, who saw in Morris and his circle the example of looking to national tradition as a source of renewal.

Broadly tracing the reception, social and historical contexts, and critical arguments around the Arts and Crafts movement in fin-de-siècle francophone Europe, I aim to show how designers and critics encountered British goods and ideas through publications, commercial shops, and exhibitions. While I discuss the ways that the Arts and Crafts movement affected visual culture in both Belgium and France, French-speaking Belgians more readily accepted the lessons of the Arts and Crafts; therefore, I have given greater weight to their example. In both countries, and throughout Europe, debates around the social effects of industrialization, the importance of national identity, and a desire for a cohesive vision of art and life were tied to the flourishing applied arts movement and the example of British Arts and Crafts design.

The British monthly magazine The Studio, which began in 1893, found a ready audience in France and Belgium, even before the integration of French-language supplements after 1898 (7.1). One of the most significant agents in the transmission of ideas, The Studio employed European correspondents and used the latest techniques of reproduction. The Belgian cultural weekly L'Art Moderne commented that "no other art review is comparable to it, neither in England, nor especially on the continent, and, in perusing it, one is convinced of the extent to which English decorative art has already arrived at the highest degree of perfection.. It is an influence, a movement that will be counted in the history of art."5 Modeled on The Studio, Art et Décoration and L'Art Décoratif, whose first issues appeared in 1897 and 1898 respectively, continued the promotion of English design and furthered the burgeoning idea of the decorative arts as a vital form of modern artistic activity. Through publications, commerce, and exhibitions, Arts and Crafts theory and design were interpreted to serve different cultural histories and national identities.

Arts and Crafts ideas from England were assimilated more thoroughly in Belgium than in France because of Belgium's widespread Anglophilia and its similar heritage of early industrialization. Established as a sovereign nation only in 1830, Belgium was already the most highly industrialized and densely populated on the continent. In Wallonia (the southern hal o Belgium), with its concentration of textile, steel, and glass-making factories, bourgeois leaders advocated a laissez-faire attitude toward the development of industry, but the more agricultural northern region of Flanders was also dotted with industrial enterprises. The first passenger train on the European continent ran from Brussels to Mechelen in 1834. Although industrial manufacture enriched the Belgian bourgeoisie throughout the nineteenth century, the working classes suffered from long hours, less-than-subsistence wages, and some of the most belated and ineffective labor laws in Europe.

While heavy industry and colonial enterprise (following King Léopold II's sovereignty over the vast African Congo in the mid-1880s) dominated the young country's economic outlook, the traditional industries based on agriculture and craft production were central in the formation of its national identity. Crafts such as spinning, lace making, and weaving, if hugely diminished in the face of industrialization, were still supported by government subsidies and shown in exhibitions as evidence of a national heritage of industriousness that predated modern borders. In 1880, with grand celebrations marking the fiftieth anniversary of the Kingdom of Belgium, national self-consciousness was at its height.

With the support of the bourgeoisie came a surge in avant-garde activity in turn-of-thecentury Belgium. The Brussels exhibition society Les XX (Les Vingt, or The Twenty, after the number of founding members in 1883) showed an international range of like-minded artists at their annual salon.Among the first in Europe to include the decorative arts as a medium of artistic expression on a par with painting and sculpture, the Belgian groups Pour I'Art (For Art), Les XX, and especially the latter's successor, La Libre Esthétique (The Free Aesthetic) exhibited numerous examples of British Arts and Crafts work before 1900. Books made at Morris's Kelmscott Press, C. R. Ashbee metalwork, James Powell & Sons glassware, Jeffrey & Co. wallpapers, T. J. Cobden-Sanderson bindings, prints from the Fitzroy Picture Society, and C. F. A. Voysey drawings were all shown in Brussels in the last years of the nineteenth century. One of the most influential English designers and proselytizers, Walter Crane, achieved considerable fame in turn-of-the-century Belgium. Georges Lemmen, a painter, graphic and decorative artist, and founding member of Les XX, praised Crane's decorative sensibilities in a two-part article in LArt Moderne. Jane Block has shown how Lemmen's admiration for English graphic design is also legible in his cover design for Les XX's 1892 exhibition catalogue (7.2). Adopting the symbolic imagery of the fruit-bearing tree that Selwyn Image had designed for the Century Guild's magazine The Hobby Horse (one of the first Arts and Crafts publications), Lemmen created a bold composition with saturated colors that also owes a debt to Crane's woodblock-printed children's books, of which he owned a significant number. The prominent red flag emblazoned with the name of the group hints at the radical politics and aesthetics that Crane and many Les XX members supported."

While the introduction of Arts and Crafts ideas found favor among the avant-garde in Belgium, in France, a country with an altogether different cultural and industrial heritage, ancien-régime notions of artistic integrity preoccupied modern designers, critics, and government officials. As Nancy Troy has observed, France's economic competition with England and Germany was a significant factor in the renewed attention it gave to the applied arts at the turn of the century.The waning exports of French decorative arts industries during the second half of the century stimulated close examination of British design schools, factories, and major figures, but France, which experienced large-scale industrialization only in the 1860s, still cherished its image of prerevolutionary craftsmanship. Debora Silverman has shown how an increasingly conservative Third Republic employed associations with eighteenth-century artistic and decorative traditions in an effort to reconcile the political and social tensions of the late nineteenth century. Admiration of aristocratic taste was bound up with the image of France as the worldwide purveyor of elegance and craftsmanship. The luxury industries of the ancien régime, such as fine furniture making, porcelain, and silk weaving, continued to carry connotations of refinement.

![](images/30f8044b39b968c55fbf03e8c280893656b8b926f5781f4a2dcad1e74f457367.jpg)

The criticand poet Jean Lahor (the pseudonym f French doctor Henri Cazalis), in an article ened ". William Morris t art décorati en Angleterre"William Morris and Decorative Ar in England), recognized that the expansive success of the English decorative arts movement was due, in large part, to the veneration of national heritage." For Lahor and many other turn-of-thecentury critics, indigenous architecture and decoration seemed to reveal, unconsciously and without pretense, the true character  national style. He argued that the French Revolution had severed the rightful heritage of popular culture from the French people. Folk songs, dances, pottery, and architecture of the eighteenth century were, for him, the "primitive" soul of the nation. Since this "authentic" art had been cut off from the people, a new version had to be developed. Unlike Morris, Crane, and Ashbee, who believed the workingman not only had the potential to create art but also would be uplifted by such endeavors, Lahor charged the bourgeoisie with rt

Although French and Belgian critics advocated their own national traditions, they shared an international enthusiasm for the English dwelling and its furnishings. Revered for its common sense and for its picturesque qualities, the English house was praised throughout the nineteenth century by continental architects, such as the influential French proponent of the Neo-Gothic Eugène-Emmanuel Viollet-le-Duc, who was also an architectural theorist and restorer. Viollet-le-Duc emphasized principles of rationality in structure and decoration, which he believed were embodied by medieval architecture. He wrote prolifically on domestic architecture of all periods, and he especially admired the forthright qualities of vernacular buildings in northern Europe, which he saw as a model for contemporary culture. He commented, "The English have maintained, in the disposition of the country houses that they erect today, these traditions of the Middle Ages without regret and apply without difficulty these true principles to modern life. We happily recognize that the English are our masters in comfort (they discovered the word)." While the English house was venerated for its practicality, many francophone writers also stressed its fresh, cohesive, and artful decoration.

Gabriel Mourey, the French correspondent for The Studio, was a key figure in the popularization of the Arts and Crafts movement in France. In his 1895 book Passé le détroit (Across the Channel), Mourey discussed the centrality of the dwelling in English culture and praised the Arts and Crafts artisans for their fresh designs and devotion to the idea of "home," a word that was often left untranslated in French texts. Mourey suggested that the English proclivity for practicality, intimacy, and honesty was evident in their preference for the well-designed details of the domestic interior—furniture, wallpapers, lighting fixtures, and textiles—all of which indicated that "the special love, the profound Anglo-Saxon passion for home [is] without a doubt a strong aid in the efflorescence of this renaissance of industrial art." In Belgium Olivier-Georges Destrée, who visited England to study poetry, painting, and the decorative arts, also emphasized the English idea of home and the design of the interior. In his 1894 book about the Pre-Raphaelite painters and poets, Destrée observed that the "decorative unity" of the English house distinguished it from the "horrible medley of all styles and all eras," and from the poor quality that marked continental dwellings. 18

The idealization of the British dwelling focused principally on the aesthetic coordination of the interior. The availability of artistically designed textiles, furniture, and silver through firms such as Morris & Co. and Liberty & Co. perhaps suggested to Europeans that interest in well-designed decorative art was more widespread in British culture than it actually was. By the mid-1890s several continental entrepreneurs had also established businesses that both handled English goods and commissioned European designers to produce items for the dwelling. The Belgian architect Gustave Serrurier-Bovy was one of the first continental dealers to carry Liberty products, and he had been in the business of selling English objects at his shop in Liège since 1884. (By the early 1890s he was producing his own designs as well.) Ten years later Edmond Picard, a lawyer and art critic, turned his townhouse in Brussels into La Maison d'Art, a commercial venture dealing in decorative objects.'" By at least 1895 the architect and designer Georges Hobé was selling Essex & Co. wallpapers and James Powell & Sons glass as well as his own furniture at his shop in Brussels. Hobé's admiration for English design also informed his own projects. One of the two interiors he showed at the International Exhibition of Modern Decorative Art in Turin in 1902, rendered by Antoine Pompe, a young assistant in Hobé's office, garnered praise for its simplicity (7.3).21

![](images/059d8b8feb46f9038ea72e810ba9f8593f39b85687d0d3c86eb0cbef51e800ca.jpg)  
Wao tt 0Geor Ho

In Paris Siegfried Bing's L'Art Nouveau, perhaps the most influential of these gallery-shops, was an extension of his earlier business selling Japanese art. Transforming his premises, Bing commissioned an international selection of artists to design fully conceived ensembles. L'Art Nouveau opened in late December 1895 to hostile, xenophobic reviews, yet Bing became one of the most important dealers in English, American, and continental decorative art. In the first years, Bing exhibited and sold Morris wallpapers and textiles, W. A. S. Benson metalwork and furniture, Frank Brangwyn's rugs, as well as Arthur Silver's textile designs for Liberty & Co. He also sold glass by Louis Comfort Tiffany and ceramics by the Grueby and Rookwood potteries. In 1899 Bing's friend Julius Meier-Graefe, the hugely influential German art critic and publisher of Dekoativ us ndArt Déaalsoena ars shop that secialize i ital decorativ r, LMaisonModerne. The comercial ert, together wit illustratedaga zines and exhibitions, aimed to transform continental bourgeois taste.

Reformers also hoped to stimulate a new visual culture suitable to the modern era. Designers refined the long-standing argument that a country's culture was legible in its national style and offered new ways to reconcile tradition with modern life. Among the late-nineteenthcentury Belgian bourgeoisie, romantic longing for a national style was embodied in the Flemish

Renaissance—its revival paid homage to another era of bourgeois prosperity and artistic

![](images/33560b37a49e35c93c36ce0cda150f523e8a92bfb058740b76219fca90958f96.jpg)

achievement. The architect Paul Hankar, who spent a long apprenventions of historicism in his architectural and decorative works. Hankar often designed both the architecture and the interiors for his projects. The Brussels townhouse he built for himself between 1892 and 1893 included an ensemble of woodwork, furniture, and other   
details. A lantern from this interior shows how Hankar interpreted reviv  
alism in wrought iron (7.4). Following Viollet-le-Duc, who advocated the   
use of iron both in structure and in decoration, Hankar produced designs   
for iron from the 1880s onward. As he did with the iron details on the   
façade of his dwelling and in many other projects, he had the metal on   
the lantern manipulated to resemble plantlike tendrils extending from   
each corner. He paired these natural forms, however, with four flat  
tened shapes resembling Flemish Renaissance Revival strap work.23 In the tradition of the master builder, a romantic figure who was both   
OE architect and craftsman (an ideal that Viollet-le-Duc himself had emulated), Hankar transcended the professional boundaries   
9 between architect and decorative artist.

In 1897 Adolphe Crespin, Hankar's close friend and the designer of many sgraffito panels for his projects, designed a poster advertising the architect's talents (7.5). Shown in profile, casually leaning against his desk, Hankar is surrounded by signs of his trade—plumb line, ruler, and T squares—together with the bees and honeycomb of industriousness. A strong outline and unmodulated expanses of color define the figure of Hankar, who is juxtaposed with decorative motifs similar to those that embellished the façade and interior of his house. Like Crane, who combined Japanese, Greek, and Renaissance details in his wallpapers (which Hankar may have aniui ok ireo heclu wit eec Japanese mon pattern, a key motif on the lamp, and the turned legs of the stool.4

Hankar's commitment to a modern expression in architecture and design included an interest inBritih domesticarchitecture ust as he tranorme revivalstmoti in his deative designs and urban architecture, Hankar, like Viollet-le-Duc, saw in the model of the British country house bo a venerable traditionand amodern type. Hankar' "otage"or Phil Wolfers, like many f his commissions, was designed for an artist (Wolfers was a renowned fin-de-siècle jeweler and sculptor) who was also a close friend (7.7). Located in the countryside outside Brussels, the Cottage Wolfers was a grand brick structure with a covered porch, prominent half-timbered gables, and intricate wooden balconies extending from the central block. In this design, Hankar employs allusions to vernacular architecture as a means of reformulating the nature of the modern artistic dwelling. The chair and footstool from this project show how Hankar also incorporated this approach in decorating the interior (7.6). The chair's oak frame with its prominent pegs, carved legs, and seat and back of woven leather straps evokes a vaguely historical tradition and, simultaneously, the stylized rusticity of modern country life.

The idea of a simple life in the country was a popular conceit among the bourgeoisie throughout Europe, and one that urban artists found particularly restorative.2 In the 1890s Gustave Serrurier-Bovy, the Belgian architect, designer, and entrepreneur, showed ensembles of furniture that offered a palliative to the stress of urban life. His Study (1894) and Artisan's Room (1895; 7.8) were exhibited in Brussels at La Libre Esthétique along with unprecedented selections of English decorative art. Serrurier-Bovy probably saw the Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society display in London in 1893, for he wrote to Octave Maus (secretary of Les XX and then La Libre Esthétique) recommending C. F. A. Voysey, Selwyn Image, Heywood Sumner, Lewis F. Day, R. Anning Bell, and the glass-making firm James Powell & Sons, who "exhibited in a remarkable fashion at the 'Arts and Crafts." Although Serrurier-Bovy later denied any influence from English art, contemporary critics perceived his rooms, which were elaborately installed to reemble domestic interiors,as evoking "home" and the simple life. He suggested in a pam phlet produced for the Artisan's Room that he wanted to show that "art is not only in the service of richness. Quite to the contrary, taste has more to fear from luxury than from modesty and the exigencies of simplicity." While the rush seats and turned legs of Serrurier-Bovy's Artisan chairs evoke a cozy rural vernacular, these elements are paired with a spare, flattened, wing-shaped back that gives the chairs the appearance of modernity (7.9).

![](images/74eee5a9e22d1017e5c8f0f1c4a5b12bb7a06cebaa8486fcb58bca1a0f58a6ec.jpg)

![](images/f68f0ce39b0b4670362573e807b5a5cbfda7394e8c75f83486728fd826c4de1c.jpg)

![](images/555f4b04e36b41376a59eb1ba778d67a3abe433fd828ad220ee375c6ae12ba61.jpg)

![](images/4ef7176ba7c1554c05230535be095f273e18a2a31701f5ac91a5cfc6d7e97a69.jpg)

![](images/7930ce6291ea01776bf972f74c825e6049c6f2c9fa3de74da39d312ef499106d.jpg)

Serrurier-Bovy's stores in Liège and Brussels, as well as L'Art dans I'Habitation (Art in the Dwelling) in Paris, were known for lavish ensembles that were displayed as complete rooms. Always concerned with every detail of the space, Serrurier-Bovy also designed smaller items, including vases, picture frames, and other furnishings, such as a mahogany tray lined with an embroidered linen scarf (7.10). Many of the objects that he produced and sold in the late 1890s were made in African hardwoods (which were plentiful in Belgium because of King Léopold II's personal control over the Congo) and had sumptuous curving forms, yet details such as fine enameled mounts and hammered hinges indicate an ongoing dialogue with British design (7.11). Around 1903 Serrurier-Bovy received a commission for a small château in the Cantal (Auvergne) called La Cheyrelle. A standing clock from this project was part of an integrated suite of furniture, textiles, and wall decoration (7.12). Serrurier-Bovy incorporated greatly elongated copper mounts, emphasizing the verticality of this traditional form. The purely decorative nature of the mounts plays with the relationship between utility and ornamentation. The enameled decoration and angular forms on the face were entirely at home in the rustic ensemble at La Cheyrelle.

Born in Antwerp and trained as a painter in Belgium and France, Henry Van de Velde is another central figure in the diffusion and reinterpretation of Arts and Crafts ideas on the continent. Van de Velde's activities in Brussels and then in Wilhelmine Germany show how the ideas and forms of the British Arts and Crafts movement were shifted to reflect conditions in continental Europe. Amid the influx of decorative arts into the Belgian salons and the Post-Impressionist interest in the decorative surface, Van de Velde's search for an artistic identity culminated in his adoption of Arts and Crafts ideas. Already experimenting with decorative design by 1892, Van de Velde declared that he had been introduced to the ideas of Ruskin and Morris through Alfred William Finch, his close friend and fellow member of Les XX. Finch, who was born in Belgium of English parentage, was active as a printmaker and painter in Brussels. In the 1890s he learned how to make ceramic pots, exploring in particular the tradition of low-fire peasant wares. For Van de Velde, Finch, and others, the English handicraft movement manifested the ideal that welldesigned and well-made objects, rather than painting or sculpture, could uplift and embellish daily life.

Van de Velde's zealous conversion to craft as an ethical code is embodied in his appliqué called Lα Veillée des anges (The Angels' Watch), which was executed with the help of an aunt (7.13). Shown at Les XX in 1893, The Angels'Watch was listed in the catalogue with a quote from a thirteenth-century guild leader about the masterpiece tradition required of a craftsman. The appliqué (or "tapestry," as the artist called it) also summed up Van de Velde's knowledge of

oherevB avant-garde artistic ideas, combining contrasting threads against the flat expanses of color he had seen in the work of painters Paul Gauguin and Maurice Denis. Similarly flat, abstract, and decorative, Tdepic  grou peasant women he traditional bonnetcmmunion v ating a sacred child. The profoundly moral tone ofVan de Velde's first and most overtly religious work ofdecorative art symbolizes his personal transformation from easel painter to designer of objects for everyday life.33

![](images/4a0f0647f76dfe32b468a527993d9abe3af6652085d4cbd59c11eda6b831a7b1.jpg)
