![](images/ae0bc52c6982bf6d817da9ffc53fc2f655befb5ed21a981f6f1580626ef180f0.jpg)

L a new identity as he proselytized his faith in applied art. When Maria Sèthe, his future wife, planned a trip to London in January 1893, Van de Velde asked her to visit Morris and gather samples of English alpapr hiche at s  n sein crs h pli

![](images/215310f1ce5dfe238785eceefb6ef373a17485a3c968fca7206a0344e29f2389.jpg)

Van de Velde's lectures drew heavily on English theory, especially Walter Crane's Claims f Decorative Art (1892), which was translated into Dutch as Kunst en Samenleving (Art and Society) in 1894, and which Van de Velde assige in   his casses.Crane beli in n art or all reior de Velde's reverence for the peasant, or "primitive" man, who seemed to possess an instinctive sense of ornamentation.36

Van de Velde extended his quest for an authentic form of artistic expression to the dwelling. In 1894 he began building a small house for himself and   
his wife on a plot adjacent to his mother-in-law's house in Uccle, a rural village   
outside Brussels. Called Bloemenwerf (Dutch for a field of flowers), Van de Velde   
based his design on the model of modern cottage architecture so widely admired   
on the continent (7.15). Françoise Aubry has discussed how Van de Velde may   
have adapted the designs of M. H. Baillie Scott and C. F. A. Voysey, which were   
puliheu. ey  y   
Velde and his wife, was fitted out with varnished oak furniture and rush-seat   
chairs to evoke a vernacular ideal. In the dining room, Van de Velde placed the   
slender chairs around a table inlaid with stoneware tiles by the French potter   
Alexandre Bigot (7.14 and 7.16). On the walls were papers of his own design evok  
ing the example of the English wallpapers that he o admired. In an adjacent sitting room, Van de Velde upholstered armchairs in Morris fabric, and in a series of photographs taken at the house around 19oo, Maria Van de Velde wears reform dresses also made from Morris fabric (7.17). ³8 A key figure in her husband's initial contact with English art, Maria Van de Velde continued to play an important role as a partner and designer. Her Dahlia wallpaper was used at Bloemenwerf and became a signature composition even before it was exhibited as part of an ensemble at the Munich Secession in 1899 (7.18).

![](images/15bacd022d2374411454cd1709797024dc788227595148e7fbeba5ce67c72353.jpg)

![](images/00d982af9997721c8a41fe04deb6811861ea7d25353fa1005bf9a37a0b2bddfe.jpg)  
.7 Maria and Henry Van deVelde t Bloemenwerf c.1900 .18W 8n

The attempt to create a refuge for art and life at Bloemenwerf was indebted to the ideas and designs of the British Arts and Crafts movement, but it was also part of a broader European movement to idealize everyday life andgive it an aestheticform; in other words,tostage and dramatize the ordinary as a grand Gesamtkunstwerk, or total work of art.

As Van de Velde embraced handicraft as a mission, he also turned to the business of selling items for the home. While completing Bloemenwerf, he designed three interiors for the 1895 inaugural exhibition of Bing's L'Art Nouveau, including a dining room with the Nabis members Paul Ranson and Edouard Vuillard (7.19). Nationalists such as Arsène Alexandre derided Bing's enterprise and Van de Velde's ensembles as diseased, unhealthy, and a caricature of English art.3 Yet Camille Mauclair, a prolific critic of the modern decorative arts, commented favorably on the rusticity and harmony of the ensemble as a "memory of English cottages and Norman interiors." Working out of Bloemenwerf, Van de Velde and his wife designed wallpaper and friezes, jewelry, light fixtures, textile patterns, and bookbindings, including one for English Bookbindings in the Collection of the British Museum of 1895 (7.21). Finch sold some of his first pots through this early incarnation of Van de Velde's business, the Société Van de Velde (7.20). After 1897 Van de Velde designed his most characteristic compositions, including a silverplated candlestick with six arms poised in dynamic tension (7.22). The free, swinging curve that animates this composition is evidence of how he incorporated a painter's perception of line and form into the design of useful goods.

![](images/851874f2655dd093749b9cac4000b9b90dda98031280ee880b864e4d4af303fa.jpg)

![](images/bb32b3649298222b5ce073aad9db06e1962647263ed7cb7708c12643d107148c.jpg)  
Vas, c.89798AlfWillii  
Henry Van de Velde

![](images/f455b0f6a248098fc62ae30be8b79838d9e080a9fd97f25368b87bde2f13745c.jpg)

The chief investors in Van de Velde's enterprise were Germans, including the aesthetes and art patrons Eberhard von Bodenhausen and Harry Kessler. Van de Velde owed his German connections to the efforts of Julius Meier-Graefe, who as editor of L'Art Décoratif devoted numerous pages to Van de Velde's work in 1898." In 1899, when Meier-Graefe opened La Maison Moderne, his decorative arts gallery in Paris, he commissioned Van de Velde to design the interior and exterior of the shop. An advertising poster by Maurice Biais shows a well-dressed woman peering into a Van de Velde vitrine (7.23). In 1898 Van de Velde created an armchair he had first designed for Bloemenwerf, shown at Bing's L'Art Nouveau, and then included in several German commissions with slight variations from the original design. This example, purchased from La Maison Moderne by the Nordenfjeldske Kunstindustrimuseum in Trondheim around 1900, was executed in African padouk and is the only one known to survive with the riginal silk batik upholstery by Van de Velde's close friend the Dutch painter and designer Johan Thorn-Prikker (7.24).

Van de Velde's efforts to outfit his house and set up his own business have been viewed as direct imitation of Morris.Van de Velde himself had acknowledged Morris in early writings, and after he began his business, he particularly encouraged the comparison. In 1898 he published a study of Morris through the socialist press L'Avenir Social, and he continued to lecture and write about Morris throughout his career." In francophone Europe, Morris became even more revered after his death in 1896, a moment that coincided with the renewed attention on the decorative arts. As the Arts and Crafts movement gained popular attention, its ideas were fitted into existin rameworks that suited established values and theoris of decoration in continental Europe.

While British goods and ideas were widely respected in turn-of-the-century France, decorative artists did not accept them with the same enthusiasm as their Belgian counterparts. Historical tensions between France and Britain and perceived cultural differences in temperament (in the racialized language  the nineteenth centuryngosaxon" was opoed to "a were frequently offered as justification for not following British design. Belgium, by contrast, such as Emile Galle, Jean Dampt, Auguste Delaherche, Pierre-Adrien Dalpayrat, Ernest Chaplet, Alexandre Charpentier, Charles Plumet, and many others who designed and made objects as modern artistic commodities, never formed a single cohesive group that followed the tenets of the British Arts and Crafts. Critics such as Lahor and Soulier believed that this was because of the lack of a charismatic leader such as Morris, the cultural weight of the historical styles, and pride in French design. The classical and medieval traditions remained forceful aesthetic and cultural models for decorative artists of the nineteenth century, and eighteenth-century Rococo forms were upheld as examples of "good taste" and the epitome of French craftsmanship during the 1880s and 1890s. The graphic designer Georges Auriol, an admirer of English accomplishments (and the designer of an advertisement for the French-language supplement for The Studio [see

![](images/a67f61112fd8beccf4c5f4455fb40b1b1086ec9695280cf060728d6341ac38b6.jpg)

![](images/f2b3614e9d952817ed86bba3dea1d6982fc522448c60e12c0fddbdb78931c1d8.jpg)

7.1]), commented that while the new decorative tendencies might be easily adapted in Belgium, Germany, and Holland, France was less receptive "because in this country we stil respect certain art traditions."46

The histories of short-lived groups, such as the Groupe de I'Art dans Tout (Art in Everything) or Les Cinq (The Five), reveal how the rhetoric of the Arts and Crafts was integrated into their thinking along with Viollet-le-Duc's widely accepted emphasis on rationality in construction and decoration. In the catalogue for an 1896 exhibition, the members of Les Cinq (Charpentier, Plumet, Dampt, Henry Nocq, and Henri Sauvage) claimed that

The objects that we exhibit are all meant above all to "serve.". Another condition that we intend to observe is sincerity, that is, professional horesty. Our silk fabrics are real silk, our gold jewelry is made of gold, chased by hand; our furniture is innocent of all "trickery" and "disguise" and that alone—though it may seem paradoxical—is rare enough that it deserves to be pointed out. Artistic honesty, respect for materials, the necessary usage of objects destined to be a part of our lives—that is what we are searching for.48

Employing some of the key ideas of the Arts and Crafts, such as the elevation of the decorative arts and respect for "honest" use of materials, Les Cinq (which became Les Six in 1897 when Nocq left and was replaced by Etienne Moreau-Nélaton and Tony Selmersheim) created restrained but deliberately luxurious objects, such as a dressing table by Plumet and Selmersheim (7.25). In formal terms, however, these artists held to French decorative traditions, updating and assimilating eighteenth-century models.

Like other continental nations, France's established art industries were not dependent on the adoption of British Arts and Crafts ideals. They did flourish, however, under the new attention and acceptance given to the decorative arts. Emile Gallé transformed his father's ceramics business into a large factory best known for the production of art glass and furniture. Even the potters Chaplet, Delaherche, and Dalpayrat, who were mostly trained within the conservative ceramics industry, rose to become celebrated figures whose work with porcelain, stoneware, and high-fire glazes was widely praised at international exhibitions, avant-garde galleries, and the reformed French Société Nationale des Beaux-Arts, which began to include decorative arts in 1890. These fin-de-siècle French craftsmen relied on the established aesthetic traditions of production and artisanal potteries, but they also drew from the concerns and fashions of their own time. In the 1880s Chaplet, for example, embellished his pots with imagery from the British illustrator Kate Greenaway's books for children, which had become a sensation in Paris. As their pots found a sophisticated audience at the salons and in the decorative arts magazines, they were received into a climate that increasingly favored an Arts and Crafts romanticism of the deliberately untutored, or "primitive," craftsman. The widely promoted image of the humble potter, for example, was extended to Chaplet's porcelain, as well as Delaherche's matte stoneware and his exotic sang-de-boeuf flambé glazes emulating Asian examples. Delaherche's work particularly impressed American potter William Grueby when he saw it at the World's Columbian Exposition in Chicago in 1893 (7.26).

As modern decorative arts and crafts found sophisticated collectors and began to enter museum collections, critics and artists called for the applied arts movement to play a social role. Since they were associated with indigenous popular culture as well as artisanal and national tradition, the decorative arts were given a prominent place in the rhetoric of social reformers who sought an art for everyday life. Morris and his colleagues famously championed the applied arts as a popular expression and as a means of elevating the worker socially and spiritually. Yet many continental designers sympathetic to Arts and Crafts ideas were not craftsmen, nor did they practice the same kind of anti-industrial skepticism concerning modern machinery. The French critic Roger Marx believed that a combination of efficient production and authentic cultural form would constitute a vital "I'art social." Avowedly anti-Ruskin, Marx held

![](images/64bce3bf7d3ae6e50b5213f2f23920db5671e1cb552c1451d5097e5cd8838fd9.jpg)

that rather than looking across the channel, the simple lessons of national tradition would provide a genuine model. Designers in Belgium and France did not emphasize the personal satisfaction of making things as much as they did the possibility of making life joyful through the integration of art into daily life. This idea was taken up by both socialists, who sponsored model housing and programs of art education, and conservatives, who sought to improve taste and inculcate bourgeois values. At the center of this discourse was the dwelling and its decoration.

Serrurier-Bovy made his name on the rustic Artisan's Room, but he found commercial success with his flamboyant designs in exotic woods. The obvious luxuriousness of his designs and commissions of the late 1890s, including the exclusive Pavilion Bleu restaurant for the Exposition Universelle in Paris in 1900, did not detract from his image as a humble artisan.51

![](images/26a78e96846c38fcdab6c2a92e04c003c98a10752dbc68baadac39c19b26a5ea.jpg)

After 1900 Serrurier-Bovy returned with renewed determination to egalitarian ideals. Projects for an exhibition of worker housing in his native Liège and an interior from 1904 published in LArt Décoratifillustrate how he reconsidered his designs from both a social and a visual perspective (7.27). The cohesive, wholesome image of the dining room interior not only recalls his exhibition designs from the mid-189os, but also shows the new line of furniture in varnished woods that he introduced around this time. Serrurier-Bovy designed this series, called Silex, as an inexpensive, easy-to-assemble answer to the challenge of producing low-cost furniture. With increasingly angular forms and the prominent use of metal screws and stencil decoration, Serrurier-Bovy's Silex furniture embodied the abstract qualities of forthrightness and economy. A night table from a bedroom suite in production around 1904 demonstrates how he substituted plain oak, painted steel plate, and stenciled motifs for the costly materials and enameled mounts of more expensive objects. Although Silex was intended to be cheap and plain, Serrurier-Bovy did not neglect the relationship between aesthetics and utility, leaving space for a chamber pot behind the night table's sliding metal door (7.28).

The full assimilation of Arts and Crafts ideas and values was evident in the cooperative organization Le Cottage and its periodical of the same name, which was edited in Brussels (with headquarters in Paris) from 1903 to 1905 (7.29). Dedicated to "all that concerns habitation,"

Le Cottage took its name and mandate from an Arts and Crafts ideal of simple, healthy living in the countryside. The magazine contained articles on contemporary and vernacular domestic architecture, interior design, and the decorative arts. It published extensively on British reform culture, particularly on philanthropic model villages, such as Bourneville and Port Sunlight. Another focus was on the development of the Garden City, and Le Cottage published translations of works by Ebenezer Howard, who originated this particular ideal of a town where the municipal center contained a park, surrounded by houses and gardens and finally by an agicultural bel.Cnriutors tgeinsisteon the cutrysies  i physicl societal health and looked to an Arts and Crafts model of decentralized housing. Published decorative schemes by the Chevalier de Wouters de Bouchout, a librarian and amateur designer, frankly imitated those by M. H. Baillie Scott and other English architects. Acknowledging the wholesome image of Arts and Crafts design, Wouters de Bouchout offered readers practical solutions for "cottage" interiors. In a lesson on wall decoration, he pronounced contemporary continental design with its abstracted natural motifs and restless line as "defective"; the better" solution incuded spare angularity, plain wood, and landscape friezes (7.30).

![](images/56e361f68e07ebab5758f1e3aa4c3e8b6b9c079cc4710f4f6c24bcd6dd6df11c.jpg)  
0 u 7.28 Night table, Silex line, 1905-6. Gustave Serrurier-Bovy

![](images/14cac7fdf8275caebac8a38dad3e4d57cbf6faeec4449173c451ed684a54877c.jpg)

Critical differences between Art Nouveau and Arts and Crafts aesthetics and ideas, however, are more difficult to distinguish than Wouters de Bouchout insinuates. Art Nouveau, the name Bing chose for his Paris gallery, is a term now used to describe an international range o

decorative and architectural expressions that flourished around 1900. Yet in its own time, Art Nouveau was one of many different names, including Modern Style, Jugendstil, or Nieuwe Kunst, that suggested the visual and ideological diversity of its many incarnations. The first scholars of turn-of-the-century design established a set of formal characteristics to define Art Nouveau style, including a preference for curvilinear shapes, abstracted natural motifs, and aestheticism, which seemed to set it apart from the Arts and Crafts emphasis on rectilinearity, close observation of nature, and socialism.³3 While continental Art Nouveau has often been defined in contrast to the Arts and Crafts movement, its close relationship to British design and design reform ideas has always been acknowledged. This contradictory situation is made more confusing in writings of the period. Although Walter Crane famously distanced British design from the "disease" of Art Nouveau, Jean Lahor opined that the works and ideas of the British designers constituted an "art nouveau."5" Instead of focusing on formal appearance, recent scholarship has emphasized the underlying values-including the significance of regional and national identity—that so many of the international applied arts movements shared.55

![](images/aec0f4109f416afb5421b5340b7800468ae6025f4707be09931bff4cdd7813f4.jpg)

![](images/8c7fdb9d7a8c594a97670a4d1d63cea9d81c7d329703b49f0e68508de333a9e9.jpg)

The image of the Arts and Crafts movement, received through illustrated magazines and in artfully arranged shops and exhibition salons, stimulated wide debate on politics, economics, and artistic culture in

Belgium and France at the turn of the century. Belgian artists and intellectuals praised the reformist vision of the British designers and used the model to reinforce their image of Belgium as a modern industrial culture with a splendid heritage of craftsmanship. While Belgian turn-ofthe-century design is rarely discussed without mention of Arts and Crafts ideas, the reverse is true for French design of the same period. Economic competition and cultural nationalism were perhaps the chief factors in the wary assessment of British ideas and designs in France. Although French critics and designers admired British products and theories, most did not emulate them formally or ideologically. Instead, the British Arts and Crafts movement was received largely as an aesthetic theory in France. The emphasis on beauty, evident in studies such as Robert de la Sizeranne's Ruskin et la religion de la beauté (Ruskin and the Religion of Beauty, 1897), also extended to forthright followers such as Van de Velde and Serrurier-Bovy, whose stridently socialist commitment to improving the life of working people through objects for the home was achieved as a grand Gesamtkunstwerk. Utopian in outlook, Arts and Crafts theory and design had tangible consequences, nourishing a profound and long-lasting reassessment of the production and broader cultural value of the applied arts throughout Europe.

# AMERICA: THE QUEST For DemocRAtic design

![](images/9b3a3021676d2bba9d670f68057aee17fbf0010146a327a865ccecd20c0397dd.jpg)

W especially wanted individual fulfillment for craftspeople and believed this was best achieved by making things by hand. But since he also wanted the objects to be affordable, his declaration reveals a fundamental paradox of the Arts and Crafts movement: craft is labor-intensive, so a fair wage for handwork usually precluded large-scale production. Morris defined art as "the expression of man's pleasure in labour," but the goal of earning a living by its creation while making objects widely available remained elusive.²

"Democratic design" was the term commonly used around 19oo for simple, good-quality objects made for a broad audience. This is what Europeans perceived as America's contribution to the Arts and Craft movement, as did Americans themselves. Design reformers on both sides of the Atlantic saw the country's triumph as one of technological innovation and the rational use of the machine. Like their counterparts in Britain and elsewhere, American advocates had vowed to change society through the transformation of the work process, but their focus was on democratic design. While the term was used in European countries, it had a more complex definition here, because the United States had the most democratic political system.

All the essays in this volume have demonstrated the inaccuracy of a common perception about the Arts and Crafts movement—that it was monolithically anti-industrial and antimodern. In fact, even the British movement accommodated the needs of commercial production. America (closely followed by Germany) was the least ambivalent about marketing the Arts and Crafts movement, and anti-industrialism was far less rooted in the country's culture. Consequently, the Arts and Crafts reached the broadest section of the populace here, with far less of Britain's ideological component. Historian Eileen Boris succinctly summarized the basic difference by observing that the American protagonists "democratized even as they diminished the legacy of Ruskin and Morris."3

The American story, however, is not a simple one about how the country diluted the movement's ideals by not adhering to its most profound aspirations. Among its many manifestations here, the movement adopted more than its fair share of Arts and Crafts idealistic paradigms: efforts to improve the lot of urban immigrants and the rural poor through craft; design schools that emphasized manual training rather than rote copying; groups of socialists (in cities such as Chicago and New York) committed to the creation of truly oppositional production methods; utopian yet unprofitable art colonies.

C. R. Ashbee, a protagonist of the British Arts and Crafts movement, extolled the American art colonies for their success in placing joy in labor above wide accessibility or even quality (which was the British movement's most heralded characteristic). Summing up a series of visits, he asserted, "We cannot measure the output of these personal shops by the high standards of the very best. That is what we in England have been doing—we of the A&C—and have thus made of a great social movement a narrow and tiresome aristocracy working with high skil for the very rich."

Furthermore, the United States had the largest middle class in the world, and thus the best conditions for the "do-it-yourself" aspect of the Arts and Crafts to thrive. Americans had the leisure necessary to pursue crafts as personal fulfllment—a domestic hobby apart from the white-collar world.® Embroidery kits, blanks for china painting, and classes for woodworking were widely available, as were instruction books such as Handicrafts in the Home." Architects could reach a far broader clientele by publishing model-house designs in mass-market journals. The most fmous and longest-running series (asting more than fifteen years) was sponsored by Edward Bok, the progressive editor of the Ladies' Home Journal. In 1895 he began commissioning architects (Frank Lloyd Wright among them) to design plans and elevations for moderately priced houses. Bok also asked Will Bradley, America's best-known graphic artist, to contribute ideal interiors, al with the goalof disseminating good design to midle-class consumers (8.3).

A Hungarian critic was one of many in Europe who observed that "the Americans are adapting to modern developments in their arts and crafts. Their purpose is to abandon handcraft and to work only with machinery This is the realization of democracy in art."While this statement about complete mechanization is as exaggerated as Ashbee's wistful musings about communities of individual happy craftsmen, both represent how Americans adapted the paradox of British ideals to American circumstances. After looking at the reform efforts in America, which were modeled closely on British precedent, this essay will explore how the achievement of product accessibility and American regional variations made the movement here distinct.

The moral aesthetics of American reformers derived from Britain, most notably from John Ruskin and William Morris. Their writings were best-sellers; reading groups met throughout the United States to discuss them; American leaders invoked their names with an almost religious fervor. Arts and Crafts ideals and designs were disseminated via hundreds of organizations modeled on London's Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society. British art journals such as The Studio were widely available; an American edition, The International Studio, began publication in 1897, four years after the magazine was founded in London.

Although neither Ruskin nor Morris ever visited the United States, many British leaders did make the journey. In addition to Ashbee, others—including Walter Crane, Oscar Wilde, T. J. Cobden-Sanderson, and Morris's daughter May—either gave lectures or accompanied exhibitions of their work. All met with American designers and critics, offering advice about what the country could do to further the agenda of design reform. Friendships were made during these visits; for example, Ashbee maintained close contacts with Frank Lloyd Wright and recorded visits to many other important Arts and Crafts figures, such as Will Price (the founder of the Rose Valley art colony outside Philadelphia) and Charles Sumner Greene in Pasadena. In turn, American leaders went to Britain to participate in settlement houses and art colonies, or just to meet their colleagues and often role models. Charles Eliot Norton, Harvard's first professor of the history of art, had a long-standing friendship with Ruskin and became the first president of Boston's Society of Arts and Crafts. Ernest Batchelder, who founded the Handicraft Guild in Minneapolis (and later, an important tile company in Pasadena [8.1]), traveled to Ashbee's art colony in Chipping Campden in 1905. He chronicled the experience in Gustav Stickley's Craftsman magazine, the most influential American journal devoted entirely to the Arts and Crafts movement.

![](images/81789ca058b7f6dd3fc9b2a1c2cd49f3811d7aa93501e3993fd072b14e9b9294.jpg)

![](images/696159973d2be179e86a3f74f03855c566f182a8abc8ea6994419f630e294271.jpg)

![](images/a4d3d8bb9f0570d8703a63e3833ddb6f889068cb3212f1fa795ac24ffe25dba1.jpg)

Jane Addams visited the pioneering settlement house Toynbee Hall in East London and returned to Chicago in 1889 to found Hull House, which became America's most important social settlement. There, immigrants demonstrated craft activities at the Hull House Labor Museum to affirm their native traditions as a bulwark against the alienation of life in a new country, often in a hostile urban environment. Programs were also initiated to provide the economically disadvantaged with new skills, not just recreational relief from the tedium of factory work or psychological solace. Ellen Gates Starr, cofounder of Hull House, spent more than a year as an apprentice to T. J. Cobden-Sanderson at the Doves Bindery and then established a hand bindery at Hull House in 1898 (8.4). Her bindery was decorated with framed photographs of Ruskin and Morris as well as pages from the Kelmscott and Doves presses. Starr was a committed socialist who believed in the redeeming power of art and in changing the nature of labor. Like many rueful radicals, however, she would later concede, "If I had thought it through, I would have realized that I would be using my hands to create books that only the rich could buy."8

The mixture of charity and design training proved a potent one in many other media, such as ceramics, metalwork, and furniture. The designers tended to be middle- and upper-class women, as were the patron-philanthropists who sponsored their efforts. Financially supported by Mrs. James Storrow, the Paul Revere Pottery exemplifies this relationship. Established in 1908 to produce and sell breakfast and tea sets, lamps, tiles, and vases, the pottery developed out of the Saturday Evening Girls' Club at the Boston Public Library's North End branch. Pottery decoration gave the daughters of Italian and Jewish immigrants an alternative to factory employment and an opportunity to earn money while receiving artistic instruction. Professional designer Edith Brown oversaw all the work; decorators like Sara Galner executed it (8.5).

Many other art potteries furthered a social agenda. The Marblehead Pottery in Massachusetts was founded in 1904 to provide occupational therapy for "nervously worn-out patients" recovering from tuberculosis. The following year, when the work proved too onerous for the patients, the pottery was separated from the sanatorium. It soon became one of the leading art potteries in America, known for its subtle glazes and abstract geometric decoration (8.6). The original intentions of Marblehead were followed by Arequipa in Fairfax, California, where the decoration of ceramics was to provide "art as a tonic" for tubercular patients, and its sale a subsidy for their stay at the pottery's associated sanatorium (8.7).°

Textiles-embroidery, rugs, weaving, and lace—were the most important media for arts and crafts in the service of philanthropy. Educational institutions such as London's Royal School of Art Needlework (8.9) inspired the establishment of American associations devoted to design training for women. As Candace Wheeler, the first professional woman interior deierinAmeri pointeut,he l  theroupshe was isrental  fon i the 187 an 8s, lie he Society  Deative Art in New York,was o turnhecn annalinableheritageminiskill in he  theee nt men  rprs sion and pecuniary profit."

In 1879 Wheeler joined Louis Comfort Tiffany (and others) to design interiors as Associated Artists. When the successful firm disbanded four years later, Wheeler adopted the name, and she and her family operated the new company for twenty-five more years—her daughter Dora was one of the chief designers (8.8). Wheeler's interests lay in helping gentlewomen fallen on hard times or in encouraging the creative strivings of people like herself, for whe rpeng was oevn rvatin  e souls of women who pined for independence." New opportunities for design training and product distribution did much for women's advancement; the greater fluidity of American society opened more possibilities than were available in Europe for a woman to establish an identity as an artistic professional.

The preceding discussion represents the two distinct but often overlapping types of craft activity that Juliet Kinchin describes in her essay in this volume as "home industries" and "artistic craft": the former were more numerous and less documented; the latter, far better documented and consisting of (rather than mentored by) professional artists. Each country discussed in this book had many groups that represented these two types of craft, both emphatically part of the Arts and Crafts movement.

Wheeler was the cofounder in 1878 of the Women's Exchange, devoted to helping women sell products made at home regardless of their aesthetic merit, but most of the organizations she led fit the category of "artistic craft." The next decades—from 1890 to 1910—would see the establishment of hundreds of these organizations. The Newcomb Pottery, for example, was part of a women's college at Tulane University in New Orleans. It was founded in 1895, to fulfill "a dual purpose—to create pottery (and soon after other hand-crafted objects) of high artistic quality, and to provide young women at the college an opportunity to earn money. It was to be a 'model' industry,"13

The goal of the "home-industry" groups was slightly different—to provide impoverished immigrant or rural people (largely women) with instruction from those more economically privileged and formally trained in design (often at schools for "artistic craft"). Like Florence Vere O'Brien, with her revival of lace making in Ireland, and Mrs. Gyarmathy, who promoted

the production and marketing of textiles made by peasant women in Hungary, American women also formed groups to teach and market crafts that emanated from indigenous "folk" or "primitive" segments of society. In the American South, more than one hundred settlement schools (usually operated by Protestant missions) were established, mostly by designers from the northeast states. In North Carolina, for example, professional artists supplied local women with designs based on Appalachian weaving. These designs were modified—either to standardize production or to appeal to particular notions that northerners, who were a primary market, had about the rural South.'4

The mission of these home industries was to encourage the survival, or in some cases revival, of design traditions. Their efforts reinforced Arts and Crafts aesthetic values about regionalism and reflected the contemporary nostalgia for an idealized preindustrial past. The artistic crafts also drew upon a reverence for place. According to the director of the Newcomb Pottery: "The whole thing was to be a southern product, made of southern clays, by southern artists, decorated with southern subjects!"s Consequently, magnolias, live oaks, hanging moss, cypress trees, and other southern vegetation were the primary motifs on the pottery and other crafts made there (8.11 and 8.12).

The drive to preserve a national past that also informed the present could provide as strong a motivation as philanthropy. Margaret Whiting and Ellen Miller founded the Society of Blue and White in Deerfield, Massachusetts, in 1896 to preserve the techniques of eighteenthcentury embroidery they had admired in their local historical society. Whiting later explained their creative adaptation of these designs: "Since it was the spirit of the Colonial work that called for revival.the quarry of drawings and specimens of old work were used with discriminating freedom."16 The embroidery Mayflower pays homage to the American past, but it also freely departs from the colonial model (8.10). Within a few years, Whiting and Miller were successfully marketing the society's textiles throughout the United States.

Trained at art schools in New York, Whiting and Miller designed all of the society's patterns, which were usually made by local women as a way of supplementing their incomes. Welloff women who summered in Deerfield also participated in the execution of the work. Passionate about quality and the preservation (as well as the updating) of a threatened craft, Whiting and Miller were not among those whose primary concern was the improvement of social conditions. Whiting insisted, "It is not charity but art which founds and maintains a craft."17

![](images/d9f5dc7e9aa922d1ee6d8d54ec0975655ce3ea0077da5350bfb3522b4bd92d96.jpg)

![](images/228403a269176294a44f7ac5ac30d7a57da442b5a5ba4633d7a1981cb207b7a7.jpg)

All of the many groups linked under the broad rubric "Arts and Crafts" ardently believed that objects and buildings made from natural materials taken from the local environment would provide uplift and cultural cohesion for people who made or used them. This British concept of the central importance of the domestic vernacular had two manifestations in America. The frst, and more significant, was the doctrine of fidelity to place and traditional techniques, which encouraged designers to look to the American landscape and past. The material culture of colonial NewEngland and themiAtlantc atepeale themoral shetiArts andCat movement enthusiasts. They saw in these historical precedents the "natural" and straightforward use of materials that they were advocating for modern building and design.

The oher manifestation, especially prevalent in the Northeast, was the direct borrowing of British styles. Partners Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue and Ralph Adams Cram provide a good example. Leadersof design reform in Boston, they helped found the Society of Arts and Crafts there in 1897, and they drew heavily from the English Gothic for their architecture. Goodhue designed the type, borders, and decorative initials for the best-known American Arts and Crafts publication, The Altar Book (8.13), which is also the one most influenced by Morris's Kelmscott Press (see fig. 2.21). Goodhue's later work embraced Spanish and Byzantine design, as well as any other vocabulary he deemed appropriate for a commission.'8

![](images/943e16244b0e4746fcf41b9e80d18c24f7905b0077d43c1f87dd7c72b8e76aaf.jpg)

This approach was especially characteristic of   
American architects and craftsmen—for them, the Arts and Crafts was not so much a style as an attitude toward the making of objects. The Philadelphia workshop of Samuel Yellin, who championed   
the revival of architectural wrought iron, demonstrates   
eclectic sources—he borrowed from medieval France and   
Germany as well as Renaissance Italy. His prototype for a   
ceilngrille  Renaissance n nspiration, but the mo   
facing birds indicates that sixteenth-century Florentine   
artists were also borrowing from Islamic sources (8.14).   
Yellin might also have been looking more locally for this   
motif—to eighteenth-century Pennsylvania German pot  
tery and furniture. No matter where his sources came   
from, the important thing for Yellin was that the work   
be forged by hand. He declared, "There is only one way   
to make good decorative iron work and that is with the   
hammer at the anvil."9 In 1925 he evoked the by-then sacred name of William Morris to emphasize the

![](images/3d4950aeaa3e8f6d4cfd7595cf87794a5d68a430538f9933920edd35b4bef917.jpg)

absolute necessity of drawing inspiration from the past. The key was never to fall prey to "a slavish imitation or repetition of old things, which were created in a different age, under entirely different circumstances."20

If American Arts and Crafts ideology is most distinguished by efforts to make design more democratic, then its aesthetic is most distinctive in regional expression, as seen in the architecture and decorative arts of the West Coast and Midwest. The Prairie School was the most ahistoric of American regional styles. With Louis Sullivan and Frank Lloyd Wright as its leading lights, the Prairie School was a group of mostly Chicago-based architects (including George Grant Elmslie and George Washington Maher) who in their search for original American design "had raised their standard of revolt against the disciplined ranks and array of custom."2' The architect Irving Pond wrote about this shared quest for indigenous expression, one particularly suited to the Midwest, stating, "horizontal lines...appeal to the disciples of this school as echoing the spirit of the prairies of the great Middle West, which to them embodies the essence of democracy."22 These architects shared the conviction that a building must respond to its surroundings: it must be horizontal, like the prairie, and tied to the earth through low-hipped roofs, broad eaves, and extensions—porches, terraces, and pergolas.

The ideal of an "organic" architecture originated with Sullivan, who beginning in the 1880s advocated the creation of a new American architecture through the simplification of mass and ornamentation based on the growth of plants. Through his voluminous writings, as well as his innovative buildings, such as the Carson Pirie Scott department store, Sullivan became the philosophical leader of the Prairie School. The members of the group were linked in many ways: some shared office space and some were instrumental in founding organizations such as the

Architectural League of America and the Chicago Society of Arts and Crafts.

Organic ornamentation was integral to George Grant Elmslie's work, a legacy of the twenty years (1889—1909) he spent in Sullivan's office (fifteen as chief designer). The rug Elmslie designed for the Henry B. Babson House (8.16) serves as a unifying device; its central medallion is repeated in many elements of the décor, including other rugs, stained-glass windows, andirons, and a tall-case clock. When Elmslie left Sullivan's office and began a partnership with William Gray Purcell (much of the interior furnishing for the Babson House was designed after the two became partners), they explained the function of these repeating geometric motifs abstracted from nature as "an organic part of a structure, an efflorescence of the idea represented in the building itself."23

![](images/299d9f57601fe61ae800b429c11eae1a161168c9a873c8c63d57e78d262eddfb.jpg)

George Washington Maher followed this ideal of design unity even more fervently. He developed his own version, the "motif rhythm theory," which involved the constant repetition of a motif drawn from nature, preferably one unique to the site, or one particularly meaningful to the client. This motif would "bind the design together." The stylized thistle on the fireplace surround from the Patrick J. King House in Chicago, for example, is found throughout the interior (8.17).

Maher embraced the notion that democracy in architecture "mean[s] that buildings should be erected to fit the actual needs of the people." The editors of a periodical devoted to the Midwest, The Western Architect, saw this as analogous to the fundamental principles of a democratic government, which they defined as "one conducted for all the people with equal opportunities and no special privileges."s This multifaceted definition of democratic design, which included not only availability to the middle classes but also an organic relationship to the lancapeanda incusive politialagenda, isaparticularlyAmicanintepretatioArt n Crafts principles, most fully realized in the Midwest.

![](images/f2804638001df86365162c3efc26bcf09d1e137932536ca7fad4bff165e93d04.jpg)

While Sullivan provided the philosophical underpinnings of the Prairie School, it was Wrigt whoater leaving his posiion as draan in Sulliva ff in893evolv aden language that made him the preeminent member of the group. As H. Alen Brooks originally pointed out, Wright's major development was to greatly expand upon the design of interior spaces that were not enclosed in the traditional sense. In the innovative Ward Willits House of 1902, the corners between the dining room, living room, and porch were abolished, creating interpenetrating spaces and freeing the walls to define distinct areas rather than enclose them (8.18). The house was thus opened from within, and like the Francis W. Little House (8.19), its long horizontal lines, low-pitched roofs, overhanging eaves, and expansive porches tied it to the environment.

Maintaining their traditional protective function, the walls also serve as screens, open at the corners to allow a continuous flow of space. Wright intended that the leaded-glass windows he designed for his houses also function this way, as "light screens." A second-story window, now known as Tree of Life, from the Darwin D. Martin House is among Wright's most revered designs (8.20). Wright's client Mr. Martin, however, had objected to using it on the ground floor as well, "because of the density of the design, which would limit the light and prevent anyone standing in the room fromseeingut.Wright insiste that his windowdesi did ot nefere with their function and instead "add[ed] a higher architectural note to the effect of light itself." His furnishings were not meant to be seen as autonomous works of art, but only in the context of the Gesamtkunstwerk, the total work of art. Clients were expected to restrain their ho

![](images/d05449b73a8e317ac0cac29da04ec95e365bd0b4323121b8fa81ed6b78c12a69.jpg)

![](images/36bd9e414341d0eefe17a4894adc51c3ec6f6555d7b2fd67214f0568938a0a95.jpg)

![](images/3c2bc64a3bc26faa0f7acbad9d29a49cd925dff27ee93893366425d161e364ee.jpg)

Although few Prairie architects went as far as Wright's "destruction of the box," all shared with him the Arts and Crats gal  des nity, and all  least paid lip service otheir partic lar ideal of democratic design. Anthony Alofsin succinctly summarized the concept of organic architecture that Wright had stated in his "In the Cause of Architecture" (198). All six principles enumerated are fundamental to the Arts and Crafts movement as well as to the beliefs of the other Prairie School architects: truth to "the nature of materials"; conventionalized (stylized) design; honest structure; simplicity; a multiplicity of styles; and the essential dictate, that the building be tied to the landscape hrough the use color, local materials, and the sies own geography. The pitch of the lampshade for the lamp Wright designed for the Susan Lawrence Dana House, for example, echoes the slope of the roof of the house, and the earth tones of the glass reflect the colors of the surrounding prairie (8.22).

Despite his commitment to invest individuality in specific commissions, Wright did not hesitate to reuse a design if he particularly liked it. His copper urn and weed vase reappear in several Wright residences, among them the Dana House, the Avery Coonley House, and Wright's

![](images/ba6019f507577e42bc97aeeb638cf9348d674b2629ec24ffe6c44fc65a7476dc.jpg)

own home and studio (8.21 and 8.23). Wright designed a chair composed of intersecting planes about 1904, and he used variations of it in his studio (8.24) and in many buildings, such as the Unity Church, the Hillside Home School, and the Larkin Office Building. Such repetition was permitted, even desired, because Wright defined organic composition as the geometric relationships that form the basis of all structures. All objects should be reduced to their basic geometry—the circle, the triangle, the square, the octagon, and the cube. These forms were easily executed by machines, so Wright believed the machine was a crucial tool for the realization of his artistic and social goals, as will be more thoroughly discussed later in this essay.

Like the Midwest, California is a region that is associated with particular design characteristics. Sleeping porches, pergolas, and patios (features almost unknown in inclement Great Britain) were certainly used throughout America, but never more often or to greater effect than in California, where the mild climate and lush vegetation were most conducive to the Arts and Crafts ideal of living close to nature. The work of brothers Charles Sumner and Henry Mather Greene epitomizes the high end of Arts and Crafts architecture and design. Ashbee deeply admired their Pasadena architecture as "among the best there is in this country." He compared it favorably to the work of Frank Lloyd Wright, noting, "It is more refined and has more repose," and he speculated that those qualities were intrinsic to the region: "Perhaps it is California that speaks rather than Illinois."31

![](images/51b765f59b18f936864d48b37c117f5c640bfac3d964f3abe2e130327f38ec46.jpg)

![](images/f47ae6120eee075bcd5b631104294cd434eca4fdf74fb88418500b973bb71f1f.jpg)

![](images/7b817cc6e8fe90b2d079f17fa576c4a4dce7ccb8b581140b1221fe77cff6245c.jpg)

The Greenes' deepest desire was to make California "speak," but as with any living dialect, their vocabulary came from many sources, as did that of the Arts and Crafts movement itself. These influences included the manual training high school they attended in St. Louis (the first nonvocational institution to consider handwork to be as important as the liberal arts), their close examination of The Craftsman magazine, and the British Arts and Crafts movement. Charles wrote to a client in 1902, "I am in thorough sympathy with the Wm. Morris movement, in fact the whole inside of [your] house is influenced by it in design." Asian design was equally important. Charles collected Japanese prints and studied books about Japanese architecture; both brothers visited the Asian pavilions at the world's fairs in Chicago (1893) and St. Louis (1904). They saw in the spare, asymmetric design and superb craftsmanship of China and Japan the ultimate aesthetic ideal.

The Robert R. Blacker House provides a paradigm essential to the Arts and Crafts ideal, albeit one confined to the wealthy—the Gesamtkunstwerk, a complete work of art perfectly integrated with, and indeed of, its surroundings (8.26). For the exterior, the Greenes chose locally made "clinker" bricks and wood from the West Coast (California redwood and Oregon pine and cedar), and boulders from the Arroyo Seco (the local ravine) surround the pond, which was central to their landscape design. They united the exterior and interior with pergolas, terraces, balconies, and dramatic overhangs with projecting timbers. Bringing aspects of the garden indoors as leitmotifs further contributed to the overarching design unity. As Edward Bosley points out, "The Indian lotus [floating in the pond] would become the decorative thematic identity," and it is seen in the living room frieze and the iridescent glass lanterns (8.25).33

Although the exterior of the house displayed modest materials, chosen for their associations with the immediate surroundings or for their pleasing irregularity (clinker bricks are rejected in conventional building because they are overfired and consequently have uneven coloring and shapes), the interiors displayed the Greenes' equal delight in exotic materials, as well as their passion for the arts of Asia. The entry hall cabinet is made of teak and ebony, with elaborately carved panels depicting California oaks, whose "trunks" function as handles for the cupboards (8.28). Furnishings for other rooms are made of the finest mahogany, often with fruitwood inlay (8.27).

![](images/485d2f37d4cecf82508935ce84c14de885c346287921cb64883080b951045f63.jpg)

RobertR.Blacker House, Pasadena, California, 907 Hery Mather Greene andCharles SumnerGreen

![](images/a8b67af21bfba671e580a2b8a02620657a06d9101ca5162c7677a0dd71c217e9.jpg)  
h

The Greenes represent the Arts and Crafts at its most refined and elite; however, California also provided the movement with its most potent symbol of the democratization of art—the bungalow (8.29). Although built throughout the country, the bungalow has always been associated with California; its open interior, one-story plan, prominent porch, and low-pitched, overhanging roof offered the ventilation and protection from the sun suitable to the state's climate, and its rapid assembly, affordability, and informality made it particularly advantageous for the state's mobile scy.

The wood-framed bungalow was one manifestation of the local vernacular in California; ohr architectural styles were inspired by the state's Spanish, Mexican, and Native American traditions, as well as by the Mediterranean region. Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue, who had many commissions for buildings in California, received his most influential in 1911, when he was appointed consulting architect to the Panama-California International Exposition in San Diego. Much of the Spanish Colonial Revival was a response to his work there (8.30). As Kevin Starr points out, "Goodhue was just at that stage where he could remarkably fulfill San Diego's needs: poised between past and present, glimpsing the modern but flled with nostalgia for an imagined past." On the West Coast, the Spanish colonial past was idealized in a way analogous to how the English colonial past was perceived in the East: a simpler time when a spirit of community prevailed, and people lived in harmony with nature and took pride in working with their hands. This is especially true of the furniture and architecture inspired by the eighteenth- and early nineteenth-century Spanish adobe missions. The evolution of the term "Mission"—from its erroneous association with the chairs in one San Francisco church to a generic name (still in use today) for all Arts and Crafts furniture—is a perfect example of Eric Hobsbawm's concept of the invention of tradition."5

![](images/1c33c7980bdbe801a6877f5400fb1b72e5126f893c036ff749f1109814deae69.jpg)  
BasaCalo rhuHe

![](images/fc6097ceb119568d09e14d374bd5e975ba2da62b8dee8a0175692c4504c62e43.jpg)

The Swedenborgian Church in San Francisco was built in 1894 by the office of A. Page Brown in collaboration with Joseph Worcester, the church's minister and an amateur architect (8.31). A. C. Schweinfurth and, to a lesser extent, Bernard Maybeck were the architects from Brown's office who worked on the design. The church furniture was designed in Brown's office, but the person responsible remains unknown. Eighty chairs were executed in maple by the local cabinetmaking firm Alexander J. Forbes. Their simplicity and utter lack of ornamentation appealed to the church's architects: both Schweinfurth and Maybeck had Forbes make nearly identical chairs for a few other domestic

and ecclesiastical clients—only the wood varied. This oak example was probably made for one of those slightly later commissions (8.33).

Now the story changes to one of transformation and dissemination. As Anna D'Ambrosio relates in her study of New York manufacturer Joseph P. McHugh, "Between 1894 and 1897, Eudora Martin, a San Francisco decorator and a colleague of McHugh, sent him a chair either from the Church of the New Jerusalem or one modeled on its chairs; McHugh sold it and a dozen or so more that Martin supplied. Soon after [in 1898], the company carried an entire line based on the form.. McHugh called the simple, rush-seated chair the Mission Chair, and applied the term as well to an armchair and small bench."36 The line was an immediate success: it was inexpensive to produce and McHugh skillfully marketed its novelty through trade catalogues (8.32).

Despite the fact that furniture that looked like this was never used in California missions, the association was so seductive that it still endures today—"Mission" has become synonymous with Arts and Crafts "style," especially in California. Writers of the time who were knowledgeable about mission architecture were well aware there was no such thing as mission furniture—in 1905 George Wharton James wrote that mission furnishings had been made of any material available and were "not homogeneous in style," but the power of invented traditions is strong.

Imagined histories, enhanced by their foundations in documented culture, pervade the Arts and Crafts movement. In this case, the philosophy espoused by a Swedenborgian minister was embodied in a simple chair, an entirely appropriate design for a religion with the central tenet that "all things in the natural world had a corresponding meaning in the spiritual world. Such a belief fit in well with the Ruskinian ideals that Worcester embraced; therefore, he chose architects linked to the Arts and Crafts movement to build his church. The church's chairs were adapted by a big commercial company, which marketed them as Mission. Other manufacturers then rushed to produce nearly identical furniture, and vociferous arguments ensued over who had actually introduced the ur-form.39

![](images/8e28417ae2241096213340a88a35f0d33958223214912780132aec7c35f44f20.jpg)  
huf Hug  8 8.33 Side chair, 1894. Alexander J. Forbes

The term Mission had profound resonance. It supported the satisfying American myth that the Spanish missions were a "distinguished chapter in the history of American colonization," instead of one characterized by the shameless exploitation and decimation of a native population. Equally important was how neatly the term dovetailed with the ideology of the Arts and Crafts movement. The trade journals (as well as the "shelter" magazines) capitalized on this fortuitous combination of morality, marketing, and ease of manufacture. One declared that "furniture of the mission type..is furniture with a mission, and that mission is to teach that the first laws of furniture making should be good material, true proportion and honest workmanship." The term adhered with such tenacity that it soon came full circle and was reapplied to the church's chairs. In February 1901 House Beautiful described the chairs of the Swedenborgian Church as "beautifully modeled, suggestive of the primitive old mission furnishings."

As the United States assumed a new position of global power at the turn of the century, the need for a national American art—inextricably tied to ideals about democracy—became conflated with a need to compete economically with the major European powers. An aesthetic of simplicity was well suited to mass production and mass consumption. Straight lines and unornamented forms were far easier to achieve using machine technology than were curvilinear and carved ones.

This is not to deny the philosophical idealism of the American Arts and Crafts movement or its efforts as part of the larger Progressive movement to ameliorate the great injustices and economic dislocations resulting from unprecedented urbanization and industrialization. The life of Oscar Lovell Triggs—socialist, professor at the University of Chicago, cofounder of both the William Morris Society of Chicago and the Industrial Arts League—embodies these reform principles. Triggs was devoted to the establishment of the "New Industrialism," which would "encourage the founding of.manufactories upon the basis of artistic craftsmanship" and build on Morris's belief "in the value of industrial education [as] a factor in the modern arts andcrafts movement."3 He responded to the call for organic wholeness, convinced it could be achieved by combining proper design training with a transformation of the factory system: machines would do the soul-deadening repetitious work, allowing individual expression to flourish. Higher quality work would be done on a smaller scale in a pastoral setting, where the factory would include resources for continuing education, sports, and some farming.

Architects of the Prairie School also subscribed to the ideal that the machine could be an ally rather than an adversary of human creativity. Ironically, it was at a meeting of the Chicago

Society of Arts and Crafts that Wright publicly declared his allegiance to the machine as the natural extension of the movement's principles: "William Morris pleaded well for simplicity as the basis of all true art. Let us understand the significance to art of that word—SIMPLIciTY—for it isvial  ther the Machie.Wrigtmaintained that imachis were use correcty, they r e   T iwruc hphig aneeivpy,haa  psle i w hat    e    u of clean, strong forms."4

But professors such as Triggs and architects like Wright were not the American harbingers of the Arts and Crafts movement. This was the achievement of the entrepreneurial manufacturers, who would spread the Morrisian ideal widely, if not deeply, among the country's broad middle classes. If in Britain many Arts and Crafts protagonists were architects, and in Germany, they were most often painters who had converted to design, in the United States they were businessmen.

Gustav Stickley has been mentioned earlier in the context of his role as the hugely influential founder and publisher of The Cratsman magazine, which featured articles by or about the leading practitioners of design reform in Europe as well as in the United States. In the pages o The Craftsman, Stickley, with his combination of commerce and passion, emerges as a uniquely American leader of the Arts and Crafts movement. He used the magazine as a vehicle to promote

![](images/4190640725476261a16562e1e713efe8d7e4cc37aec1b3b8479bae31a475ca05.jpg)

the furniture, textiles, and metalwork made by his company, and he published

low-cost "Craftsman Homes" in every issue Complete working plans could be ordered from his Craftsman Home Builders' Club, free of charge to all subscribers to the magazine, or his company could be engaged to actually construct the houses. Although he took ful advantage of the economic synergy resulting from such interrelated efforts, he was genuinely devoted to improving public taste, working conditions, and the quality of American domestic life.

Although Stickley published the first issue of The Craftsman as a panegyric to Morris and socialism, and devoted the second to Ruskin, he was also a pragmatic businessman. He introduced his New Furniture line in 19oo, not at an exhibition society, but at the furniture fair in Grand Rapids, Michigan, the very center of the trade. These early forms were labor-intensive (the moldings, complicated chamfering, and inlaid tile on a plant stand made about 1900 were expensive to produce [8.34]). However, the cornparison of a 1901 serving table with one advertised in his 1905 catalogue demonstrates the compromises Stickley made to achieve the goals expressed in his article "A Plea for a Democratic $\bar { \mathsf { A } } \boldsymbol { \Gamma } \boldsymbol { \mathsf { t } } ^ { \prime \prime }$ (8.36 and 8.37). The arched drawers and beveled edges of the 1901 table required a good deal of handwork, but given the far greater rectilinearity of the later example, "my structural lines made no demands on the wood which it was not able to meet." As Stickley's biographer David Cathers explains, "This shift in emphasis toward plainness and standardization enabled Stickley to streamline his production process..from 1904 onwards Stickley turned increasingly to the more orthodox dictates of machines." Ultimately, he abandoned the profit sharing of the early years when it proved economically insupportable, and his plans for the cooperative community Craftsman Farms, which echoed Triggs's vision of the New Industrialism, were never realized.

What has become known as "standard Stickley," while never really inexpensive, was affordable to the middle class. It continued to display the hallmarks of Arts and Crafts furniture, with typically expressed structure of mortise and tenon joints, and quarter-sawn oak fumed with ammonia to add color while preserving the natural appearance of the wood. Seats and upholstery were also made of natural materials—leather or rush—although for a brief period he experimented with the kind of metal and wood inlays that would seem to violate the prohibition against decoration not required for actual construction (8.35).

The Roycroft community in East Aurora, New York, is another example of economic viability due to leadership provided not by an artist-designer but by a commercial proselytizer. The community's founder, Elbert Hubbard, had been a partner at the Larkin Soap

Co., where he pioneered mass-marketing techniques that he later

applied at Roycroft. After visiting Morris's workshops in London in 1893, he returned to America determined to establish a hand-printing enterprise along the lines of the Kelmscott Press. The Roycroft Press was immediately successful (8.38) and led to the establishment of a bindery and a leather shop. Within a few years, furniture, metalwork, and leaded-glass studios were added (8.39 and 8.40). All the goods were available through catalogues and many furnished the Roycroft Inn, where guests could stay while observing life at the community. At its height, Roycroft had more than four hundred workers.

Hubbard's system was one of benevolent capitalism rather than the socialist democracy he claimed.48 Working conditions were excellent by early-twentieth-century standards, but Hubbard paid relatively low wages (albeit supplemented with gifts of food and coal) and exercised autocratic control over the community. Roycroft was financially successful; its products sold throughout the country. Perhaps this was because Hubbard believed that "the World of Commerce is just as honorable as the World of Art and a trifle more necessary."

![](images/dff3c7fb81b4e32f2e621b69da5a0771a117702b465f817cb8270c5b3150f4e4.jpg)

![](images/9fce9862e04c9a6c6ec351fe483a2f04835b6cfd52b1ede8089dbb4d45bc4dc6.jpg)  
viable, 90Gusta StickeratWor  
v iablehop

![](images/49fee0a81b244d7a75284d99504d53f8f6cb0c1aee7aaa0b7e570492d2f2f5a9.jpg)  
8.38 Book, The Last Ride, 1900. Roycroft Press 8Cellarette, no01, c.906. Furniture Shop ofthe Roycroers

The "World of Commerce" also ensured that the products were widely available, although at the expense of the craftsman's joy in labor and control over quality. Arts and Crafts furnishings grew so popular in the first decade of the century that many manufacturers (including Sears Roebuck) responded by focusing merely on style, without heeding any of the movement's philosophy. These companies sold cheap imitations of Stickley's Craftsman furniture, using veneers to imitate quarter-sawn ak, tenons applied to appear structural, and cast-brass and coppr hardware instead of hammered materials. In silver, hammer marks could be added by hand to medieval-looking forms after the piece had been spun on a lathe (8.42).

Triggs's dream for the New Industrialism was best realized by quality products and enlightened working conditions within a factory context. In 1902 Triggs published Chapters in the History f the Arts and Crafts Movement, in which he describes the Rookwod Pottery o Cincinnati as an "Ideal Workshop" (8.41).° Rookwood was the most successful American art pottery and the first to receive international acclaim, winning major awards at expositions such as Paris (1900) and Turin (1902). While Rookwood might have been more accurately described as an idealized workshop, since the shapes were standardized and the division of labor specialized, the decorators were responsible for complete surface designs and each piece was individually painted.51

![](images/37f05e3c0df35f9632b3bc24a61819b238165350ebf6c59686479ab50fba9653.jpg)

![](images/11e886670e8af3f40a9d2c5b8627f341fdff1042227059e93113445f8003cbf5.jpg)  
8.41 Vase, Iris line, 1907. Rookwood Pottery 8.42 Punch bowl, c. 1910. Shreve & Co.

In concluding this study of which aspects of the American Arts and Crafts were variants of the British movement and which were particularly characteristic of the United States, the art pottery industry serves as a useful paradigm. Just as America had emulated Britain in the establishment of settlement houses, design training, home industries, and artistic crafts, the United States also had its "pure" artists committed to creating every stage ofmanufacture themselves. Tly tBritish precedent. Although renowned on both sides of the Atlantic, Adelaide Alsop Robineau sold only about six hundred pieces of porcelain during her career; she supported herself instead by teaching and publishing the popular journal Keramic Studio (8.43). George Ohr worked outside the mainstream of even the most progressive art potters at his studio in Biloxi, Mississippi. He single-handedly created virtuoso vessel forms out of local clay—working them until they were as thinas porcelain, thencushing them into the deie shape before applyn equallyusal glazes—but he could not support himself through their sale (8.44). As a journal observed in 1909, I  o  he  wovn dfmilu

What distinguished "American" Arts and Crafts most, however, was its success with largerscale production. In the case of art pottery, this came from established commercial potteries that, recognizing a market for greater individuality, added special lines to their regular output.

![](images/f14fbd419a6c798b939bc0e5e9c1592acd055c2cf72fa891c40da1b734d84ed9.jpg)  
8 Bowl, 1917. Adelaide Alsop Robinau

![](images/5b29133050c5666d3c092f9bcad13f46ae8a3108d8c2ed6b8f13f53896bc1249.jpg)

In 1909 the Fulper Pottery Co. added the Vasekraft line, characterized by flambé and crystalline art glazes, to their production of food containers and other completely utilitarian stoneware. And in 1908 Gates Potteries' Teco Art Pottery line was described by The Studio as demonstrating that "America is coming to her own in pottery." William Day Gates commissioned designs from Prairie School architects, such as Elmslie and Hugh Garden (8.45), as well as from well-known sculptors. The successful company was already equipped for the production of architectural terra-cotta, and Gates used molds instead of hand throwing. Thus he was able to achieve his artistic goals while offering his pottery at a price the average consumer could afford. The Studio also wrote admiringly of the picturesque location of the pottery, forty-five miles from Chicago, "where the artists are in close communion with nature," and of an atmosphere infused with "the enthusiasm of the workers." Here is as close a description of the New Industrialism as Triggs cu wish,p wi the o

In medium-sized shops, such as the widely imitated Grueby Faience Co. in Boston, the vases were not one-off designs and the patterns were prescribed, but each piece was thrown, not molded, and the decorators were allowed some individual variation (8.47). However, molded wares, like those in the Teco line, were not beyond the pale of full consideration as Arts and Crafts products. Some designers, such as Artus Van Briggle, who left Rookwood to start his own poery Colorao, beli work was be realize byeaigmastr mold  theal, from which slip-cast pottery was produced in plaster molds (8.46).

Arts and Crafts objects were available on many different economic levels in America and they were produced in many ways. They could be made at home, ordered from catalogues, pur-

![](images/de215bf100dc8894eb65c7174aaf3f0ced898a93898cf56ccbc65a316c4e496d.jpg)

![](images/3f508949b0606d7cfcefa50c977bbd9f3a0d3ab61e46ba2ad15d3ce8317d881c.jpg)

chased at department stores, or acquired in the more rarified contexts of exhibition societies and small workshops. Some of the movement's fundamental ideals may have been diluted, but the products reached a greater audience in America than in any other country. No artistic, political, or philosophical movement (and the Arts and Crafts was all three) has ever flourished without compromise.   
If Morris's assertion "I do not want art for a few,   
any more than education for a few, or freedom   
for a few" was the paramount goal, then the   
American story of democratizing design can be   
seen as one of unprecedented success.

![](images/8b55a740c2cbdac92a4d1efa68dbfbb3e83289bbf6a18746d757f73fd02898d4.jpg)  
8.47 Vase, 1905. Grueby Faience Co.

![](images/d576dd8c81c1db478a4f7d67d98f16a3904976a90367a48bc110abd107811f21.jpg)  
xile, F

## NOTE TO THE READER

The checklist is accurate as of June 2004. Objects are listed by the country in which they were made, and then alphabetically by designer, maker, guild, school, c. which is in boldface type. Design date appears after the object identification. The production date, when kownfe t Height predes with precedes depth.
